From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
Predominantly Scottish cloth pattern
For other uses, see Tartan (disambiguation) <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Tartan_(disambiguation)>.
	
This article *may be too long <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Wikipedia:Article_size> to read and navigate comfortably*. When this tag
was added, its readable prose size <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Wikipedia:SIZERULE> was 33,000 words.Please consider splitting <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Splitting> content into sub-articles,
condensing <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Summary_style> it,
or adding subheadings <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Help:Section#Subsections>. Please discuss this issue on the article's
talk page <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Talk:Tartan>. /(June 2023)/

Photo of three samples of tartan cloth, blue, grey, and red, the grey in
a subtle palette, the others bright <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Three_tartans.jpg>Three tartans; the left and right are made with
the "modern" dye palette; the middle is made with "muted" colours.

Montage of 9 tartan designs, from simple to complex, and in a wide range
of colours <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Tartans.png>Tartans come
in a wide variety of colours and patterns.

A purple-red-and-green tartan skirt and jumper (sweater) on a mannequin
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:
1970s_Missoni_sweater_and_skirt_set,_purple,_red_and_green_plaid_knit.jpg>1970s Missoni <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Missoni> tartan knit jumper (sweater) and skirt set

*Tartan* (Scottish Gaelic <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scottish_Gaelic_language>: /breacan/ [ˈpɾʲɛxkən] <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:IPA/Scottish_Gaelic>) is a patterned cloth
with crossing horizontal and vertical bands in multiple colours, forming
simple or complex rectangular patterns. Tartans originated in woven
wool, but are now made in other materials. Tartan is particularly
associated with Scotland <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scotland>, and
Scottish kilts <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kilt> almost always have
tartan patterns. The earliest surviving samples of tartan-style cloth
are around 3,000 years old and were discovered in Xinjiang <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Xinjiang>, China.

Outside of Scotland, tartan is sometimes also known as
"*plaid*" (particularly in North America); however, in Scotland, a /
plaid <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Full_plaid>/ is a large piece of
tartan cloth which can be worn several ways.

Traditional tartan is made with alternating bands of coloured (pre-dyed)
threads woven in usually matching warp and weft <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warp_and_weft> in a simple 2/2 twill <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Twill> pattern. Up close, this pattern forms
alternating short diagonal lines where different colours cross; from
further back, it gives the appearance of new colours blended from the
original ones. The resulting blocks of colour repeat vertically and
horizontally in a distinctive pattern of rectangles and lines known as
a /sett/.

Scottish tartan was originally associated with the Highlands <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_Highlands>. Early tartans were only
particular to locales, rather than any specific Scottish clan <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_clan>; like other materials, tartan
designs were produced by local weavers for local tastes, using the most
available natural dyes <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_dye>. The
Dress Act of 1746 <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dress_Act_1746>
attempted to bring the warrior clans <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scottish_clan> there under government control by banning Highland dress
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highland_dress>, then an important
element of Gaelic <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaels> Scottish culture
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Culture_of_Scotland>. When the law was
repealed in 1782, tartan was no longer ordinary dress for most
Highlanders. It was adopted more widely as the symbolic national dress
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_dress> of all Scotland when King
George IV wore a tartan kilt in his 1822 visit to Scotland <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Visit_of_George_IV_to_Scotland>; it was promoted
further by Queen Victoria <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Queen_Victoria>. This marked an era of rather politicised "tartanry" and
"Highlandism" <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tartanry>.

While the first uniform tartan is believed to date to 1713 (with some
evidence of militia use earlier), it was not until around the early 19th
century that patterns were created for specific Scottish clans;^[1]
<#cite_note-Newsome_1994-1> most of the traditional ones were
established between 1815 and 1850. The Victorian-era <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Victorian_era> invention of artificial dyes
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aniline#Synthetic_dye_industry> meant
that a multitude of patterns could be produced cheaply; mass-produced
tartan fashion cloth was applied to a nostalgic <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nostalgia> (and increasingly aristocratic, and
profitable) view of Scottish history.

Today /tartan/ is no longer limited to textiles <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Textile>, but is also used as a name for the
pattern itself, regardless of medium. The use of tartan has spread
outside Scotland, especially to countries that have been influenced by
Scottish culture <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_culture>.
However, tartan-styled patterns have existed for centuries in some other
cultures, such as Japan, where complex /kōshi/ fabrics date to at least
the 18th century, and Russia (sometimes with gold and silver thread)
since at least the early 19th century. Maasai /shúkà/ wraps, Bhutanese /
mathra/ weaving, and Indian /madras/ cloth are also often in tartan
patterns, distinct from the Scottish style.


    Etymology and terminology[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/
    index.php?title=Tartan&action=edit&section=1>]

The English <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/English_language> and Scots
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scots_language> word /tartan/ is possibly
derived from French <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_language> /
tiretaine/ meaning 'linsey-woolsey <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Linsey-woolsey> cloth'.^[2] <#cite_note-Harper-2> ^[3] <#cite_note-
BdLC_57-3> ^[4] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_3-4> Other hypotheses are that
it derives from Scottish Gaelic <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scottish_Gaelic> /tarsainn/ or /tarsuinn/, meaning 'across' or 'crossing
over';^[3] <#cite_note-BdLC_57-3> ^[4] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_3-4> or
from French /tartarin/ or /tartaryn/ (occurring in 1454 spelled /
tartyn/)^[5] <#cite_note-DOST-5> meaning 'Tartar cloth <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Mongol_elements_in_Western_medieval_art#Mongol_Empire_%22Tatar%22_textiles_in_medieval_art>'.^[2] <#cite_note-Harper-2> It is unrelated to the superficially similar word /tarlatan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tarlatan>/, which refers to a very open-weave muslin <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muslin> similar to cheesecloth <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cheesecloth>. /Tartan/ is both a mass noun <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass_noun> ("12 metres of tartan") and a count noun <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Count_noun> ("12 different tartans").

Today, /tartan/ refers to coloured patterns, though originally did not
have to be made up of a pattern at all, as it referred to the type of
weave; as late as the 1820s, some tartan cloth was described as "plain
coloured ... without pattern".^[6] <#cite_note-6> ^[7] <#cite_note-
Scarlett2008-7> Patterned cloth from the Gaelic <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic>-speaking Scottish Highlands
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_Highlands> was called /breacan/,
meaning 'many colours'. Over time, the meanings of /tartan/ and /
breacan/ were combined to describe a certain type of pattern on a
certain type of cloth.^[7] <#cite_note-Scarlett2008-7>

The pattern of a particular tartan is called its /sett/. The sett is
made up of a series of lines at specific widths which cross at right
angles <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Right_angle> and blend into each
other;^[7] <#cite_note-Scarlett2008-7> the longer term /setting/ is
occasionally used.^[8] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2010-11-b-8> /Sett/
can refer to either the minimal visual presentation of the complete
tartan pattern or to a textual representation of it (in a /thread
count/).^[7] <#cite_note-Scarlett2008-7>

Today /tartan/ is used more generally to describe the pattern, not
limited to textiles, appearing on media such as paper, plastics,
packaging, and wall coverings.^[9] <#cite_note-9> ^[7] <#cite_note-
Scarlett2008-7> ^[1] <#cite_note-Newsome_1994-1> ^[10] <#cite_note-10>

In North America, the term /plaid/ is commonly used to refer to
tartan.^[11] <#cite_note-11> ^[12] <#cite_note-STM_faq-12> ^[13]
<#cite_note-13> ^[a] <#cite_note-16> /Plaid/, derived from the Scottish
Gaelic /plaide/ meaning 'blanket',^[16] <#cite_note-17> ^[b]
<#cite_note-20> was first used of any rectangular garment, sometimes
made up of tartan,^[c] <#cite_note-22> which could be worn several ways:
the belted plaid <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Belted_plaid> (/breacan
féile/) or "great kilt" which preceded the modern kilt <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kilt>; the arisaid <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Arisaid> (/earasaid/), a large shawl that could be wrapped into a dress;
and several types of shoulder cape, such as the full plaid <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Full_plaid> and fly plaid <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fly_plaid>. In time, /plaid/ was used to describe
blankets themselves.^[12] <#cite_note-STM_faq-12> In former times, the
term /plaiding/^[20] <#cite_note-Mackay_94-23> or /pladding/^[21]
<#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_40-24> was sometimes used to refer to
tartan cloth. ^[22] <#cite_note-25>


    Weaving and design[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
    title=Tartan&action=edit&section=2>]


      Weaving construction[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
      title=Tartan&action=edit&section=3>]

Simple diagram of black weft threads being woven under two orange warp
threads then over two of the warp threads <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/File:K%C3%B6pergewebe_2x2.jpg>Visualisation of 2/2 twill weave: the
black weft threads go two over then two under the orange warp threads,
staggered by one thread each pass (resulting in a diagonal pattern). In
the actual cloth, the white gaps would be closed.

The Scottish Register of Tartans <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scottish_Register_of_Tartans> provides the following summary definition
of tartan:^[23] <#cite_note-SRT_FAQ-26>

    Tartan (the design) is a pattern that comprises two or more
    different solid-coloured stripes that can be of similar but are
    usually of differing proportions that repeat in a defined sequence.
    The sequence of the warp colours (long-ways threads) is repeated in
    same order and size in the weft (cross-ways threads). The majority
    of such patterns (or setts) are symmetrical, i.e. the pattern
    repeats in the same colour order and proportions in every direction
    from the two pivot points. In the less common asymmetric patterns,
    the colour sequence repeats in blocks as opposed to around
    alternating pivots but the size and colour sequence of warp and weft
    remain the same.

Close-up view of scarlet red, black, yellow, azure bleu, and crimson red
tartan cloth <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Tartan_cloth_closeup.jpg>Close-up view of traditional tartan cloth,
showing pattern of diagonal "ribs" of colour; this is a five-colour
tartan, in scarlet red, black, yellow, azure blue, and crimson red.

In more detail, traditional tartan cloth is a tight, staggered 2/2 twill
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Twill> weave of worsted <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Worsted> wool: the horizontal /weft <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warp_and_weft>/ (also /woof/ or /fill/) is woven
in a simple arrangement of two-over-two-under the fixed, vertical /warp
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warp_and_weft>/, advancing one thread at
each pass.^[15] <#cite_note-Black_3-15> As each thread in the weft
crosses threads in the warp, the staggering by one means that each warp
thread will also cross two weft threads. The result, when the material
is examined closely, is a characteristic 45-degree diagonal pattern of
"ribs" where different colours cross.^[7] <#cite_note-Scarlett2008-7>
^[24] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_55-27> Where a thread in the weft crosses
threads of the same colour in the warp, this produces a solid colour on
the tartan, while a weft thread crossing warp threads of a different
colour produces an equal admixture of the two colours alternating,
producing the appearance of a third colour – a halftone /blend/ or /
mixture/ – when viewed from further back.^[25] <#cite_note-BdLC_61-28>
^[7] <#cite_note-Scarlett2008-7> ^[23] <#cite_note-SRT_FAQ-26> (The
effect is similar to multicolour halftone <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Halftone> printing, or cross-hatching <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Cross-hatching> in coloured-pencil art.)^[7] <#cite_note-
Scarlett2008-7> Thus, a set of two base colours produces three different
colours including one blend, increasing quadratically <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quadratic_growth> with the number of base colours;
so a set of six base colours produces fifteen blends and a total of
twenty-one different perceived colours.^[7] <#cite_note-Scarlett2008-7>
^[26] <#cite_note-29> ^[d] <#cite_note-30> This means that the more
stripes and colours used, the more blurred and subdued the tartan's
pattern becomes.^[7] <#cite_note-Scarlett2008-7> ^[25] <#cite_note-
BdLC_61-28> Unlike in simple /checker (chequer) <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Check_(pattern)>/ or /dicing/ patterns (like a
chessboard), no solid colour in a tartan appears next to another solid
colour, only a blend^[7] <#cite_note-Scarlett2008-7> (solid colours may
touch at their corners).^[27] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_23-31>

James D. Scarlett (2008) offered a definition of a usual tartan pattern
(some types of tartan deviate from the particulars of this definition –
see below):^[7] <#cite_note-Scarlett2008-7>

    The unit of tartan pattern, the /sett/, is a square, composed of a
    number of rectangles, square and oblong, arranged symmetrically
    around a central square. Each of these elements occurs four times,
    at intervals of ninety degrees, and each is rotated ninety degrees
    in relation to its fellows. The proportions of the elements are
    determined by the relative widths of the stripes that form them.

The sequence of thread colours in the /sett/ (the minimal design of the
tartan, to be duplicated^[7] <#cite_note-Scarlett2008-7>  – "the DNA of
a tartan"),^[28] <#cite_note-SRT_threadcount-32> starts at an edge and
either reverses or (rarely) repeats on what are called /pivot points/
or /pivots/.^[29] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_62-33> In diagram A, the sett
begins at the first pivot, reverses at the second pivot, continues, then
reverses again at the next pivot, and will carry on in this manner
horizontally. In diagram B, the sett proceeds in the same way as in the
warp but vertically. The diagrams illustrate the construction of a
typical /symmetric/^[30] <#cite_note-34> (also /symmetrical/,^[28]
<#cite_note-SRT_threadcount-32> /reflective/,^[28] <#cite_note-
SRT_threadcount-32> /reversing/,^[31] <#cite_note-35> or /
mirroring/)^[32] <#cite_note-36> ^[e] <#cite_note-38> tartan. However,
on a rare /asymmetric/^[34] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_wedding-39> (/
asymmetrical/,^[28] <#cite_note-SRT_threadcount-32> or /non-
reversing/)^[34] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_wedding-39> ^[f]
<#cite_note-40> tartan, the sett does not reverse at the pivots, it just
repeats at them.^[g] <#cite_note-42> An old term for the latter type
is /cheek/ or /cheeck/ pattern.^[36] <#cite_note-43> Also, some tartans
(very few among traditional Scottish tartans) do not have exactly the
same sett for the warp and weft. This means the warp and weft will have
differing thread counts (see below).^[h] <#cite_note-ExMacMillan-45>
Asymmetric and differing-warp-and-weft patterns are more common in
madras cloth (see § Indian madras <#Indian_madras>, below) and some
other weaving traditions than in Scottish tartan.

  *
    Diagram A, the warp <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:Tartan_diagram_(warp_and_weft)_A.svg>
    Diagram A, the warp
  *
    Diagram B, the weft <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:Tartan_diagram_(warp_and_weft)_B.svg>
    Diagram B, the weft
  *
    Diagram C, the tartan. The combining of the warp and weft. <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Tartan_diagram_(warp_and_weft)_C.svg>
    Diagram C, the tartan. The combining of the warp and weft.

A tartan is recorded by counting the threads of each colour that appear
in the sett.^[i] <#cite_note-51> The /thread count <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Units_of_textile_measurement#In_tartans>/ (or /
threadcount/, /thread-count/) not only describes the width of the
stripes on a sett, but also the colours used (typically
abbreviated).^[28] <#cite_note-SRT_threadcount-32> Usually every number
in a thread count is an even number^[43] <#cite_note-52> to assist in
manufacture. The first and last threads of the thread count are the
pivots.^[29] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_62-33> A thread count combined
with exact colour information and other weaving details is referred to
as a /ticket stamp/^[44] <#cite_note-Blakely_13-53> or simply /
ticket/.^[45] <#cite_note-54>

<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Tartan_Weaving_in_Lochcarron.jpg>Tartan weaving in Lochcarron
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lochcarron>, Scottish Highlands <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_Highlands>

There is no universally standardised way to write a thread count,^[38]
<#cite_note-STA_threadcount-46> but the different systems are easy to
distinguish. As a simple example:

  * The thread count "/K4 R24 K24 Y4/" corresponds to a mirroring
    pattern of 4 black threads, 24 red threads, 24 black threads, 4
    yellow threads, in which the beginning black and ending yellow
    pivots are /not/ repeated (after Y4/, the colours are reversed,
    first K24 then R24); this is a "/full-count/ at the pivots" thread
    count.^[29] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_62-33>
      o An equivalent notation is boldfacing the pivot abbreviations:
        *K*4 R24 K24 *Y*4.
  * The same tartan could also be represented as "K/2 R24 K24 Y/2", in
    markup that indicates that the leading black and trailing yellow /
    are/ duplicated before continuing from these pivot points (after Y/
    2, the colours are reversed as Y/2 again, then K24, then R24); this
    is a "/half-count/ at the pivots" thread count.^[28] <#cite_note-
    SRT_threadcount-32>
  * In the older and potentially ambiguous style of thread-counting,
    without the "/" (or bold) notation, a thread count like "K4 R24 K24
    Y4" is assumed to be full-count at the pivots, unless the author
    clearly indicates otherwise.^[29] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_62-33>
    ^[38] <#cite_note-STA_threadcount-46> ^[j] <#cite_note-55> 

In all of these cases, the result is a /half-sett/ thread count, which
represents the threading before the pattern mirrors and completes; a
full-sett thread count for a mirroring (symmetric) tartan is
redundant.^[38] <#cite_note-STA_threadcount-46> A "/" can also be used
between two colour codes (e.g. "W/Y24" for "white/yellow 24") to create
even more of a shorthand threadcount for simple tartans in which half of
the half-sett pattern is different from the other only in the way of a
colour swap;^[46] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_63-56> but this is not a
common style of thread-counting.

  * An asymmetric tartan, one that does not mirror, would be represented
    in a /full-sett/ thread count with "..." markup, as "...K4 R24 K24
    Y4..." (after Y4, the entire pattern would begin again from
    K4).^[28] <#cite_note-SRT_threadcount-32> 

Various writers and tartan databases do not use a consistent set of
colour names (see § Colour, palettes, and meaning
<#Colour,_palettes,_and_meaning>, below) and abbreviations,^[47]
<#cite_note-57> so a thread count may not be universally understandable
without a colour key/legend <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Chart#Features>. Some recorders prefer to begin a thread count at the
pivot with the colour name (or abbreviation) that is first in
alphabetical order (e.g. if there is a white pivot and a blue one, begin
with blue),^[28] <#cite_note-SRT_threadcount-32> but this is actually
arbitrary.

Though thread counts are quite specific, they can be modified depending
on the desired size of the tartan. For example, the sett of a tartan
(e.g., 6 inches square – a typical size for kilts)^[28] <#cite_note-
SRT_threadcount-32> may be too large to fit upon the face of a necktie
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Necktie>. In this case, the thread count
would be reduced /in proportion/ (e.g. to 3 inches to a side).^[38]
<#cite_note-STA_threadcount-46> In some works, a thread count is reduced
to the smallest even number of threads (often down to 2) required to
accurately reproduce the design;^[29] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_62-33> in
such a case, it is often necessary to up-scale the thread count
proportionally for typical use in kilts and plaids.

Before the 19th century, tartan was often woven with thread for the weft
that was up to 1/3 thicker than the fine thread used for the warp,^[7]
<#cite_note-Scarlett2008-7> which would result in a rectangular rather
than square pattern; the solution was to adjust the weft thread count to
return the pattern to square,^[24] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_55-27> or
make it non-square on purpose, as is still done in a handful of
traditional tartans.^[h] <#cite_note-ExMacMillan-45> Uneven warp-and-
weft thread thickness could also contribute to a striped rather than
checked appearance in some tartan samples.^[48] <#cite_note-
TelferDunbar1979_88-58>

The predominant colours of a tartan (the widest bands) are called the /
under-check/ (or /under check/, /undercheck/, /under-cheque/);^[49]
<#cite_note-59> sometimes the terms /ground/,^[k] <#cite_note-61> /
background/,^[51] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_47-62> or /base/^[51]
<#cite_note-Scarlett1990_47-62> are used instead, especially if there is
only one dominant colour. Thin, contrasting lines are referred to as
the /over-check/^[52] <#cite_note-63> ^[51] <#cite_note-
Scarlett1990_47-62> (also /over-stripe/ or /overstripe/).^[53]
<#cite_note-64> Over-checks in pairs are sometimes referred to as /tram
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tram> lines/, /tramlines/, or /tram
tracks/.^[54] <#cite_note-SRT_tram,_guard-65> Bright over-checks are
sometimes bordered on either side (usually both), for extra contrast, by
additional thin lines, often black, called /guard lines/ or /
guards/.^[54] <#cite_note-SRT_tram,_guard-65> Historically, the weaver
William Wilson & Son of Bannockburn sometimes wove bright over-checks in
silk, to give some added shine^[55] <#cite_note-66> ^[56]
<#cite_note-67> (commercially around 1820–30, but in regimental
officers' plaids back to at least 1794).^[57] <#cite_note-
EsleaMacDonald_2012-01-68> ^[l] <#cite_note-69> Tartan used for plaids
(not the belted plaid) often have a purled <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Knitting#Knit_(or_plain)_and_purl_stitches> fringe.^[58]
<#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2019-70>

Zoom-in on a predominantly red tartan plaid, showing its bottom edge
with a purled fringe <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Plaid_with_purled_fringe.jpg>Zoom-in on a bagpiper's full plaid
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Full_plaid> (royal Stuart tartan
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Stuart_tartan>), showing the purled
fringe style typical for such garments

An old-time practice, to the 18th century, was to add an accent on
plaids or sometimes kilts in the form of a /selvedge <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Selvedge>/ in herringbone weave <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herringbone_(cloth)> at the edge, 1–3 inches (2.5–
7.6 cm) wide, but still fitting into the colour pattern of the
sett;^[58] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2019-70> ^[59] <#cite_note-71> a
few modern weavers will still produce some tartan in this style.
Sometimes more decorative selvedges were used: /Selvedge marks/ were
borders (usually on one side only) formed by repeating a colour from the
sett in a broad band (often in herringbone), sometimes further bordered
by a thin strip of another colour from the sett or decorated in mid-
selvedge with two thin strips; these were typically used for the bottoms
of belted plaids and kilts,^[58] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2019-70>
^[60] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2020-04-72> and were usually black in
military tartans, but could be more colourful in civilian ones.^[61]
<#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2012_18-73> The more elaborate /selvedge
patterns/ were a wider series of narrow stripes using some or all of the
colours of the sett; these were almost exclusively used on household
tartans (blankets, curtains, etc.), and on two opposing sides of the
fabric.^[61] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2012_18-73> ^[58] <#cite_note-
EsleaMacDonald2019-70> The very rare /total border/ is an all-four-sides
selvedge of a completely different sett; described by Peter Eslea
MacDonald (2019) as "an extraordinarily difficult feature to weave and
can be regarded as the zenith of the tartan weaver's art",^[58]
<#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2019-70> it only survives in Scottish-style
tartan as a handful of 18th-century samples (in Scotland^[62]
<#cite_note-74> and Nova Scotia <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Nova_Scotia>, Canada, but probably all originally from Scotland).^[63]
<#cite_note-75> The style has also been used in Estonia in the weaving
of /suurrätt/ shawls/plaids <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Suurr%C3%A4tt,_229786_ERM_A530_2.jpg>.

    18th-century tartan with a herringbone selvedge at the bottom

    18th-century tartan with a herringbone /selvedge/ at the bottom
    (detail <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/

  *
    Black Watch tartan with a selvedge mark at the bottom (also
    herringbone) (detail) <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/

    Black Watch tartan with a /selvedge mark/ at the bottom (also
    herringbone) (detail <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:Black_Watch_with_herringbone_bottom_selvedge_mark.png>)
  *
    Wilsons 1819 blanket tartan with a selvedge pattern on the right
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:Wilsons_1819_blanket_tartan,_combined_with_right_selvedge_pattern.png>
    Wilsons 1819 blanket tartan with a /selvedge pattern/ on the right
  *
    Bottom-right corner of blanket with total border selvedge;
    approximation based on photo of real blanket discovered in Nova
    Scotia, but probably Scottish, c. 1780s <https://en.wikipedia.org/
    wiki/
    File:Antigonish_blanket_tartan_with_total_border_selvedge,_corner.png>
    Bottom-right corner of blanket with /total border/ selvedge;
    approximation based on photo of real blanket discovered in Nova
    Scotia, but probably Scottish, c. 1780s

Tartan is usually woven /balanced-warp/ (or just /balanced/), repeating
evenly from a pivot point at the centre outwards and with a complete
sett finishing at the outer selvedge;^[8] <#cite_note-
EsleaMacDonald_2010-11-b-8> ^[64] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2016-01-76>
^[65] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2014-11-77> e.g. a piece of tartan for
a plaid might be 24 setts long and 4 wide. An /offset/, /off-set/, or /
unbalanced/ weave is one in which the pattern finishes at the edge in
the middle of a pivot colour; this was typically done with pieces
intended to be joined (e.g. for a belted plaid or a blanket) to make
larger spans of cloth with the pattern continuing across the seam;^[8]
<#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2010-11-b-8> ^[65] <#cite_note-
EsleaMacDonald_2014-11-77> if the tartan had a selvedge mark or selvedge
pattern, it was at the other side of the warp.^[66] <#cite_note-78>

The term /hard tartan/ refers to a version of the cloth woven with very
tightly wound, non-fuzzy thread, producing a comparatively rougher and
denser (though also thinner) material than is now typical for
kilts.^[67] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_42-79> ^[68] <#cite_note-
SRT_Jacobite,_Old-80> It was in common use up until the 1830s.^[48]
<#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_88-58> There are extant but uncommon
samples of hard tartan from the early 18th century that use the more
intricate herringbone instead of twill weave throughout the entire
cloth.^[69] <#cite_note-81>

While modern tartan is primarily a commercial enterprise on large power
looms <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Power_loom>, tartan was originally
the product of rural weavers of the pre-industrial age, and can be
produced by a dedicated hobbyist with a strong, stable hand loom
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hand_loom>.^[70] <#cite_note-82> ^[71]
<#cite_note-83> ^[72] <#cite_note-84> Since around 1808, the traditional
size of the warp reed <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reed_(weaving)> for
tartan is 37 inches (94 cm), the length of the Scottish ell <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ell> (previous sizes were sometimes 34 and 40
inches).^[73] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_146-147-85> Telfer Dunbar
(1979) describes the setup thus:^[73] <#cite_note-
TelferDunbar1979_146-147-85>

    The reed varies in thickness according to the texture of the
    material to be woven. A thirty-Porter (which contains 20 splits of
    the reed) or 600-reed, is divided into 600 openings in the breadth
    of 37 inches. Twenty of these openings are called a Porter and into
    each opening are put two threads, making 1,200 threads of warp and
    as many of weft in a square yard of tartan through a 30-Porter reed.

/Splits/ are also referred to as /dents/, and /Porters/ are also
called /gangs/.^[74] <#cite_note-86>


      Styles and design principles[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/
      index.php?title=Tartan&action=edit&section=4>]

Traditional tartan patterns can be divided into several style classes.
The most basic is a simple two-colour check of thick bands (with or
without thin over-checks of one or more other colours). A variant on
this splits one or more of the bands, to form squares of smaller squares
instead of just big, solid squares; a style heavily favoured in /
Vestiarium Scoticum <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Vestiarium_Scoticum>/. A complexity step up is the superimposed check,
in which a third colour is placed centrally "on top of" or
"inside" (surrounded by) one of the base under-check colours, providing
a pattern of nested squares, which might then also have thin, bright
and/or black over-checks added. Another group is multiple checks,
typically of two broad bands of colour on a single dominant
"background" (e.g. red, blue, red, green, red – again possibly with
contrasting narrow over-checks). The aforementioned types can be
combined into more complex tartans. In any of these styles, an over-
check is sometimes not a new colour but one of the under-check colours
"on top of" the other under-check. A rare style, traditionally used for
arisaid <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arisaid> (/earasaid/) tartans but
no longer in much if any Scottish use, is a pattern consisting entirely
of thin over-checks, sometimes grouped, "on" a single ground colour,
usually white.^[75] <#cite_note-87> M. Martin (1703) reported that the
line colours were typically blue, black, and red.^[76] <#cite_note-88>
Examples of this style do not survive,^[77] <#cite_note-
Scarlett1990_40-89> at least not in the tartan databases (there may be
preserved museum pieces with such patterns).^[m] <#cite_note-91> Some
tartan patterns are more abstract and do not fit into any of these
styles,^[79] <#cite_note-92> especially in madras cloth (see § Indian
madras <#Indian_madras>, below).

  *
    Most basic check – MacGregor red-and-black (Rob Roy), as simple as
    it gets: equal proportions of two colours. <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:Robert_Roy_MacGregor,_centred,_zoomed_out_more.png>
    *Most basic check* – MacGregor red-and-black <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Gregor> (Rob Roy), as simple as it gets:
    equal proportions of two colours.
  *
    Basic check modified – Wallace red/dress, black on a slightly larger
    ground of red, laced with yellow and black over-checks. <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Wallace_tartan_(Vestiarium_Scoticum).png>
    *Basic check modified* – Wallace <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Clan_Wallace> red/dress, black on a slightly larger ground of red,
    laced with yellow and black over-checks.
  *
    Split check – MacGregor red-and-green with a wide green band split
    into three to form a "square of squares", then laced with a white
    over-check. <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:MacGregor_tartan_(Vestiarium_Scoticum).png>
    *Split check* – MacGregor <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Clan_Gregor> red-and-green with a wide green band split into three
    to form a "square of squares", then laced with a white over-check.
  *
    Superimposed check – Ruthven, a red ground with a big green stripe
    "inside" a bigger blue one, then white and green over-checks.
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:Ruthven_tartan_(Vestiarium_Scoticum).png>
    *Superimposed check* – Ruthven <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Clan_Ruthven>, a red ground with a big green stripe "inside" a
    bigger blue one, then white and green over-checks.
  *
    Multiple checks – Davidson, a green ground with equal blue and black
    bands, then with red, blue, and black over-checks. <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:Davidson_tartan,_modern,_centred,_lightened.png>
    *Multiple checks* – Davidson <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Clan_Davidson>, a green ground with equal blue and black bands, then
    with red, blue, and black over-checks.
  *
    Complex example – Ross, combines split-check and multiple-check
    styles, with one blue and two green split checks on red, with blue
    and green over-checks. <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:Ross_tartan,_modern,_tiling.png>
    *Complex example* – Ross <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Ross>,
    combines split-check and multiple-check styles, with one blue and
    two green split checks on red, with blue and green over-checks.

There are no codified rules or principles of tartan design, but a few
writers have offered some considered opinions. Banks & de La Chapelle
(2007) summarized, with a view to broad, general tartan use, including
for fashion: "Color – and how it is worked – is pivotal to tartan
design.... Thus, tartans should be composed of clear, bright colors, but
ones sufficiently soft to blend well and thereby create new shades."
James D. Scarlett (2008) noted: "the more colours to begin with, the
more subdued the final effect",^[51] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_47-62> or
put more precisely, "the more stripes to the sett and the more colours
used, the more diffuse and 'blurred' the pattern".^[7] <#cite_note-
Scarlett2008-7> That does not necessarily translate into subtlety; a
tartan of many colours and stripes can seem "busy".^[80] <#cite_note-93>

Scarlett (2008), after extensive research into historical Highland
patterns (which were dominated by rich red and medium green in about
equal weight with dark blue as a blending accent – not accounting for
common black lines), suggested that for a balanced and /traditional/
style:^[7] <#cite_note-Scarlett2008-7>

    any basic tartan type of design should have for its background, a
    "high impact" colour and two others, of which one should be the
    complement <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complementary_colors> to
    the first and the other a darker and more neutral shade; other
    colours, introduced to break up the pattern or as accents, should be
    a matter of taste. It is important that no colour should be so
    strong as to "swamp" another; otherwise, the blending of colours at
    the crossing will be adversely affected. ... Tartan is a complex
    abstract art-form with a strong mathematical undertone, far removed
    from a simple check with a few lines of contrasting colours
    scattered over it.

Scarlett (1990) provided a more general explanation, traditional styles
aside:^[81] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_45-94>

    Colours for tartan work require to be clear and unambiguous and
    bright but soft, to give good contrast of both colour and brightness
    and to mix well so as to give distinctly new shades where two
    colours cross without any one swamping another.

Further, Scarlett (1990) held that "background checks will show a firm
but not harsh contrast and the overchecks will be such as to show
clearly" on the under-check (or "background") colours.^[51] <#cite_note-
Scarlett1990_47-62> He summed up the desired total result as "a
harmonious blend of colour and pattern worthy to be looked upon as an
art form in its own right".^[82] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_7-95>

Omitting traditional black lines has a strong softening effect, as in
the 1970s Missoni fashion ensemble (top right) and in many madras
patterns (see § Indian madras <#Indian_madras>, below). A Scottish
black-less design (now the Mar <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Mar>
dress tartan) dates to the 18th century;^[83] <#cite_note-SRT_Mar-96>
another is Ruthven (1842, above), and many of the Ross tartans (e.g.
1886, above), as well as several of the Victorian–Edwardian MacDougal[l]
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_MacDougall> designs,^[84]
<#cite_note-SRT_MacDougal-97> are further examples. Various modern
tartans also use this effect, e.g. Canadian Maple Leaf (1964, at
§ Regional <#Regional>, below). Clever use of black or another dark
colour can produce a visual perception <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Optical_illusion> of depth.^[85] <#cite_note-98>


      Colour, palettes, and meaning[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/
      index.php?title=Tartan&action=edit&section=5>]

See also: Traditional dyes of the Scottish Highlands <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Traditional_dyes_of_the_Scottish_Highlands>
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:MacLeod_tartan_(Vestiarium_Scoticum).png>The brighter of the
MacLeod <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_MacLeod> tartans, known
affectionately as the "loud MacLeod", in the saturated /modern/ palette.

There is no set of exact colour standards for tartan hues; thread colour
varies from weaver to weaver even for "the same" colour.^[86]
<#cite_note-99> A certain range of general colours, however, are
traditional in Scottish tartan. These include blue (dark), crimson (rose
or dark red), green (medium-dark), black, grey (medium-dark), purple,
red (scarlet or bright), tan/brown, white (actually natural undyed wool,
called /lachdann/ in Gaelic),^[87] <#cite_note-100> ^[n]
<#cite_note-103> and yellow.^[46] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_63-56> ^[7]
<#cite_note-Scarlett2008-7> ^[o] <#cite_note-104> Some additional
colours that have been used more rarely are azure (light or sky blue),
maroon, and vert (bright or grass green),^[46] <#cite_note-
Scarlett1990_63-56> plus light grey (as seen in Balmoral tartan, though
it is sometimes given as lavender).^[90] <#cite_note-105> Since the
opening of the tartan databases to registration of newly designed
tartans, including many for organisational and fashion purposes, a wider
range of colours have been involved, such as orange^[91]
<#cite_note-106> and pink,^[92] <#cite_note-SRT_Hello_Kitty-107> which
were not often used (as distinct colours rather than as renditions of
red) in old traditional tartans.^[p] <#cite_note-108> The Scottish
Register of Tartans <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scottish_Register_of_Tartans> uses a long list of colours keyed to
hexadecimal "Web colours <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_colours>",
sorting groups of hues into a constrained set of basic codes (but
expanded upon the above traditional list, with additional options like
dark orange, dark yellow, light purple, etc.).^[93] <#cite_note-109>
This helps designers fit their creative tartan into a coding scheme
while allowing weavers to produce an approximation of that design from
readily stocked yarn supplies.

In the mid-19th century, the natural dyes <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Natural_dye> that had been traditionally used in the Highlands
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Traditional_dyes_of_the_Scottish_Highlands>^[25] <#cite_note-BdLC_61-28>
^[94] <#cite_note-Barnes_&_Allen_266-110> ^[95] <#cite_note-
StewartDW_33-111> ^[q] <#cite_note-112> (like various lichens <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lichen>, alder <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Alder> bark, bilberry <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bilberry>,
cochineal <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cochineal#Carmine>, heather
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calluna>, indigo <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indigo>, woad <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Woad>, and yellow bedstraw <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galium_verum>)
began to be replaced by artificial dyes <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Aniline#Synthetic_dye_industry>, which were easier to use and were more
economic for the booming tartan industry,^[96] <#cite_note-
TelferDunbar1979_222-224-113> though also less subtle.^[97] <#cite_note-
EsleaMacDonald2012_9-114> Although William Morris <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Morris> in the late-19th-century Arts and
Crafts movement <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arts_and_Crafts_movement>
tried to revive use of British natural dyes, most were so low-yield and
so inconsistent from locality to locality (part of the reason for the
historical tartan differentiation by area) that they proved to have
little mass-production potential, despite some purple dye (cudbear
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cudbear>) commercialisation efforts in
Glasgow in the 18th century.^[96] <#cite_note-
TelferDunbar1979_222-224-113> The hard-wound, fine wool used in tartan
weaving was rather resistant to natural dyes, and some dye baths
required days or even weeks.^[96] <#cite_note-
TelferDunbar1979_222-224-113> The dyeing also required mordants
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mordant> to fix the colours permanently,
usually metallic salts like alum <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alum>;
there are records from 1491 of alum being imported to Leith <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leith>, though not necessarily all for tartan
production in particular.^[98] <#cite_note-115> Some colours of dye were
usually imported, especially red cochineal and to some extent blue
indigo (both expensive and used to deepen native dyes), from the Low
Countries <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Low_Countries>, with which
Scotland had extensive trade since the 15th century.^[99]
<#cite_note-116> Aged human urine (called /fual/ or /graith/) was also
used, as a colour-deepener, a dye solubility <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Solubility> agent, a lichen fermenter <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Ferment>, and a final colour-fastness treatment.^[100]
<#cite_note-117> All commercially manufactured tartan today is coloured
using artificial not natural dyes, even in the less saturated colour
palettes.^[101] <#cite_note-118> ^[102] <#cite_note-Thompson_color-119>

The hues of colours in any established tartan can be altered to produce
variations of the same tartan. Such varying of the hues to taste dates
to at least the 1788 pattern book of manufacturer William Wilson & Son
of Bannockburn.^[103] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_145-120> Today, the
semi-standardised colour schemes or /palettes/ (what marketers might
call "colourways <https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/colourway>")^[104]
<#cite_note-121> are divided generally into /modern/, /ancient/, /
muted/, and /weathered/ (sometimes with other names, depending on
weaver). These terms only refer to relative dye <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dye> "colourfulness" saturation <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colorfulness> levels and do not represent distinct
tartans.^[105] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_colour-122> ^[106]
<#cite_note-Newsome_2006-123>

Modern
    Also known as /ordinary/; refers to darker tartan, with fully
    saturated colours.^[102] <#cite_note-Thompson_color-119> ^[106]
    <#cite_note-Newsome_2006-123> In a /modern/ palette, setts made up
    of blue, black, and green tend to be obscured because of the
    darkness of the colours in this scheme.^[102] <#cite_note-
    Thompson_color-119> 
Ancient
    Also known as /old colours/ (OC); refers to a lighter palette of
    tartan. These hues are ostensibly meant to represent the colours
    that would result from natural-dyed fabric aging over time. However,
    the results are not accurate (e.g., in real examples of very old
    tartan, black often fades toward khaki^[102] <#cite_note-
    Thompson_color-119> or green^[107] <#cite_note-124> while blue
    remains dark;^[102] <#cite_note-Thompson_color-119> and natural dyes
    are capable of producing some very vibrant colours in the first
    place, though not very consistently).^[106] <#cite_note-
    Newsome_2006-123> ^[81] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_45-94> ^[108]
    <#cite_note-125> This style originated in the first half of the 20th
    century.^[109] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_43-126> ^[106] <#cite_note-
    Newsome_2006-123> This /ancient/ is not to be confused with the same
    word in a few names of tartans such as "ancient Campbell".
Weathered
    Also called /faded/; refers to tartan that is even lighter (less
    saturated) than /ancient/, as if exposed for a very long time.^[106]
    <#cite_note-Newsome_2006-123> This style was invented in the late
    1940s.^[109] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_43-126> 
Muted
    Refers to tartan which is between /modern/ and /ancient/ in
    vibrancy. Although this type of colouring is very recent, dating
    only from the early 1970s, these hues are thought to be the closest
    match to the colours attained by natural dyes used before the
    mid-19th century.^[106] <#cite_note-Newsome_2006-123> 

Some particular tartan mills have introduced other colour schemes that
are unique to that weaver and only available in certain tartans. Two
examples are Lochcarron's /antique/,^[106] <#cite_note-Newsome_2006-123>
between /modern/ and /ancient/; and D. C. Dalgliesh's /reproduction/, a
slight variation on /weathered/,^[105] <#cite_note-
EsleaMacDonald_colour-122> dating to the 1940s and claimed to be based
on 18th-century samples.^[110] <#cite_note-127>

A general observation about /ancient/old/, /weathered/faded/, and /
muted/ are that they rather uniformly reduce the saturation of all
colours, while actual natural-dyed tartan samples show that the
historical practice was usually to pair one or more saturated colours
with one or more pale ones, for greater clarity and depth, a "harmonious
balance".^[111] <#cite_note-128> ^[106] <#cite_note-Newsome_2006-123>
^[105] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_colour-122> According to Scarlett
(1990): "The colours were clear, bright and soft, altogether unlike the
eye-searing brilliance or washed-out dullness of modern tartans".^[82]
<#cite_note-Scarlett1990_7-95>

The same tartan in the same palette from two manufacturers (e.g.
Colquhoun <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Colquhoun> muted from
D. C. Dalgliesh and from Strathmore) will not precisely match; there is
considerable artistic license involved in exactly how saturated to make
a hue.^[102] <#cite_note-Thompson_color-119>

Tartan-generation software can approximate the appearance of a tartan in
any of these palettes. The examples below are all the "Prince Charles
Edward Stuart" tartan:^[112] <#cite_note-SRT_PCES-129>

  *
    Modern palette <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:Prince_Charles_Edward_Stuart_(modern)_tartan,_tileable.png>
    /Modern/ palette
  *
    Ancient or old colours palette <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:Prince_Charles_Edward_Stuart_(ancient_or_old-
    colours)_tartan,_tileable.png>
    /Ancient/ or /old colours/ palette
  *
    Weathered or faded palette <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:Prince_Charles_Edward_Stuart_(weathered_or_faded)_tartan,_tileable.png>
    /Weathered/ or /faded/ palette
  *
    Muted palette <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:Prince_Charles_Edward_Stuart_(muted)_tartan,_tileable.png>
    /Muted/ palette
  *
    Lochcarron-style antique palette <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:Prince_Charles_Edward_Stuart_(antique)_tartan,_tileable.png>
    Lochcarron-style /antique/ palette
  *
    D. C. Dalgliesh-style reproduction palette <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:Prince_Charles_Edward_Stuart_(reproduction)_tartan,_tileable.png>
    D. C. Dalgliesh-style /reproduction/ palette

Scottish tartans that use two or more hues of the same basic colour are
fairly rare. The best known is the British royal family's Balmoral^[113]
<#cite_note-130> (1853, two greys, both as under-check – see
illustration at § Family and individual <#Family_and_individual>,
below). Others include: Akins <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Akins>^[114] <#cite_note-131> (1850, two reds, one as over-check and
sometimes rendered purple), MacBean <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Clan_MacBean>^[115] <#cite_note-SRT_MacBean-132> (1872, two reds, one as
over-check and sometimes rendered purple), Childers Universal
regimental^[116] <#cite_note-133> (1907, two greens, both under-check),
Gordon <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Gordon> red^[117]
<#cite_note-134> (recorded 1930–1950 but probably considerably older;
two blues and two reds, one of each used more or less as over-checks),
Galloway <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galloway> district hunting/
green^[118] <#cite_note-135> ^[119] <#cite_note-136> (1939/1950s, two
greens, both under-check), US Air Force Reserve <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Air_Force_Reserve_Command> Pipe Band^[120]
<#cite_note-137> (1988, two blues, both under-check), McCandlish
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/McCandlish>^[121] <#cite_note-138> ^[122]
<#cite_note-139> ^[123] <#cite_note-140> (1992, three variants, all
under-check), Isle of Skye <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isle_of_Skye>
district^[124] <#cite_note-141> (1992, three greens, all arguably under-
check, nested within each other), and Chisholm <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chisholm_(surname)> Colonial^[125]
<#cite_note-142> (2008, two blues, one an over-check, the other nearly
blended into green). The practice is more common in very recent
commercial tartans that have no association with Scottish families or
districts, such as the Loverboy <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Charles_Jeffrey_(fashion_designer)> fashion label tartan^[126]
<#cite_note-SRT_Loverboy-143> (2018, three blues, one an over-check).

The idea that the various colours used in tartan have a specific meaning
is purely a modern one,^[127] <#cite_note-144> notwithstanding a legend
that red tartans were "battle tartans", designed so they would not show
blood. It is only recently created tartans, such as Canadian provincial
and territorial tartans <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Regional_tartans_of_Canada> (beginning 1950s) and US state tartans
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_U.S._state_tartans> (beginning
1980s), that are stated to be designed with certain symbolic meaning for
the colours used. For example, green sometimes represents prairies
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prairie> or forests, blue can represent
lakes and rivers, and yellow might stand for various crops.^[128]
<#cite_note-MacDonald1995_48-145> In the /Scottish Register of Tartans/
(and the databases before it), colour inspiration notes are often
recorded by a tartan's designer. However, there is no common set of
tartan colour or pattern "motifs" with allusive <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allusion> meanings that is shared among
designers.^[r] <#cite_note-148>

More abstractly, from an art criticism <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Art_criticism> perspective, design historian Richard Martin <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Martin_(curator)> (1988) wrote of tartans
as designs and tartan as a textile class having no truly endemic or
objectified meanings, but being an art that "has the property of being a
vessel or container of meaning, a design form that exists not only in
history but through history", capable of conveying radically different,
even contradictory, contextual meanings "ever changing and evolving"
through socio-cultural transmutation of the fabric's use. Thus tartan
could veer from symbol of anti-union and Jacobite Highland rebellion to
emblem of pan-British loyalty to empire in the space of two generations,
or serve different fashion markets in the same recent decades as both a
sartorial status symbol of traditional values and a punk and grunge
rebel banner.^[131] <#cite_note-149>


    Early history[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
    title=Tartan&action=edit&section=6>]


      Pre-medieval origins[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
      title=Tartan&action=edit&section=7>]

Classical Roman writers made various references to the continental Gauls
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauls>, south of Britain, wearing striped
or variegated clothing; Latin seems to have lacked an exact word for
'checked'. For example, Virgil <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virgil> in
the Aeneid <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aeneid> (29–19 BC, book VIII,
line 660) described the Gauls as /virgatis lucent sagulis/ (or /
sagalis/) meaning something like 'they shine in striped cloaks' or
'their cloaks are striped brightly'.^[132] <#cite_note-
Thompson1992_iv-150> ^[133] <#cite_note-151> ^[134] <#cite_note-
Adam_385-152> Other writers used words such as /pictae/ and /virgatae/
^[135] <#cite_note-StewartDW_Cody-153> with translations like 'marled',
'variegated', 'particoloured', etc. Scarlett (1990) warns: "What is not
reasonable is the ready assumption by many modern authors that every
time one of these words, or something like it, was used, tartan was
intended."^[136] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_10-154> It might have been
intended sometimes, or the writer might have just meant linear stripes
like seersucker <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seersucker> cloth. Both
Scarlett and Thompson (1992) decry the unsustainable assumption by a few
earlier modern writers (e.g. James Grant, 1886) that Gauls must have
been running around in clan tartans.^[136] <#cite_note-
Scarlett1990_10-154> ^[132] <#cite_note-Thompson1992_iv-150> The Romans
particularly wrote of Gauls as wearing striped /braccae <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Braccae>/ (trousers). E. G. Cody in remarks in his
1885 edition of John Lesley <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
John_Lesley>'s /Historie of Scotland/ hypothesized that this was
actually a Gaulish loanword <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Loanword> and
was cognate <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cognate> with Gaelic /
breacan/.^[135] <#cite_note-StewartDW_Cody-153> This is one of many
"tartan legends" that is not well accepted; rather, /braccae/ is
considered by modern linguists <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Linguistics> a cognate of English /breeches/, Gaelic /briogais/
('trousers'), etc.^[137] <#cite_note-155>

The earliest documented tartan-like cloth in Britain, known as the
"Falkirk tartan",^[138] <#cite_note-156> dates from the 3rd
century AD.^[139] <#cite_note-Chen-157> It was uncovered at Falkirk
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Falkirk> in Stirlingshire, Scotland, near
the Antonine Wall <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antonine_Wall>. The
fragment, held in the National Museum of Scotland <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Museum_of_Scotland>, was stuffed into the
mouth of an earthenware pot containing almost 2,000 Roman <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Britain> coins.^[140] <#cite_note-158> The
Falkirk tartan has a simple "Border check" <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Border_tartan> design, of undyed light and dark wool.^[s]
<#cite_note-159> Other evidence from this period is the surviving
fragment of a statue of Roman Emperor <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Roman_Empire> Caracalla <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caracalla>, once
part of the triumphal arch <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Triumphal_arch> of Volubilis <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volubilis>
completed in 217 AD. It depicts a Caledonian <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Caledonia> Pictish <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Picts> prisoner
wearing tartan trews <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trews> (represented
by carving a checked design then inlaying <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Inlay> it with bronze and silver alloys <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Alloy> to give a variegated appearance).^[141] <#cite_note-160>
^[t] <#cite_note-161> Based on such evidence, tartan researcher James D.
Scarlett (1990) believes Scottish tartan to be "of Pictish or earlier
origin",^[142] <#cite_note-162> though Brown (2012) notes there is no
way to prove or disprove this.^[143] <#cite_note-163>

Early forms of tartan like this are thought to have been invented in
pre-Roman times, and would have been popular among the inhabitants of
the northern Roman provinces <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Roman_province>^[144] <#cite_note-164> ^[145] <#cite_note-165> as well
as in other parts of Northern Europe <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Northern_Europe> such as Jutland <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Jutland>, where the same pattern was prevalent,^[146] <#cite_note-166>
^[147] <#cite_note-167> ^[148] <#cite_note-168> and Bronze Age <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bronze_Age> Sweden <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Sweden>.^[149] <#cite_note-169>

That twill <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Twill> weave was selected,
even in ancient times, is probably no accident; "plain (2/2) twill for a
given gauge of yarn, yields a cloth 50% heavier [denser] – and hence
more weather-proof – than the simple 1/1 weave <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plain_weave>."^[7] <#cite_note-Scarlett2008-7>
According to Scarlett (2008):^[7] <#cite_note-Scarlett2008-7>

    [T]here are sound reasons why such a type of pattern-textile should
    have developed almost automatically in isolated, self-sufficient ...
    communities. Such communities are unlikely to possess large dye-
    vats, and so cannot piece-dye woven cloth; such processes as batik
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Batik> and tie-dye <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tie-dye> are unavailable. ... Stripes are the
    practical solution, since they use small quantities of a colour at a
    time and are interspersed with other colours, but the scope is
    limited ...; stripes across both brighten the colours and add many
    mixtures. From there on it is really only a matter of getting
    organised; the now-geometric pattern reduces to a small unit, easier
    to remember and to follow in a world where little was written down;
    it is further simplified by being split into two equal halves and,
    with weft as warp, the weft pattern can be followed from the warp.


      Medieval[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
      title=Tartan&action=edit&section=8>]

Fairly crude painting (detail) of a man using some kind of tool, wearing
a cotehardie (tunic) of half red cloth and half complex tartan <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Plaid_cotehardie.jpg>Detail of Spanish
altarpiece by the "Master of Estamariu <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Estamariu>", late 14th century, showing a particoloured cotehardie
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cotehardie> with a three-colour, complex
tartan

There is little written or pictorial evidence about tartan (much less
surviving tartan cloth) from the Medieval era <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Middle_Ages>. Tartan use in Britain between the 3rd-century Falkirk
tartan and 16th-century samples, writings, and art is unclear.^[150]
<#cite_note-BdLC_66-68-170> ^[151] <#cite_note-171> Cosmo Innes
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmo_Innes> (1860) wrote that, according
to medieval hagiographies <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hagiography>,
Scots of the 7th–8th centuries "used cloaks of variegated colour,
apparently of home manufacture".^[152] <#cite_note-172> Based on
similarities of tartans used by various clans, including the Murrays
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Murray>, Sutherlands <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Sutherland>, and Gordons <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Gordon>, and the history of their family
interactions over the centuries, Thomas Innes of Learney <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Innes_of_Learney> estimated that a regional
"parent" pattern, of a more general style, might date to the 12th or
13th century,^[153] <#cite_note-Innes_of_Learney_10-173> but this is
quite speculative. The cartularies <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Cartulary> of Aberdeen <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aberdeen> in the
13th century barred clergymen from wearing "striped" clothing, which
could have referred to tartan.^[154] <#cite_note-174>

In 1333, Italian Gothic artists <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Gothic_art> Simone Martini <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Simone_Martini> and Lippo Memmi <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Lippo_Memmi> produced the /Annunciation with St. Margaret and St.
Ansanus <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Annunciation_with_St._Margaret_and_St._Ansanus>/, a wood-panel painting
in tempera <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tempera> and gold leaf
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_leaf>. It features the archangel
Gabriel in a tartan-patterned mantle <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Simone_Martini_080.jpg>, with light highlights where the darker
stripes meet, perhaps representing jewels, embroidery <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Embroidery>, or supplementary weaving <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supplementary_weaving>. Art historians consider it
an example of "Tartar" (Mongol) textile influence <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Mongol_elements_in_Western_medieval_art#Mongol_Empire_%22Tatar%22_textiles_in_medieval_art>; it likely has no relation to Scottish tartan.^[u] <#cite_note-175> "Tartar" cloth came in a great array of patterns, many more complex than tartan (such as the fine detail in Gabriel's robe in the same painting); patterns of this sort were influential especially on Italian art in the 14th century.

There are several other continental European paintings of tartan-like
garments from around this era (even back to the 13th century), but most
of them show very simple two-colour basic check <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Check_(pattern)> patterns, or (like the Martini
and Memmi /Annunciation/ example) broad squares made by thin lines of
one colour on a background of another. Any of them could represent
embroidery or patchwork <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Patchwork> rather
than woven tartan. There seems to be no indication in surviving records
of tartan material being imported from Scotland in this period. In the
second half of the 14th century, the artist known only as the "Master of
Estamariu <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Estamariu>" (in Catalonia,
Spain) painted an altarpiece of St Vincent, one of the details of which
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Plaid_cotehardie.jpg> is a man in a
cotehardie <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cotehardie> that is red on one
half and a complex three-colour tartan on the other, which is very
similar to later-attested Scottish tartans.

Sir Francis James Grant <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Francis_James_Grant>, mid-20th-century Lord Lyon King of Arms <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lord_Lyon_King_of_Arms>, noted that records showed
the wearing of tartan in Scotland to date as far back as 1440.^[155]
<#cite_note-176> However, it is unclear to which records he was
referring, and other, later researchers have not matched this early date.


      16th century[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
      title=Tartan&action=edit&section=9>]

A faded and somewhat tattered span of tartan cloth, presently looking
tan with various dark lines across it <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Glen_Affric_tartan.jpg>The Glen Affric tartan (c. 1500–1600 AD),
discovered in a peat bog in the 1980s

Man in belted yellow checkered tunic, black shoes, blue shorts, and
light-red cloak, with sword and dirk. <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:%C3%89scossois_sauvage_(Lucas_de_Heere,_c._1567-80).jpg>/Éscossois
sauvage/ ('Savage Scotsman') by Lucas de Heere <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lucas_de_Heere>, c. 1567–80

The oldest surviving sample of complex, dyed-wool tartan (not just a
simple check pattern) in Scotland has been shown through radiocarbon
dating <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating> to be from the
16th century; known as the "Glen Affric tartan", it was discovered in
the early 1980s in a peat bog <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peat_bog>
near Glen Affric <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glen_Affric> in the
Scottish Highlands; its faded colours include green, brown, red, and
yellow. On loan from the Scottish Tartans Authority <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_Tartans_Authority>, the 55 cm × 42 cm
(22 in × 17 in) artefact went on display at the V&A Dundee <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/V%26A_Dundee> museum in April 2023.^[139]
<#cite_note-Chen-157> ^[156] <#cite_note-177> ^[157] <#cite_note-178>
^[158] <#cite_note-179> ^[v] <#cite_note-180>

The earliest certain written reference to tartan by name is in the 1532–
33 accounts of the Treasurer of Scotland: "Ane uthir tartane galcoit
gevin to the king be the Maister Forbes" ('Another tartan coat given to
the king by the Master Forbes'),^[5] <#cite_note-DOST-5> followed not
long after by a 1538 Scottish Exchequer <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Exchequer> accounting of clothing to be ordered for King James V of
Scotland <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_V_of_Scotland>, which
referred to "heland tertane to be hoiss" ('Highland tartan to be hose
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hose_(clothing)>').^[159] <#cite_note-
SRT_NRS-181> ^[160] <#cite_note-182> ^[w] <#cite_note-183> Plaids were
featured a bit earlier; poet William Dunbar <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/William_Dunbar> (c. 1459 – c. 1530) mentions "Five thousand ellis
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ell> ... Of Hieland pladdis".^[161]
<#cite_note-184> The earliest surviving image of a Highlander in what
was probably meant to represent tartan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:%C3%89scossois_sauvage_(Lucas_de_Heere,_c._1567-80).jpg> is a 1567–
80 watercolour <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Watercolour> by Lucas de
Heere <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lucas_de_Heere>, showing a man in a
belted, pleated yellow tunic with a thin-lined checked pattern, a light-
red cloak, and tight blue shorts (of a type also seen in period Irish
art), with claymore <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Claymore> and dirk
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dirk>.^[162] <#cite_note-185> It looks
much like medieval illustrations of "Tartar" cloth and thus cannot be
certain to represent true tartan. By the late 16th century, there are
numerous references to striped or checked plaids. Supposedly, the
earliest pattern that is still produced today (though not in continual
use) is the Lennox district tartan,^[163] <#cite_note-186> (also adopted
as the clan tartan of Lennox <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Clan_Lennox>)^[164] <#cite_note-187> said to have been reproduced by
D. W. Stewart in 1893 from a portrait of Margaret Douglas <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Margaret_Douglas>, Countess of Lennox, dating to
around 1575.^[165] <#cite_note-SCSC_history-188> However, this seems to
be legend, as no modern tartan researchers or art historians have
identified such a portrait, and the earliest known realistic one of a
woman in tartan dates much later, to c. 1700.^[166] <#cite_note-
EsleaMacDonald2016-189> Extant portraits of Margaret show her in velvet
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Velvet> and brocade <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brocade>.^[167] <#cite_note-190>

Tartan and Highland dress in the Elizabethan era <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elizabethan_era> have been said to have become
essentially classless <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_class>^[x]
<#cite_note-196>  – worn in the Highlands by everyone from high-born
lairds <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laird> to common crofters
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Croft_(land)>,^[173] <#cite_note-
Spinning-197> at least by the late 16th century. The historian John
Major <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Major_(philosopher)> wrote in
1521 that it was the upper class, including warriors, who wore plaids
while the common among them wore linen, suggesting that woollen cloth
was something of a luxury.^[174] <#cite_note-198> But by 1578, Bishop
John Lesley <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Lesley> of Ross wrote
that the belted plaid <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Belted_plaid> was
the general Highland costume of both rich and poor, with the nobility
simply able to afford larger plaids with more colours.^[171]
<#cite_note-StewartDW_7-194> (Later, Burt (1726) also wrote of gentlemen
having larger plaids than commoners.)^[20] <#cite_note-Mackay_94-23> If
colours conveyed distinction, it was of social class not clan.^[175]
<#cite_note-Trevor-Roper_23-199> D. W. Stewart (1893) attributed the
change, away from linen, to broader manufacture of woollen cloth and
"the increased prosperity of the people".^[171] <#cite_note-
StewartDW_7-194>

Many writers of the period drew parallels between Irish and Highland
dress, especially the wearing of a long yellow-dyed shirt called the /
léine <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/L%C3%A9ine>/ or saffron shirt
(though probably not actually dyed with expensive imported saffron
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saffron>),^[176] <#cite_note-200> worn
with a mantle (cloak) over it, and sometimes with trews <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trews>.^[177] <#cite_note-201> It is not entirely
certain when these mantles were first made of tartan in the Highlands,
but the distinctive cloth seems to get its recorded mentions first in
the 16th century, starting with Major (1521). In 1556, Jean de Beaugué
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jean_de_Beaugu%C3%A9>, a French witness
of Scottish troops on the continent at the 1548 Siege of Haddington
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Siege_of_Haddington>, distinguished
Lowlanders from Highland "savages", and wrote of the latter as wearing
shirts "and a certain light covering made of wool of various
colours".^[178] <#cite_note-202> ^[179] <#cite_note-Adam_387-203> George
Buchanan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_Buchanan> in 1582 wrote
that "plaids of many colours" had a long tradition but that the Highland
fashion by his era had mostly shifted to a plainer look, especially
brown tones, as a practical matter of camouflage.^[180] <#cite_note-204>
^[y] <#cite_note-207> Fynes Moryson <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Fynes_Moryson> wrote in 1598 (published 1617) of common Highland women
wearing "plodan", "a course stuffe, of two or three colours in Checker
worke".^[183] <#cite_note-208>

A man in belted plaid, blue bonnet, and diced hose, with longbow and
sword; woman in plaid as a cloak, over a reddish-orange dress, with a
lace collar and lace head covering. <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Highlanders_by_Hieronymus_Tielsch,_c._1603%E2%80%931616.png>Highland man and woman in tartan, c. 1603–1616, by Hieronymus Tielsch <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hieronymus_Tielsch&action=edit&redlink=1>. The crude attempt to represent tartan shows a blue and green pattern with red over-check, but did not blend the colours.^[z] <#cite_note-Tielsch-210>

Its dense weave requiring specialised skills and equipment, tartan was
not generally one individual's work but something of an early cottage
industry <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Putting-
out_system#Cottage_industry> in the Highlands – an often communal
activity called /calanas/, including some associated folk singing
traditions <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Waulking_song> – with several
related occupational specialties (wool comber, dyer, waulker <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Waulking>, warp <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Warp_and_weft>-winder, weaver) among people in a village, part-time or
full-time,^[185] <#cite_note-211> especially women.^[186]
<#cite_note-212> ^[aa] <#cite_note-215> The spinning wheel <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spinning_wheel> was a late technological arrival
in the Highlands, and tartan in this era was woven from fine (but fairly
inconsistent) hard-spun yarn that was spun by hand on drop spindles
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spindle_(textiles)>.^[7] <#cite_note-
Scarlett2008-7> The era's commerce in tartans was centred on Inverness
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inverness>, the early business records of
which are filled with many references to tartan goods.^[189]
<#cite_note-216> Tartan patterns were loosely associated with the
weavers of particular areas, owing in part to differences in
availability of natural dyes,^[96] <#cite_note-
TelferDunbar1979_222-224-113> ^[190] <#cite_note-217> ^[94] <#cite_note-
Barnes_&_Allen_266-110> ^[191] <#cite_note-218> and it was common for
Highlanders to wear whatever was available to them,^[1] <#cite_note-
Newsome_1994-1> often a number of different tartans at the same
time.^[192] <#cite_note-219> ^[ab] <#cite_note-221> The early tartans
found in east-coastal Scotland used red more often, probably because of
easier continental-European trade in the red dye cochineal <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cochineal>, while western tartans were more often
in blues and greens, owing to the locally available dyes.^[165]
<#cite_note-SCSC_history-188> (See also § Colour, palettes, and meaning
<#Colour,_palettes,_and_meaning>.) The greater expense of red dye may
have also made it a status symbol <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Status_symbol>.^[194] <#cite_note-222> Tartan spread at least somewhat
out of the Highlands, but was not universally well received. The General
Assembly of the Kirk of Scotland <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Kirk_of_Scotland> in 1575 prohibited the ministers and readers of the
church (and their wives) from wearing tartan plaids and other "sumptuous
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sumptuary_law>" clothing,^[195]
<#cite_note-223> ^[196] <#cite_note-224> while the council of Aberdeen,
"a district by no means Highland", in 1576 banned the wearing of plaids
(probably meaning belted plaids).^[197] <#cite_note-StewartDW_10-11-225>

A 1594 Irish account by Lughaidh Ó Cléirigh <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Lughaidh_%C3%93_Cl%C3%A9irigh> of Scottish gallowglass mercenaries
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gallowglass> in Ireland clearly describes
the belted plaid, "a mottled garment with numerous colours hanging in
folds to the calf of the leg, with a girdle round the loins over the
garment".^[198] <#cite_note-226> The privately organised early
"plantations <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Plantations_of_Ireland>" (colonies) and later governmental Plantation of
Ulster <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plantation_of_Ulster> brought
tartan weaving to Northern Ireland in the late 16th to early 17th
centuries.^[199] <#cite_note-227> Many of the new settlers were Scots,
and they joined the population already well-established there by
centuries of gallowglass and other immigrants. In 1956, the earliest
surviving piece of Irish tartan cloth was discovered in peaty loam just
outside Dungiven <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dungiven> in Northern
Ireland <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_Ireland>, in the form of
tartan trews <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trews>, along with other
non-tartan clothing items.^[200] <#cite_note-228> It was dubbed the
"Dungiven tartan" or "Ulster tartan".^[201] <#cite_note-229> The sample
was dated using palynology <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palynology> to
c. 1590–1650^[202] <#cite_note-Wilton_2019-230> ^[203] <#cite_note-
PatersonMB_182-231> (the soil that surrounded the cloth was saturated
with pollen from Scots pine <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scots_pine>,
a species imported to Ulster from Scotland by plantationers).^[204]
<#cite_note-SmithC-232> ^[19] <#cite_note-Newsome_Wilkinson-21>
According to archaeological textile expert Audrey Henshall <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Audrey_Henshall>, the cloth was probably woven in
County Donegal <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/County_Donegal>, Ireland,
but the trews tailored in the Scottish Highlands^[204] <#cite_note-
SmithC-232> ^[205] <#cite_note-Newsome_disguise-233> at some expense,
suggesting someone of rank,^[206] <#cite_note-234> possibly a
gallowglass.^[202] <#cite_note-Wilton_2019-230> Henshall reproduced the
tartan for a 1958 exhibit;^[204] <#cite_note-SmithC-232> ^[19]
<#cite_note-Newsome_Wilkinson-21> it became popular (and heavily
promoted) as a district tartan for Ulster^[19] <#cite_note-
Newsome_Wilkinson-21> (both in a faded form, like it was found,^[207]
<#cite_note-235> and a bright palette that attempted to reproduce what
it may have originally looked like),^[208] <#cite_note-236> and seems to
have inspired the later creation of more Irish district tartans.^[19]
<#cite_note-Newsome_Wilkinson-21> ^[209] <#cite_note-
Newsome_rethinking-237> (see § Regional <#Regional>, below). There is
nearly nothing in period source material to suggest that the Irish also
habitually wore tartan; one of the only sources that can possibly be
interpreted in support of the idea is William Camden <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Camden>, who wrote in his /Britannia/
(since at least the 1607 edition) that "Highlandmen ... wear after the
Irish fashion striped mantles".^[210] <#cite_note-StewartDW_15-238>
^[211] <#cite_note-239> ^[ac] <#cite_note-240>


      17th century[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
      title=Tartan&action=edit&section=10>]

<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Scottish_mercenaries_in_the_Thirty_Years_War.jpg>The earliest image
of Scottish soldiers wearing tartan belted plaids and trews; 1631 German
engraving by Georg Köler <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
title=Georg_K%C3%B6ler&action=edit&redlink=1>.

The earliest unambiguous surviving image of Highlanders in an
approximation of tartan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Highlanders_by_Hieronymus_Tielsch,_c._1603%E2%80%931616.png> is a
watercolour, dating to c. 1603–1616 and rediscovered in the late 20th
century, by Hieronymus Tielsch <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
title=Hieronymus_Tielsch&action=edit&redlink=1> or Tielssch. It shows a
man's belted plaid, and a woman's plaid (arisaid, /earasaid/) worn as a
shawl or cloak over a dress, and also depicts diced short hose and a
blue bonnet <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blue_bonnet>.^[184]
<#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_53-209> ^[212] <#cite_note-241> ^[z]
<#cite_note-Tielsch-210> Clans had for a long time independently raised
militias <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Militia>, and starting in 1603,
the British government itself mustered irregular <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irregular_military> militia units in the
Highlands, known as the Independent Highland Companies <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Independent_Highland_Companies> (IHCs).^[213]
<#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_155-242> Being Highlanders, they were
probably wearing tartan (1631 Highland mercenaries certainly were, and
the ICHs were in tartan in 1709^[213] <#cite_note-
TelferDunbar1979_155-242> and actual uniforms of tartan by 1725).^[214]
<#cite_note-Newsome_Black_Watch-243> ^[215] <#cite_note-
Anderson_1939-244> ^[216] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_26-27-245> Tartan was
used as a furnishing fabric <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Domestic_furnishing_in_early_modern_Scotland>, including bed hangings
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bed_hangings> at Ardstinchar Castle
