<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ardstinchar_Castle> in 1605.^[217]
<#cite_note-246> After mention of Highlanders' "striped mantles" in
Camden's /Britannia/ of 1607,^[210] <#cite_note-StewartDW_15-238> poet
John Taylor <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Taylor_(poet)> wrote in
1618 in /The Pennyless Pilgrimage/ of "tartane" Highland garb in detail
(in terms that generally match what was described and illustrated even
two centuries later); he noted that it was worn not just by locals but
also by visiting British gentlemen.^[ad] <#cite_note-248> ^[ae]
<#cite_note-249> The council of Aberdeen again cracked down on plaids in
1621, this time against their use as women's head-wear,^[197]
<#cite_note-StewartDW_10-11-225> and the kirk in Glasgow <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glasgow> had previously, in 1604, forbidden their
wear during services;^[219] <#cite_note-250> similar kirk session
rulings appeared in Elgin <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elgin,_Moray>
in 1624, in Kinghorn <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kinghorn> in 1642
and 1644, and Monifieth <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monifieth> in
1643, with women's plaids more literarily censured in Edinburgh in 1633
by William Lithgow <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
William_Lithgow_(traveller_and_author)>.^[220] <#cite_note-251> In 1622,
the Baron Courts <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manorial_court> of
Breadalbane <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Breadalbane,_Scotland> set
fixed prices for different complexities of tartan and plain cloth.^[221]
<#cite_note-252>

In 1627, a tartan-dressed body of Highland archers served under the Earl
of Morton <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
William_Douglas,_7th_Earl_of_Morton>.^[222] <#cite_note-253> More
independent companies were raised in 1667.^[213] <#cite_note-
TelferDunbar1979_155-242> The earliest image of Scottish soldiers in
tartan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Scottish_mercenaries_in_the_Thirty_Years_War.jpg> is a 1631
copperplate engraving <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Copperplate_engraving> by Georg Köler <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/
index.php?title=Georg_K%C3%B6ler&action=edit&redlink=1> (1600–1638); it
features Highland mercenaries of the Thirty Years' War <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War> in the forces of Gustavus
Adolphus <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gustavus_Adolphus> of
Sweden.^[223] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_12-254> ^[224] <#cite_note-255>
Not long after, James Gordon, Parson of Rothiemay <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rothiemay>, wrote in /A History of Scots Affairs
from 1637 to 1641/ of the belted plaid as "a loose Cloke of several Ells
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ell>, striped and party colour'd, which
they gird breadthwise with a Leathern Belt ...." He also described the
short hose and trews ("trowzes").^[225] <#cite_note-256> A 1653 map, /
Scotia Antiqua/ <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Blaeu_-
_Atlas_of_Scotland_1654_-_SCOTIA_ANTIQUA_-_Old_Scotland.jpg> by Joan
Blaeu <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joan_Blaeu>, features a cartouche
that depicts men in trews and belted plaid; the tartan is crudely
represented as just thin lines on a plain background,^[226]
<#cite_note-257> and various existing copies are hand-coloured
differently. Daniel Defoe <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Daniel_Defoe>,
in /Memoirs of a Cavalier/ (c. 1720) wrote, using materials that
probably dated to the English Civil War <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
English_Civil_War>, of Highlanders invading Northern England back in
1639 that they had worn "doublet, breeches and stockings, of a stuff
they called plaid, striped across red and yellow, with short cloaks of
the same".^[227] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_28-29-258>

Besides the formerly often-chastised wearing of head-plaids in church,
women's dress was not often described (except in earlier times as being
similar to men's).^[af] <#cite_note-260> The Highland and island women's
equivalent of the belted plaid was the arisaid <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arisaid> (/earasaid/), a plaid that could be worn
as a large shawl or be wrapped into a dress. Sir William Brereton
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sir_William_Brereton,_1st_Baronet> had
written in 1634–35 (published 1844) of Lowland women in Edinburgh that:
"Many wear (especially of the meaner sort) plaids ... which is [/sic
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sic>/] cast over their heads and covers
their faces on both sides, and would reach almost to the ground, but
that they pluck them up, and wear them cast under their arms." He also
reported that women there wore "six or seven several habits and
fashions, some for distinction of widows, wives and maids", including
gowns, capes/cloaks, bonnets with bongrace <https://en.wiktionary.org/
wiki/bongrace> veils, and collar ruffs, though he did not address tartan
patterns in particular in such garments.^[229] <#cite_note-261>

While tartan was still made in the Highlands as cottage industry, by
1655 production had centred on Aberdeen <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Aberdeen>, made there "in greater plenty than [in] any other place of
the nation whatsoever",^[21] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_40-24> though
it was also manufactured in Glasgow <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Glasgow>, Montrose <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Montrose,_Angus>, and
Dundee <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dundee>, much of it for
export.^[21] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_40-24> In Glasgow at least,
some of the trade was in tartan manufactured in the Highlands and the
Hebrides and brought there for sale along with hides and other
goods.^[21] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_40-24> Impressed by the trade
in Glasgow, Richard Franck <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Richard_Franck_(captain)> in his /Northern Memoirs/ of 1658 wrote that
the cloth was "the staple of this country".^[230] <#cite_note-262> In
1662, the naturalist John Ray <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Ray>
wrote of the "party coloured blanket which [Scots] call a plad, over
their heads and shoulders", and commented that a Scotsman even of the
lower class was "clad like a gentleman" because the habit in this time
was to spend extraordinarily on clothing,^[231] <#cite_note-263> a habit
that seems to have gone back to the late 16th century.^[232]
<#cite_note-264> A Thomas Kirk of Yorkshire commented on trews, plaids,
and possibly kilts of "plaid colour" in 1677;^[233] <#cite_note-265>
more material by Kirk was printed in the 1891 /Early Travellers in
Scotland/ edited by Peter Hume Brown <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Peter_Hume_Brown>, recording "plad wear" in the form of belted plaids,
trews, and hose.^[234] <#cite_note-266> A poem by William Cleland
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Cleland_(poet)> in 1678 had
Scottish officers in trews and shoulder plaids, and soldiers in belted
plaids.^[235] <#cite_note-267> In 1689, Thomas Morer, an English
clergyman to Scottish regiments, described Lowland women as frequently
wearing plaids despite otherwise dressing mostly like the English.^[236]
<#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_97-268>

Oil portrait of a young man in red, tan, and black tartan belted plaid,
white shirt, split jerkin of gold embroidery, red diced short hose, and
broad flat cap, with musket <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:John_Michael_Wright_-
_Lord_Mungo_Murray_(Am_Morair_Mungo_Moireach),_1668_-
_1700._Son_of_1st_Marquess_of_Atholl_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg>Mungo
Murray, c. 1683, by John Michael Wright <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
John_Michael_Wright> (Scottish National Portrait Gallery <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_National_Portrait_Gallery> version),
featuring a very complex tartan

The earliest known realistic portrait in tartan Highland dress <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:John_Michael_Wright_-
_Lord_Mungo_Murray_(Am_Morair_Mungo_Moireach),_1668_-
_1700._Son_of_1st_Marquess_of_Atholl_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg> is a
piece (which exists in three versions) by John Michael Wright <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Michael_Wright>, showing a very complicated
tartan of brown, black, and two hues of red;^[237] <#cite_note-269> it
is dated to c. 1683 and is of Mungo Murray, son of John Murray, Marquess
of Atholl <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
John_Murray,_1st_Marquess_of_Atholl>.^[238] <#cite_note-270> ^[ag]
<#cite_note-272>

In 1688, William Sacheverell, lieutenant governor of the Isle of Man
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lieutenant_governor_of_the_Isle_of_Man>,
wrote of the tartan plaids of the women of Mull <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isle_of_Mull> in the Inner Hebrides <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inner_Hebrides> as "much finer, the colours more
lively, and the squares larger than the men's .... This serves them for
a veil, and covers both head and body."^[240] <#cite_note-Stewart/
Dunbar_Sacheverell-273> In the 1691 poem /The Grameid/,^[241]
<#cite_note-Grameid-274> James Philip of Almerieclose described the 1689
Battle of Killiecrankie <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Battle_of_Killiecrankie> in terms that seem to suggest that some clan
militias had uniform tartan liveries <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Livery>, and some historians have interpreted it thus.^[242]
<#cite_note-StewartDW_22-23-275> ^[243] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_13-276>


      18th century[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
      title=Tartan&action=edit&section=11>]

It is not until early 18th century that regional uniformity in tartan,
sufficient to identify the area of origin, is reported to have
occurred.^[150] <#cite_note-BdLC_66-68-170> Martin Martin <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martin_Martin>, in /A Description of the Western
Islands of Scotland/, published in 1703, wrote, after describing trews
and belted plaids "of divers Colours ... agreeable to the nicest Fancy",
that tartans could be used to distinguish the inhabitants of different
places.^[ah] <#cite_note-279> Martin did not mention anything like the
use of a special pattern by each family.

In 1709, the Independent Highland Companies <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Independent_Highland_Companies> were wearing everyday Highland
dress, not uniforms of a particular tartan, to better blend in with
civilians and detect Jacobite <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jacobitism>
treachery.^[213] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_155-242> In 1713, the
Royal Company of Archers <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Royal_Company_of_Archers> (a royal bodyguard unit first formed in
1676),^[246] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2012-280> became the first unit
in service to the British crown who adopted a particular tartan as a
part of their formal uniform. The militiamen of Clan Grant <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Grant> may have been all in green-and-red
tartan (details unspecified) as early as 1703–04^[247] <#cite_note-
StewartDW_26-28-281> ^[165] <#cite_note-SCSC_history-188> and wearing a
uniform tartan livery by 1715.^[248] <#cite_note-Mackay_50-282> It is
not a surviving pattern, and modern Grant tartans are of much later
date.^[249] <#cite_note-283> (For details on early uniform tartans, see
Regimental tartan § Pre-regiment military use <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Regimental_tartan#Pre-regiment_military_use>.)

An account of the Highland men in 1711 had it that they all, including
"those of the better sort", wore the belted plaid.^[250] <#cite_note-
StewartDW_29-284> A 1723 account suggested that gentlemen, at least when
commingling with the English, were more likely to wear tartan trews and
hose with their attendants in the belted plaid,^[250] <#cite_note-
StewartDW_29-284> which Burt also observed;^[251] <#cite_note-285> trews
were also more practical for horseback riding.^[252] <#cite_note-286>
Also around 1723, short tartan jackets, called in Gaelic /còta-goirid/,
sometimes with slashed sleeves and worn with a matching waistcoat, made
their first appearance and began supplanting, in Highland dress, the
plain-coloured doublets <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Doublet_(clothing)> that were common throughout European dress of the
era; the /còta-goirid/ was often worn with matching trews and a shoulder
plaid that might or might not match, but could also be worn with a
belted plaid.^[253] <#cite_note-287> ^[ai] <#cite_note-288>

Oil painting of a middle-aged woman in a blue white dress with brown
leather belt, with a plaid around her shoulders of a red-and-blue tartan
with thin white over-checks <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Rachel_Gordon_of_Abergeldie_c._1700.jpg>Rachel Gordon of
Abergeldie, c. 1700 – the earliest known formal portrait of a woman in
tartan

M. Martin (1703) wrote that the "vulgar" Hebridean <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hebrides> women still wore the arisaid <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arisaid> wrap/dress,^[254] <#cite_note-289>
describing it as "a white Plad, having a few small Stripes of black,
blue, and red; it reach'd from the Neck to the Heels, and was tied
before on the Breast with a Buckle of Silver, or Brass", some very
ornate. He said they also wore a decorated belt, scarlet sleeves, and
head kerchiefs of linen.^[255] <#cite_note-290> Martin was not the only
period source to suggest it was primarily the wear of the common women,
with upper-class Highland ladies in the 18th century more likely to
weartailored gowns, dresses, and riding habits <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Riding_habit>, often of imported material, as did
Lowland and English women.^[166] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2016-189>
^[256] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_39-40-291> Highland women's dress was
also sometimes simply in linear stripes rather than tartan, a cloth
called /iomairt/ (drugget <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Drugget>).^[166] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2016-189> From the late 18th
century, as the arisaid was increasingly set aside for contemporary
womenswear, while Highland men continued wearing the belted
plaid.,^[257] <#cite_note-292> the ladies' plaids were reduced to
smaller "screens" – fringed shawls used as headdresses and as dress
accessories,^[256] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_39-40-291> "a gentrification
of the arisaid".^[166] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2016-189> (Wilsons
continued producing these in the first half of the 19th century.)^[166]
<#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2016-189> John Macky <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Macky> in /A Journey Through Scotland/ (1723)
wrote of Scottish women wearing, when about, such tartan plaids over
their heads and bodies, over English-style dress, and likened the
practice to continental women wearing black wraps for church, market,
and other functions.^[236] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_97-268> Edmund
Burt <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edmund_Burt>, an Englishman who
spent years in and around Inverness, wrote in 1727–1737 (published 1754)
that the women there also wore such plaids, made of fine worsted
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Worsted> wool or even of silk <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silk>, that they were sometimes used to cover the
head, and that they were worn long, to the ankle, on one side. He added
that in Edinburgh (far to the southeast) they were also worn, with
ladies indicating their Whig <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Whigs_(British_political_party)> or Tory <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Tories_(British_political_party)> political stance by which side they
wore long (though he did not remember which side was which).^[258]
<#cite_note-293> In Edinburgh, perennial disapproval of the "barbarous
habitte" of women wearing plaids over their heads returned in 1753
writings of William Maitland <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
William_Maitland_(historian)>. Women first appear in known painted
portraits with tartan c. 1700, with that of Rachel Gordon of Abergeldie
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abergeldie_Castle>; more early examples
are found in 1742 and 1749 paintings by William Mosman,. They show
plaids (in tartans that do not survive as modern patterns) worn loosely
around the shoulders by sitters in typical European-fashion
dresses.^[259] <#cite_note-294> Some entire dresses of tartan feature in
mid-18th-century portraits, but they are uncommon.^[166] <#cite_note-
EsleaMacDonald2016-189> In the Jacobite period, tartan was sometimes
also used as trim, e.g. on hats. Plaids were worn also as part of
wedding outfits. The monied sometimes had entire wedding dresses of
tartan, some in silk, and even devised custom tartans for weddings,
typically based on existing patterns with colours changed.^[256]
<#cite_note-Scarlett1990_39-40-291>

Woman with baby, in tartan dress, man in tartan kilt and red coat, blue
bonnet <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Munro_highland_soldier_and_wife.png>Highland soldier and family,
the woman in an arisaid <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arisaid>; by
Martin Engelbrecht <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martin_Engelbrecht> c.
1717–1754^[aj] <#cite_note-296>

Portraits became more popular among the Highland elite starting in the
early 18th century.^[261] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_22-297> Similar cloth
to that in the c. 1683 Mungo Murray portrait appears in the 1708
portrait <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Charles_Jervas_(c.
1675-1739)_-
_John_Campbell_(1696%E2%80%931782),_3rd_Earl_of_Breadalbane,_as_a_Child_in_Highland_Costume_-_PG_2934_-_National_Galleries_of_Scotland.jpg> of the young John Campbell of Glenorchy <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Campbell,_3rd_Earl_of_Breadalbane_and_Holland>, attributed to Charles Jervas <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Jervas>; and the c. 1712 portrait <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Kenneth-sutherland-3rd-lord-duffus-d-1734-jacobite.jpg> of Kenneth Sutherland, Lord Duffus <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kenneth_Sutherland,_3rd_Lord_Duffus>, by Richard Waitt <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Waitt>.^[262] <#cite_note-298> This style of very "busy" but brown-dominated tartan seems to have been fairly common through the early 18th century, and is quite different from later patterns.^[263] <#cite_note-299> As the century wore on, bolder setts came to dominate, judging from later portraits and surviving cloth and clothing samples. By the early 18th century, tartan manufacture (and weaving in general) were centred in Bannockburn <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bannockburn>, Stirling; this is where the eventually dominant tartan weaver William Wilson and Son, founded c. 1765, were based.^[264] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_144-300> ^[ak] <#cite_note-302>

Judging from rare surviving samples, the predominant civilian tartan
colours of this period, in addition to white (undyed wool) and black,
were rich reds and greens and rather dark blues, not consistent from
area to area; where a good black was available, dark blue was less
used.^[7] <#cite_note-Scarlett2008-7> The sett of a typical Highland
pattern of the era as shown in portraits was red with broad bands of
green and/or blue, sometimes with fine-line over-checks.^[7]
<#cite_note-Scarlett2008-7> ^[al] <#cite_note-303> Oil portraiture
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oil_painting> was the province of the
privileged, and "Sunday best <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Christian_clothing>" tartans with red grounds were commonly worn in them
as a status symbol <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Status_symbol>, from
the early 18th century, the dye typically being made from expensive
imported cochineal <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cochineal>.^[166]
<#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2016-189> ^[266] <#cite_note-304> Green and
blue more generally predominated owing to their relative ease of
production with locally available dyes, with more difficult yellow^[am]
<#cite_note-306> and red dyes commonly being saved for thin over-check
lines^[268] <#cite_note-307> (a practice that continued, e.g. in
military and consequently many clan tartans, through to the 19th
century – see Regimental tartans <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Regimental_tartans>). However, even local-dyestuff blues were often
over-dyed with some amount of imported indigo <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Indigo> for a richer colour.^[50] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_227-60>


        Union protest and Jacobite rebellion[edit <https://
        en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tartan&action=edit&section=12>]

The Treaty <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Treaty_of_Union> and Acts of
Union <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707> in 1706–07,
which did away with the separate Parliament of Scotland <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parliament_of_Scotland>, led to Scottish
Lowlanders <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_Lowlands> adopting
tartan in large numbers for the first time, as a symbol of protest
against the union.^[269] <#cite_note-Innes_of_Learney_10-12-308> ^[270]
<#cite_note-BdLC_75-309> It was worn not just by men (regardless of
social class),^[271] <#cite_note-310> but even influential Edinburgh
ladies,^[269] <#cite_note-Innes_of_Learney_10-12-308> ^[272]
<#cite_note-311> well into the 1790s.^[273] <#cite_note-
Armstrong_17-312> By the beginning of the 18th century, there was also
some demand for tartan in England, to be used for curtains, bedding,
nightgowns, etc., and weavers in Norwich <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Norwich>, Norfolk, and some other English cities were attempting to
duplicate Scottish product, but were considered the lower-quality
option.^[250] <#cite_note-StewartDW_29-284>

Painting of the prince in a red-and-black tartan coat with crossed
bandoliers, and an elaborate blue bonnet with white cockade <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:William_Mosman_-
_Prince_Charles_Edward_Stuart,_1720_-
_1788._Eldest_son_of_Prince_James_Francis_Edward_Stuart_-
_Google_Art_Project.jpg>Charles Edward Stuart <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Charles_Edward_Stuart>, "Bonnie Prince Charlie", in tartan and blue
bonnet with Jacobite white cockade; portrait by William Mosman <https://
en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=William_Mosman&action=edit&redlink=1>
c. 1750

The most effective fighters for Jacobitism <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Jacobitism> were the supporting Scottish clans, leading to an
association of tartan and Highland dress <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Highland_dress> with the Jacobite cause to restore the Catholic Stuart
dynasty <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/House_of_Stuart> to the throne of
England, Scotland, and Ireland. This included great kilts <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_kilt>, and trews <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Trews> (trousers) with great coats, all typically of tartan cloth,
as well as the blue bonnet <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blue_bonnet>.
The British parliament had considered banning the belted plaid after the
Jacobite rising of 1715 <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Jacobite_rising_of_1715>, but did not.^[274] <#cite_note-313> Highland
garb came to form something of a Jacobite uniform,^[273] <#cite_note-
Armstrong_17-312> ^[275] <#cite_note-314> even worn by Prince Charles
Edward Stuart <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Edward_Stuart>
("Bonnie Prince Charlie") himself by the mid-18th century,^[276]
<#cite_note-315> ^[an] <#cite_note-321> mostly in propaganda portraits
(with inconsistent tartans) but also by eyewitness account at Culloden
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_Culloden>.^[282]
<#cite_note-322> By this period, sometimes a belted plaid was worn over
tartan trews and jacket (in patterns that need not match).^[283]
<#cite_note-323>

An olive-dominated tartan with a fair amount of navy blue and black, and
over-checks of red (with sky-blue guard lines), yellow (guarded by
black), and white <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Culloden_1745_tartan,_centred,_zoomed_out.png>A pattern from a coat
(probably Jacobite) known to date to the period of the 1745 uprising

Burt had concurred c. 1728, as did his 1818 editor Robert Jamieson
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Jamieson_(antiquary)>, with
Buchanan's much earlier 1582 observation that tartans were often in
colours intended to blend into heather and other natural
surroundings.^[284] <#cite_note-324> This may just represent prejudices
of English writers of the period, however, at least by the mid-18th
century. Extant samples of Culloden-era cloth are sometimes quite
colourful. One example is a pattern found on a coat <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Culloden_1746_tartan,_tiling.png> (probably
Jacobite) known to date to around the 1745 uprising; while it has faded
to olive and navy tones, the sett is a bold one of green, blue, black,
red, yellow, white, and light blue (in diminishing proportions). While
an approximation of the pattern was first published in D. W. Stewart
(1893), the colours and proportions were wrong; the original coat was
rediscovered and re-examined in 2007.^[285] <#cite_note-325> ^[286]
<#cite_note-326> Another surviving Culloden sample, predominantly red
with broad bands of blue, green, and black, and some thin over-check
lines, consists of a largely intact entire plaid that belonged on one
John Moir; it was donated to the National Museum of Scotland in
2019.^[287] <#cite_note-327>

There is a legend that a particular still-extant tartan was used by the
Jacobites as an identifier even prior to "the '15 <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jacobite_rising_of_1715>". This story can be
traced to W. & A. Smith (1850) in /Authenticated Tartans of the Clans
and Families of Scotland/, in which they claimed that a pattern they
published was received from an unnamed woman then still living who in
turn claimed a family tradition that the tartan dated to 1712, long
before her birth, but for which there is no evidence.^[33] <#cite_note-
EsleaMacDonald2022-37> This hearsay tale was later repeated as if known
fact by other books, e.g., Adam Frank's /What Is My Tartan?/ in
1896,^[288] <#cite_note-328> and Margaret MacDougall's 1974 revision of
Robert Bain's 1938 /Clans and Tartans of Scotland/.^[ao]
<#cite_note-331> Even the often credulous Innes of Learney (1938) did
not believe it.^[291] <#cite_note-332> The pattern in question does date
to at least c. 1815–26, because it was collected by the Highland Society
of London <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highland_Society_of_London>
during that span.^[33] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2022-37> But there is
no substantiated evidence of Jacobites using a consistent tartan, much
less one surviving to the present.

Independent Highland Companies were re-raised from clans loyal to the
English crown during 1725–29.^[292] <#cite_note-Cowan_2021-333> ^[ap]
<#cite_note-334> ^[293] <#cite_note-Groves_2-335> This time they wore
uniform tartans of blue, black, and green, presumably with differencing
over-check lines.^[294] <#cite_note-Campbell_of_Airds-336> ^[293]
<#cite_note-Groves_2-335> ^[214] <#cite_note-Newsome_Black_Watch-243>
They were all normalised to one tartan during 1725–33^[214] <#cite_note-
Newsome_Black_Watch-243> ^[216] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_26-27-245>
^[215] <#cite_note-Anderson_1939-244> ^[295] <#cite_note-337> (a pattern
which probably does not survive to the present day).^[165] <#cite_note-
SCSC_history-188> The uniform tartan appears to have changed into a new
tartan, known today as Black Watch or Government, when the companies
amalgamated to become the 42nd (Black Watch) <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/42nd_Regiment_of_Foot> regiment in 1739. (See Regimental tartan
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regimental_tartan>.)


        Proscription and its aftermath[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/
        index.php?title=Tartan&action=edit&section=13>]

After the failure of the Jacobite rising of 1745 <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jacobite_rising_of_1745>, efforts to pacify the
Highlands and destroy the cultural and political life of the clans^[296]
<#cite_note-338> ^[297] <#cite_note-339> led to the Dress Act 1746
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dress_Act_1746>, part of the Act of
Proscription <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Act_of_Proscription_1746> to
disarm the Highlanders. Because tartan Highland dress was so strongly
symbolically linked to the militant Jacobite cause,^[298] <#cite_note-
Hinderks_5-7-340> the act – a highly political throwback to the long-
abandoned sumptuary laws <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Sumptuary_law>^[298] <#cite_note-Hinderks_5-7-340>  – banned the wearing
of Highland dress by men and boys in Scotland north of the River Forth
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/River_Forth> (i.e. in the
Highlands),^[aq] <#cite_note-342> except for the landed gentry^[ar]
<#cite_note-343> and the Highland regiments of the British Army.^[300]
<#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_1-344> The law was based on previous English
bans in the 16th century against traditional garb in Ireland.^[301]
<#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_3-345> Sir Walter Scott <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Walter_Scott> wrote of the Dress Act: "There was
knowledge of mankind in the prohibition, since it divested the
Highlanders of a dress which was closely in association with their
habits of Clanship and of war."^[302] <#cite_note-BdLC_84-346>

Tartans recorded shortly after the act (thus probably being patterns in
use in the period before proscription) show that a general pattern was
used in a wide area, with minor changes being made by individual weavers
to taste.^[223] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_12-254> E.g., the tartan today
used as the main (red) Mackintosh <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Clan_Mackintosh> clan tartan,^[303] <#cite_note-347> recorded by the
Highland Society of London <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Highland_Society_of_London> around 1815, was found in variants from
Perthshire <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perthshire> and Badenoch
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Badenoch> along the Great Glen <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Glen> to Loch Moy <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Loch_Moy>.^[223] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_12-254> Other such groups
can be found, e.g. a Huntly <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Huntly>-
centred Murray <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Murray>/Sutherland
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Sutherland>/Gordon <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Gordon> cluster analysed as clearly related
by Innes of Learney (1938)^[153] <#cite_note-Innes_of_Learney_10-173>  –
distinguished from a different Huntly/MacRae <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Clan_Macrae>/Ross <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Ross>/Grant
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Grant> group identified by Scottish
Register of Tartans <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scottish_Register_of_Tartans> and tartan researcher Peter Eslea
MacDonald of Scottish Tartans Authority <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scottish_Tartans_Authority>.^[304] <#cite_note-SRT_Huntly-348> ^[305]
<#cite_note-349> But Scarlett (1990) says that "the old patterns
available are too few in number to permit a detailed study of such
pattern distributions" throughout the Highlands.^[223] <#cite_note-
Scarlett1990_12-254> Portraits of the era also show that tartan was
increasingly made with identical or near-identical warp and weft
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warp_and_weft> patterns, which had not
always been the case earlier, and that the tartan cloth used was of the
fine twill, with even-warp-and-weft thickness, still used today for
kilts.^[243] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_13-276> ^[261] <#cite_note-
Scarlett1990_22-297>

Although the Dress Act, contrary to popular later belief, did not ban
all tartan^[306] <#cite_note-Tuckett_6-350> (or bagpipes, or Gaelic),
and women, noblemen, and soldiers continued to wear tartan,^[307]
<#cite_note-BdLC_84-85-351> it nevertheless effectively severed the
everyday tradition of Highland Scots wearing primarily tartan, as it
imposed the wearing of Lowland, English-like clothing for two
generations.^[300] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_1-344> ^[308] <#cite_note-
NatGeo-352> (While some Scotsmen defied the act,^[309] <#cite_note-353>
^[310] <#cite_note-354> there were stiff criminal penalties.)^[311]
<#cite_note-355> It had a demoralising effect,^[as] <#cite_note-357> and
the goal of this and related suppressive measures to integrate the Scots
into Lowland and broader British society^[301] <#cite_note-
TelferDunbar1979_3-345> was largely successful.^[298] <#cite_note-
Hinderks_5-7-340> ^[313] <#cite_note-358> By the 1770s, Highland dress
seemed all but extinct.^[314] <#cite_note-359> However, the act may also
ironically have helped to "galvanize clan consciousness" under that
suppression;^[315] <#cite_note-360> Scottish clans, in romanticised
form, were to come roaring back in the "clan tartans" run of the Regency
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regency_era> (late Georgian <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georgian_era>) to Victorian <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Victorian_era> period.

Portrait of young woman in a blue and white dress with predominantly red
tartan plaid around her shoulders, and several white roses, a Jacobite
symbol. <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Floramacdonald2.jpg>Jacobite
women continued wearing tartan during the proscription (1749 portrait of
Flora MacDonald <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flora_MacDonald> by Allan
Ramsay <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allan_Ramsay_(artist)> and Joseph
van Aken <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph_van_Aken>; the tartan is
a Tullibardine <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tullibardine> area
pattern, later the Murray <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Murray> of
Tullibardine clan tartan).^[316] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2010-11-a-361>

In the interim, Jacobite women continued using tartan profusely, for
clothing (from dresses to shoes), curtains, and everyday items.^[317]
<#cite_note-362> ^[307] <#cite_note-BdLC_84-85-351> While Classicism
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classicism>-infused portraiture of 18th-
century clan nobles (often painted outside Scotland) typically showed
them in tartan and "Highland" dress, much of it was loyalist regimental
military stylings, the antithesis of Jacobite messaging;^[318]
<#cite_note-363> it foreshadowed a major shift in the politics of tartan
(see § Late Georgian <#Late_Georgian>, below). Nevertheless, this
profuse application of tartan could be seen as rebellious to some
extent, with the reified <https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/reification>
Highlander becoming "a heroic and classical figure, the legatee
<https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/legatee> of primitive virtues."^[319]
<#cite_note-364> And by the 1760s, tartan had become increasingly
associated with Scotland in general, not just the Highlands, especially
in the English mind.^[320] <#cite_note-365>

Girl around age 8, in red-and-black tartan bodice and skirt, with basket
and white rose <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Helen_Murray_of_Ochtertyre_c._1750.jpg>Helen Murray of Ochtertyre,
daughter and eldest child of Sir Patrick Murray of Ochtertyre, 4th Bt;
c. 1750, artist uncertain. The tartans of the bodice and skirt do not
match exactly, and are not surviving patterns.^[321] <#cite_note-366>

After much outcry (as the ban applied to Jacobites and loyalists alike),
the Dress Act was repealed in 1782, primarily through efforts of the
Highland Society of London;^[322] <#cite_note-367> the repeal bill was
introduced by James Graham, Marquis of Graham <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/James_Graham,_3rd_Duke_of_Montrose> (later Duke of Montrose).^[323]
<#cite_note-BdLC_87-368> Some Highlanders resumed their traditional
dress,^[324] <#cite_note-369> but overall it had been abandoned by its
former peasant wearers, taken up instead by the upper and middle
classes, as a fashion.^[325] <#cite_note-370> Tartan had been
"culturally relocated as a picturesque ensemble or as the clothing of a
hardy and effective fighting force" for the crown, not a symbol of
direct rebellion.^[326] <#cite_note-371> R. Martin (1988) calls this
transmutation "the great bifurcation <https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/
bifurcation> in tartan dress",^[327] <#cite_note-MartinR_53-372> the
cloth being largely (forcibly) abandoned by the original Highland
provincials then taken up by the military and consequently by non-
Highlander civilians. During the prohibition, traditional Highland
techniques of wool spinning and dyeing, and the weaving of tartan, had
sharply declined.^[96] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_222-224-113> ^[299]
<#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2012_3-341> ^[105] <#cite_note-
EsleaMacDonald_colour-122> Commercial production of tartan was to become
re-centred in the Lowlands, in factory villages along the fringe of the
Highlands,^[328] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_14-373> among companies like
Wilsons of Bannockburn <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bannockburn> (then
the dominant manufacturer),^[329] <#cite_note-Tuckett_19-374> with the
rise of demand for tartan for military regimental dress <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regimental_tartan>.^[330] <#cite_note-375> Some
tartan weaving continued in the Highlands,^[331] <#cite_note-
Hinderks_Dziennik_127-129-376> ^[332] <#cite_note-377> and would even
see a boost in the late Georgian period.^[331] <#cite_note-
Hinderks_Dziennik_127-129-376> Tartan by this era had also become
popular in Lowland areas including Fife <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Fife> and Lothian <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lothian> and the urban
centres of Edinburgh <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edinburgh> and
Stirling <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stirling>.^[306] <#cite_note-
Tuckett_6-350> From 1797 to 1830,^[264] <#cite_note-
TelferDunbar1979_144-300> Wilsons were exporting large quantities of
tartan (for both men's and women's clothing), first to the British
colonies in Grenada <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grenada> and Jamaica
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jamaica> (where the affordable, durable,
and bright material was popular for clothing enslaved people <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Slavery_in_the_British_and_French_Caribbean>),^[328] <#cite_note-
Scarlett1990_14-373> and had clients in England, Northern and Central
Europe, and a bit later in North and South America and the
Mediterranean.^[333] <#cite_note-378> ^[334] <#cite_note-379> However,
by the end of the 18th century, Wilsons had "stiff competition" (in
civilian tartan) from English weavers in Norwich <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Norwich>.^[335] <#cite_note-380>

Because the Dress Act had not applied to the military or gentry, tartan
gradually had become associated with the affluent, rather than "noble
savage <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Noble_savage>" Highlanders,^[336]
<#cite_note-381> ^[337] <#cite_note-Trevor-Roper_25-382> ^[338]
<#cite_note-Scarlett1990_35-383> from the late 18th century and into the
19th,^[339] <#cite_note-384> along with patriotic military-influenced
clothing styles in general;^[340] <#cite_note-Tuckett_10-11-385> tartan
and militarised Highland dress were being revived among the fashion-
conscious across Britain, even among women with military
relatives.^[341] <#cite_note-386> The clans, Jacobitism, and anti-
unionism (none of them any longer an actual threat of civil unrest) were
increasingly viewed with a sense of nostalgia,^[173] <#cite_note-
Spinning-197> ^[342] <#cite_note-387> ^[343] <#cite_note-388> ^[338]
<#cite_note-Scarlett1990_35-383> especially after the death of Prince
Charles Edward Stuart in 1788,^[344] <#cite_note-
EsleaMacDonald_2020-10-389> even as Highland regiments proved their
loyalty and worth.^[338] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_35-383> Adopting the
airs of a Tory <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Tories_(British_political_party)> sort of tartaned "Highlandism
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highlandism>"^[345] <#cite_note-390>
provided a post-union and resigned sense of national (and militarily
elite) distinction from the rest of Britain, without threatening empire
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Empire>.^[346] <#cite_note-391>
Even the future George IV <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_IV>
donned Highland regalia for a masquerade ball <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Masquerade_ball> in 1789.^[347] <#cite_note-392> By the 1790s, some
of the gentry were helping design tartans for their own personal use,
according to surviving records from Wilsons.^[173] <#cite_note-
Spinning-197> Jane (Maxwell) Gordon, Duchess of Gordon <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jane_Gordon,_Duchess_of_Gordon>, was said to have
"introduced tartan to [royal] court <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Royal_court> ... wearing a plaid of the Black Watch, to which her son
had just been appointed", in 1792; she triggered a fashion of wearing
tartan in London and Paris, though was not immune to caricature by the
disapproving.^[348] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_102-393>

R. Martin (1988) wrote, from a historiographical <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Historiography> perspective, that after the Dress
Act:^[327] <#cite_note-MartinR_53-372>

    the idea of Highland dress was stored in the collective historical
    attic; when it was revived in the years leading up to 1822, it had
    been forgotten by some two or three generations in civilian dress
    and could be remembered, however deceptively, however naively, to
    have been the ancient dress of the Highlands, not that so recently
    worn as the standard peasant dress before 1746. The ban on tartan
    was hugely successful, but so inimical to a natural historical
    process, that it promoted the violent re-assertion of the tartan,
    sanctioned by a spurious sense of history, in the next century.

The tumultuous events of 18th-century Scotland led to not just broader
public use of tartan cloth, but two particular enduring tartan
categories: regimental tartans and eventually clan tartans.


    Regimental tartans[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
    title=Tartan&action=edit&section=14>]

Main article: Regimental tartan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Regimental_tartan>
Coloured line-drawing of two early Highland regiment soldiers in green
tartan great kilts, red-and-white diced hose, and blue bonnets, one with
a musket <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Highland_soldier_1744.jpg>Soldiers from a Highland regiment c. 1744
wearing tartan belted plaids <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Belted_plaid> (great kilts).

After the period of the early clan militias and the Independent Highland
Companies <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Independent_Highland_Companies>
(IHCs), over 100 battalions of line, fencible, militia, and volunteer
regiments were raised, between c. 1739 and the end of the Napoleonic
Wars in 1815, in or predominantly in the Highlands,^[349]
<#cite_note-394> a substantial proportion of them in Highland dress. Of
these units, only some had distinct uniform tartans, and of those, only
a small number were recorded to the present day.

Busy scene of 8 men in various forms of Highland regimental uniform,
with 3 children and dog also in-scene. The central figure is doing a
Highland sword dance, near a bagpiper, while the other figures look on.
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:The_Sword_Dance_by_David_Cunliffe,_1853.jpg>/The Sword Dance
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_sword_dances>/ by David
Cunliffe, 1853, depicting men of the 42nd and 93rd. The dancer in the
centre wears the 42nd's red band tartan.

The IHCs were amalgamated in 1739 to become the 43rd (later 42nd)
Regiment of Foot <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
42nd_Regiment_of_Foot>,^[350] <#cite_note-395> called the Black
Watch.^[351] <#cite_note-396> It was the first proper governmental
Highland regiment <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highland_regiment>,
part of the British Army <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Army>,
and they wore the belted plaid <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Belted_plaid> ("great kilt") for dress, and the tailored small kilt
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Small_kilt> for undress uniform.^[352]
<#cite_note-397> ^[293] <#cite_note-Groves_2-335> ^[353]
<#cite_note-398> For the former garment,^[354] <#cite_note-
TelferDunbar1979_178-399> they used a distinctive tartan, which was
designed for the unit.^[355] <#cite_note-Groves_3-400> It was originally
called the "42nd tartan",^[355] <#cite_note-Groves_3-400> so it probably
was not adopted until after the unit was renumbered the 42nd in
1749.^[214] <#cite_note-Newsome_Black_Watch-243> It seems likely that
the tartan was based on those used by the IHCs earlier, but with double
black "tram line" over-checks added.^[214] <#cite_note-
Newsome_Black_Watch-243> ^[356] <#cite_note-401> The Black Watch pattern
was used by various other regiments, and it has been estimated that to
clothe them all, some 30–40 miles (48–64 km) of the tartan had to be
woven before 1750 alone.^[60] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2020-04-72> It
became the basis of various later regimental (and eventually clan)
tartans.^[214] <#cite_note-Newsome_Black_Watch-243> It remains popular
in general-public use under the names "Black Watch", "Government", and
any of "old Campbell <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Campbell>",
"hunting Grant <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Grant>", or "hunting
Munro <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Munro>",^[214] <#cite_note-
Newsome_Black_Watch-243> but today officially called "Government No. 1"
by the military. (See illustration in § Popular designs
<#Popular_designs>, below.) The 42nd had separate tratans for its small
kilt until c. 1814^[354] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_178-399> ^[357]
<#cite_note-402> (also used for grenadiers <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Grenadier#Elite_status_in_the_18th_century>' belted plaids),^[354]
<#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_178-399> for pipers <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pipe_band#Military_pipes_and_drums>,^[358]
<#cite_note-403> ^[359] <#cite_note-404> and for drummers.^[360]
<#cite_note-405>

After the Jacobite uprisings <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Jacobite_uprisings>, raising a regiment in service to the king was, for
many Scottish lairds <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laird>, a way of
rehabilitating the family name, assuring new-found loyalty to the
British (now Hanoverian <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
House_of_Hanover>) crown, and currying royal favour (even regaining
forfeited estates).^[361] <#cite_note-406> Exempt from the Dress Act
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dress_Act>, men in these Highland
regiments of the empire <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Empire>
were given Highland dress as a sort of safe, subsumed Scottish
nationalism.^[362] <#cite_note-407> From c. 1770 onward into the 19th
century, virtually all the regimental tartan was produced by the company
William Wilson & Son of Bannockburn <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Bannockburn>, the dominant tartan weaver.^[329] <#cite_note-
Tuckett_19-374> Regimental uniforms, including tartans, were left –
usually within the general Black Watch-based colour scheme of black,
blue, and green – to their commanders.^[363] <#cite_note-408> ^[364]
<#cite_note-Hinderks_Dziennik_8-409>

Seven solidiers in action, in red tartan trews, with red coats and black
feather bonnets, and one with a tartan shoulder plaid; most have rifles
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:
72nd_Highlanders,_uniform,_1844.jpg>72nd Duke of Albany's Own
Highlanders <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
72nd_Regiment,_Duke_of_Albany%27s_Own_Highlanders> during a trews-
wearing period, c. 1844, in the tartan named for Prince Charles Edward
Stuart

Some surviving early regimental tartans include:

  * Loudoun's Highlanders <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Loudoun%27s_Highlanders> (64th, raised in 1745), used a tartan
    similar to Black Watch, but with over-checks of red and yellow, and
    lacking the two black "tram lines" of Black Watch.^[365]
    <#cite_note-410> ^[366] <#cite_note-411>
  * The 78th (Highlanders) <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    78th_(Highlanders)_Regiment_of_Foot> or Ross-shire Buffs (raised
    1793), MacLeod's Highlanders <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    71st_(Highland)_Regiment_of_Foot> (73rd, later 71st, raised 1777–
    78), and the original Seaforth Highland Regiment <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    72nd_Regiment,_Duke_of_Albany%27s_Own_Highlanders> (78th, later
    72nd, raised 1778)^[at] <#cite_note-412> first used Black Watch,
    then in 1787 adopted a variant of it with thin over-checks of red
    and white.^[367] <#cite_note-413> ^[368] <#cite_note-
    Barnes_&_Allen_84-86-414> ^[369] <#cite_note-415> It eventually
    became the Clan Mackenzie <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Clan_Mackenzie> tartan,^[368] <#cite_note-Barnes_&_Allen_84-86-414>
    and it remains used as an official British military tartan,
    designated "Government No. 5A". A slight variation, with yellow in
    place of white, became one of the Clan MacLeod <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_MacLeod> tartans.^[370] <#cite_note-416>
  * The 74th (Highland) Regiment of Foot <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    74th_(Highland)_Regiment_of_Foot> (raised 1787)^[au]
    <#cite_note-417> used another variant of the Black Watch tartan with
    a black-guarded white over-check.^[371] <#cite_note-418> Also in
    1787, the 75th (Highland) Regiment <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    75th_(Stirlingshire)_Regiment_of_Foot>, later 75th (Stirlingshire),
    probably used a more distinct tartan, not based on Black Watch, of
    purple and black on a green ground, with thin white and black over-
    checks; it was later called "No. 64 or Abercromby" by Wilsons, and
    though it did not become adopted as an Abercromby/Abercrombie clan
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Abercromby> tartan, variants of
    it became two unrelated clan patterns.^[372] <#cite_note-419>
  * The Gordon Highlanders <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    92nd_(Gordon_Highlanders)_Regiment_of_Foot> (100th, later 92nd,
    raised 1794) also wore an altered Black Watch <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:
    92nd_(Gordon_Highlanders)_Regiment,_tileable.png>, this time with a
    thin yellow over-check.^[373] <#cite_note-420> ^[374]
    <#cite_note-421> In a rare show of competition to Wilsons, the
    pattern was designed in 1793 and supplied by weaver William Forsyth
    of Huntly <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Huntly>, Aberdeen.^[375]
    <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_159-160-422> This pattern became the
    main tartan of Clan Gordon <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Clan_Gordon>.^[376] <#cite_note-423> ^[377] <#cite_note-424>
    Something nearly identical (perhaps with the yellow over-check in a
    different width) was also used by the 8th (Rothesay and Caithness)
    Fencibles <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    8th_(Rothesay_and_Caithness)_Fencibles>.^[378] <#cite_note-425>
  * The Cameronian Volunteers <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Queen%27s_Own_Cameron_Highlanders> (79th, later Queen's Own Cameron
    Highlanders, raised 1793) used a comparatively distinct tartan
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:
    79th_Regiment_(Cameronian_Volunteers,_Queen%27s_Own_Cameron_Highlanders)_and_Cameron_of_Erracht_tartan,_tileable.png>, later the family tartan of the Cameron of Erracht <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cameron_of_Erracht> branch of Clan Cameron <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Cameron>.^[57] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2012-01-68> ^[379] <#cite_note-426> ^[380] <#cite_note-427> It is structurally much like Black Watch, but without black over-checks and with a number of yellow and red over-checks. It has been said to have been designed by the unit leaderor a family member.^[57] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2012-01-68> ^[381] <#cite_note-428>
  * The Fraser Fencibles <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Highland_Fencible_Corps#Fraser_Fencibles_(1794)> (raised 1794–95)
    used a tartan with a red ground and green and blue bands, unrelated
    to the Black Watch style.^[382] <#cite_note-429> ^[383]
    <#cite_note-430>
  * The Sutherland Highlanders (93rd) <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    93rd_(Sutherland_Highlanders)_Regiment_of_Foot> raised 1799, and
    later the Argyll and Sutherland Highlanders <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argyll_and_Sutherland_Highlanders> (Princess
    Louise's, formed 1881 by amalgamation of the 93rd with the 91st
    Argyllshire Highlanders <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    91st_(Argyllshire_Highlanders)_Regiment_of_Foot>), may have worn a
    lightened version of Black Watch,^[384] <#cite_note-431> ^[385]
    <#cite_note-432> now sometimes used as one of the Clan Sutherland
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Sutherland> tartans;^[386]
    <#cite_note-433> ^[387] <#cite_note-434> it is also still militarily
    used as sett "Government No. 1A".
  * The Loyal Clan Donnachie Volunteers (raised in 1803) had its own
    uniform tartan, which was later adopted as the hunting Robertson/
    Donnachie/Duncan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Robertson> clan
    tartan.^[388] <#cite_note-Newsome_2005-435> ^[389] <#cite_note-436>
  * The Duke of Albany's Own Highlanders <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    72nd_Regiment,_Duke_of_Albany%27s_Own_Highlanders> (formerly
    Seaforth's 72nd), during a trews <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Trews>-wearing period of 1823–1881, wore a tartan called Prince
    Charles Edward Stuart <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:Prince_Charles_Edward_Stuart_(regimental)_tartan,_tileable.png>, similar to royal Stewart <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Stewart_tartan>,^[112] <#cite_note-SRT_PCES-129> as shown in a period painting <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:72nd_Highlanders,_uniform,_1844.jpg>. Identified in surviving cloth samples from the mid-18th century^[64] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2016-01-76> (before the regiment) it is one of the oldest setts in continuous production.^[64] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2016-01-76> 

For more detail, and an image gallery of these setts, see Regimental
tartan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regimental_tartan>.

Two Highland-regiment pipe majors in kilts, Glengarry bonnets, and
undress army blouses with insignia, as well as leather shoes and hose
with flashes; a woman in a white skirt is examining the pleats of one of
the kilts. <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:The_British_Army_in_Italy_1944_NA16084.jpg>An Italian woman
inspects the kilts of two pipe majors in Rome, 1944, toward the end of
kilts as undress uniform in Highland regiments

By the turn of the 18th and 19th centuries, women in Scotland were
especially "desirous to dress in the uniform plaids of their husbands",
in particularly fine-quality cloth, according to records of Wilsons of
Bannockburn.^[348] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_102-393> After the
Highland regiments proved themselves fearless and effective in various
military campaigns, the glory associated with them did much to keep
alive, initially among the gentry and later the general public, an
interest in tartan and kilts, which might have otherwise slipped into
obscurity due to the Dress Act's prohibition.^[390] <#cite_note-437> The
belted plaid was abandoned in favour of the small kilt, around
1814.^[337] <#cite_note-Trevor-Roper_25-382> ^[354] <#cite_note-
TelferDunbar1979_178-399> After the "clan tartanry" rush of the early to
mid-19th century (see below), various later Highland regiments adopted
some of the recently minted clan tartans for their uniforms (reversing
the original regimental-into-clan-tartan flow). Some of these adoptions
remain in regimental use today.

The Lowland regiments <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lowland_regiments>
(dating in some form to 1633 and never before dressed in Highland garb
but in a variant of regular army uniform) were outfitted in tartan trews
in 1881. This both linked them with and distinguished them from the
tartan-kilted Highland regiments.^[391] <#cite_note-438> Typically the
"Government" (Black Watch) tartan was used, though some units later
diversified. Several Highland regiments were again assigned new tartans
that were clan tartans rather than unit-specific ones, into the early
20th century.^[292] <#cite_note-Cowan_2021-333>

Today, about a dozen tartans are officially used (and half a dozen more
unofficially) between all of the surviving historical Scottish regiments
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_regiment>, which have largely
been amalgamated since 2006 as battalions into the Royal Regiment of
Scotland <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Regiment_of_Scotland>,
part of the Scottish, Welsh and Irish Division <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish,_Welsh_and_Irish_Division>.^[392]
<#cite_note-439> These tartans are only worn in dress and pipe-band
uniforms, after the practical uniform changes introduced in the early
part of World War II, which did away with tartan kilts and trews in
undress uniforms. (For further information on these tartans and the
modern units using them, see List of tartans § UK military or government
tartans <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
List_of_tartans#UK_military_or_government_tartans>.) Some military units
in other countries also have their own tartans. In all, there are at
least 38 documented tartans that have at one time or another been
associated with regiments, though many of them also with clans.^[av]
<#cite_note-441>


    Clan tartans[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
    title=Tartan&action=edit&section=15>]

Further information: List of tartans § Scottish clan tartans <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_tartans#Scottish_clan_tartans>

With an exception dating to 1618^[394] <#cite_note-
Innes_of_Learney_9-10-442> and another to c. 1703–1715^[248]
<#cite_note-Mackay_50-282> (neither of which appear to have survived),
it is generally regarded that tartans associated by name with Scottish
clans <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_clan> mostly date to the
early-to-mid 19th century,^[1] <#cite_note-Newsome_1994-1> ^[395]
<#cite_note-PatersonMB_155-443> ^[396] <#cite_note-Ward_Edwards-444>
^[397] <#cite_note-445> ^[132] <#cite_note-Thompson1992_iv-150> ^[398]
<#cite_note-HaswellMiller1956-446> ^[399] <#cite_note-
EsleaMacDonald2012_2-447> some few to the late 18th at the
earliest,^[94] <#cite_note-Barnes_&_Allen_266-110> ^[400] <#cite_note-
MackayScobie-448> ^[338] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_35-383> depending on
how one defines "clan tartan". The belief that the clan tartans are an
"ancient" system of symbolic family differentiation is pervasive, even
passionate, but lacks substantive evidence even as it is overwhelmed by
counter-evidence. It is what J. C. Thompson (1992) called "the Great
Tartan Myth",^[132] <#cite_note-Thompson1992_iv-150> and James D.
Scarlett (1990) "the Tartan Cult".^[245] <#cite_note-
Scarlett1990_34-278> Lt.-Col. M. M. Haldane (1931) called it an
assumption, which "has acquired such a formidable weight from mere
reiteration" without "critical examination of evidence".^[401]
<#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_15-16_Haldane-449> Barnes & Allen (1956)
observed:^[94] <#cite_note-Barnes_&_Allen_266-110>

    There is no doubt that many 'setts' had been traditional to certain
    districts for centuries, but the theory that they were a sort of
    Clan uniform seems now to have been quite discredited.

Responding to the claim that clan tartans have "an ancient political
significance", Richard Martin <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Richard_Martin_(curator)>, curator of the Fashion Institute of
Technology <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Fashion_Institute_of_Technology> museum and later the Costume Institute
at the Metropolitan Museum of Art <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art>, wrote (1988): "[This] assertion about
history is wrong and can be demonstrated to be perniciously <https://
en.wiktionary.org/wiki/pernicious> wrong".^[402] <#cite_note-450>
According to National Galleries of Scotland <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/National_Galleries_of_Scotland> curator A. E. Haswell Miller
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archibald_Eliot_Haswell_Miller>
(1956):^[398] <#cite_note-HaswellMiller1956-446>

    To sum up, the presumed heraldic or "family badge" significance of
    the tartan has no documentary support, and the establishment of the
    myth can be accounted for by a happy coincidence of the desire of
    the potential customers, the manufacturer and the salesman. Although
    the antiquity of the "clan tartans" is exaggerated, what might be
    termed their unofficial registration took place during the
    nineteenth century, and if we are prepared to accept some hundred
    and fifty years as sufficient to create "tradition", it may be
    excusable to accept the /fait accompli/ as a pleasant – and perhaps
    not entirely useless – national vanity.

Highland-dress <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highland_dress> researcher
and curator John Telfer Dunbar added:^[403] <#cite_note-451>

    The desire to give to relics of all kinds greater antiquity than
    they truly possess is manifold. It is a pity that tradition should
    be degraded in this way and the acceptance of such claims by later
    students has been a constant obstacle to research. The more
    difficult task of searching back to original sources has often been
    avoided in favour of easy acceptance.

Just that sort of research was performed by Peter Eslea MacDonald of the
Scottish Tartans Authority <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scottish_Tartans_Authority>, who – using every available surviving
company record and sample – reconstructed and traced the history of
tartan patterns from the leading weaver of the late Georgian through
Edwardian eras, a company instrumental in the actual design, spread, and
acceptance of clan tartans. His conclusion:^[399] <#cite_note-
EsleaMacDonald2012_2-447>

    Today, books and shops dealing with Highland dress <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highland_dress> will be mainly, if not
    exclusively, concerned with clan tartans. They may seek to suggest
    that these are the actual patterns worn by the Scottish clans
    throughout history, up to and including the Battle of Culloden
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_Culloden> in 1746. This is
    not the case. The majority of the pre-1850 patterns bearing clan
    names can only be traced back to the early 19th century and to the
    famous weaving firm of William Wilson & Son of Bannockburn, near
    Stirling.

The notion of clan tartans has been called "an astonishingly successful
marketing story"^[395] <#cite_note-PatersonMB_155-443> and an example of
an invented tradition <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Invented_tradition>,^[404] <#cite_note-452> though one that became very
well-accepted by the clans to whom it pertained and by the weaving
industry starting in 1815, as well as by the general public from around
1822 – "adopted enthusiastically by both wearer and seller alike".^[405]
<#cite_note-PatersonMB_Airds-453>

Precursors of clan tartans were regionally distinctive tartans (since at
least the early 18th century, perhaps even the 16th), regimental uniform
tartans (from 1725 onward), and personal tartans of nobles (dating to
perhaps the mid-18th century if not earlier).

Today, clan tartans are an important aspect of Scottish clans, and every
clan has at least one tartan attributed to its name (some officially,
some not, and in a few cases one tartan is shared between multiple
clans). Clan tartans may not have actually been traditional, but they
became conventional.


      Long-running debate[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
      title=Tartan&action=edit&section=16>]

Painting of a curly-haired, portly, middle-aged man in a red-and-black
tartan outfit, with sword belt over his shoulder <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mosman;_John_Campbell_1749.jpg>John Campbell
of the Bank, 1749, by William Mosman <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/
index.php?title=William_Mosman&action=edit&redlink=1>. The present
official Clan Campbell <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Campbell>
tartans are predominantly blue, green and black.^[406] <#cite_note-
Ian_Campbell-454>

Various writers on tartans have supported or opposed the idea of clans
long using distinctive tartans as an identifying badge, interpreting the
scarce evidence as suited their viewpoint.^[aw] <#cite_note-456> Where
one saw a militia uniform, or an individual noble's plaid, another saw a
clan identifier. The 19th-century Celtic scholar John Francis Campbell
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Francis_Campbell> of Islay was
certain that while tartans in general were quite old, "uniform clan
tartans are no older than clan regiments", a view backed by Haldane
(1931) in a series of articles in /The Scots Magazine <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Scots_Magazine>/,^[408] <#cite_note-457>
followed by many tartan writers later.

The earliest evidence summarised below could have been more a matter of
militia uniform than clan-wide dress; a distinction in that era is
difficult to be certain of today, because troops then were led by landed
gentry and a unit was raised largely on its commander's land from his
clansmen.^[ax] <#cite_note-458> Such definitional uncertainty could also
apply to the 1691 /Grameid/ poem;^[241] <#cite_note-Grameid-274>
describing what appear to be some soldierly uniform tartans,^[ay]
<#cite_note-459> it could be reinterpreted as supporting an early notion
of clan tartans, if one wanted to define that as 'what most of the men
of a clan were wearing into battle'; Scarlett (1990) confirms that there
has been "fiery argument" in favour of a clan tartans
interpretation.^[243] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_13-276> However, Robert
Jamieson <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Jamieson_(antiquary)>
(1818) reported that the "field dress" plaids of Highland <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_Highlands> men, for war and hunting, were
different from their everyday dress – made of coarser material and using
patterns intended to blend into natural surroundings, the /cath dath/
or /cath da'/ ('war colour').^[95] <#cite_note-StewartDW_33-111> ^[az]
<#cite_note-461> This casts some doubt on interpretation of militia
tartans as general clan tartans. Most of the later regimental uniform
tartans (which did not become adopted as clan tartans until around the
early 19th century or the late 18th in a few cases, when they did at
all) were variations on the dark, green-based Black Watch tartan, as
detailed above.

J. C. Thompson (1992) noted "a typical Victorian inclination to cite
previous authors with little or no attempt to evaluate their
statements .... Modern analysis cannot afford to be so
uncritical."^[410] <#cite_note-Thompson1992_iii-462> Scarlett (1990)
relatedly observed:^[411] <#cite_note-463>

    Wishful proofs are found in profusion in the literature of tartan,
    early and late, and consist of stating an opinion as a fact and
    adding some more or less relevant historical reference in support,
    either implying or stating that this proves the point. That it
    proves nothing at all is neither here nor there, so long as the
    manner of the presentation is sufficiently authoritative; given this
    treatment the wildest theory will be accepted, copied from one book
    to the next and so enter tartan lore. It is almost axiomatic that
    the wilder the theory the more acceptable it will be ....

Even D. W. Stewart (1893), who had sometimes been sympathetic toward the
idea of clan tartans existing before the 19th century, wrote:^[412]
<#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979-StewartDW-Scott-464>

    Some ... assure us that the antiquity of the so-called clan patterns
    is very great, and many writers allege in general terms that these
    designs were used as a clan distinction from the earliest
    period. ... The halo of romance surrounding the Jacobite <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jacobitism> struggle inclined many, and still
    induces others, to accept as authentic and reliable, statements
    which in different circumstances would be more closely sifted. Thus
    it is that the tartans ... have won much favour, and those who find
    one represented as bearing their name accept it as their ancient
    clan pattern without the inconvenience of investigation, or of
    posing any awkward questions.

The Victorians also engaged in some imaginative invention. Aside from
the outright forgery of the "Sobieski Stewarts <https://
en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
title=Sobieski_Stewarts&action=edit&redlink=1>" (see § 19th century
broad adoption <#19th_century_broad_adoption>, below), another extreme
case is Charles Rogers <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Charles_Rogers_(author)>, who in his /Social Life in Scotland/ (1884–86)
fantastically claimed that the ancient Picts <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Picts>' figural designs – which were painted or tattooed on their
bodies, and they went into battle nude ^[413] <#cite_note-465> – must
have been "denoting the families or septs <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Sept#Scotland> to which they belonged" and thus "This practice
originated the tartan of Celtic clans."^[414] <#cite_note-
TelferDunbar1979_23-466> Another asserted that tartan was invented
around a thousand years ago by Saint Margaret of Scotland <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saint_Margaret_of_Scotland>.^[414] <#cite_note-
TelferDunbar1979_23-466>

Aside from the unreliability of early writers (and later copiers of
them), part of the confusion and debate about clan tartans comes down to
definitions. Sir Thomas Innes of Learney <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Thomas_Innes_of_Learney>, writing in 1938 and described as "immensely
keen on [tartan] codification and the importance of it",^[415]
<#cite_note-PatersonMB_162-467> was one of the firmest proponents of the
idea of very old clan tartans (in the particular sense of 'patterns
consistently used for a period by certain clans', not 'patterns named
for certain clans and claimed by them to the present').^[ba]
<#cite_note-468> He held that some setts gradually became associated
with particular families (clans and septs thereof) over time;^[416]
<#cite_note-469> clan territories had mostly become stable by the 16th
century.^[417] <#cite_note-470> D. W. Stewart's 1893 reference shows
various cases of old district tartans later sometimes being identified
for a time with specific families before 19th-century adoption of their
own (usually different) clan tartans.^[bb] <#cite_note-473> Innes of
Learney wrote of clan tartans that (notwithstanding the unusual 1618
case covered below) "the tendency was rather to insist upon a similarity
of general hue than on similarity of detail",^[420] <#cite_note-474> a
vague sense that is not what "clan tartan" usually refers to. He also
reasoned that "it was not until about the 18th century that the clan
tartans became /conscious/ and /acknowledged/ badges of
identification".^[421] <#cite_note-Innes_of_Learney_9-475> However, the
surviving period source material lacks this "acknowledgement" and does
not actually suggest broad adoption of formal clan tartans (with clan
names, particularity of detail, and a symbolic, identifying intent)
until the early 19th century.


      Earliest evidence[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
      title=Tartan&action=edit&section=17>]

The "Sobieski Stuarts <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Sobieski_Stuarts>" (1842) and later D. W. Stewart (1893) made much of
some changes to the feu duty <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Feu_duty>
paid in woven cloth by locals of Noraboll on the island of Islay
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islay> to their lords. In 1587, under the
Macleans <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Maclean>, the cloth was to
be white, black, and green; in 1617, under the Mackenzies <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Mackenzie>, the demanded cloth-rent changed
to white, black, and grey. These writers were sure, without any further
evidence, that this represented a change of clan tartans.^[422]
<#cite_note-476> ^[bc] <#cite_note-479>

The only clear instance of a clan-based and specific livery <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Livery> tartan to an early date, rather than
simply regional and later regimental uniformity, is found in a 1618
letter from Sir Robert Gordon of Gordonstoun <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Sir_Robert_Gordon,_1st_Baronet> (in the employ of the Earl of
Sutherland <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
John_Gordon,_14th_Earl_of_Sutherland>) to Murray of Pulrossie <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Murray_of_Pulrossie>, chieftain of the Murray
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Murray> branch in Sutherland
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sutherland> but subordinate to the Earl
of Sutherland, chief of Clan Sutherland <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Clan_Sutherland> (in turn recently become subordinate to the Gordon
earls <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Gordon>). The letter
(rediscovered in 1909) requested Pulrossie "to remove the red and white
lines from the plaides of his men so as to bring their dress into
harmony with that of the other septs" of Sutherland.^[394] <#cite_note-
Innes_of_Learney_9-10-442> The letter does not specify the tartan to
which to conform; there have been sharply conflicting interpretations,
and it is not even certain that it was a tartan that survived to the
present.^[bd] <#cite_note-483>

Stylized bagpiper in green-and-red tartan kilt, red-and-white tartan
hose, red embroidered coat, dark bonnet with red cockade, and an
armorial banner blowing behind him <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:GrantPiper.JPG>This 1714 portrait, by Richard Waitt <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Waitt>, of the piper to the chief of Clan
Grant <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Grant> does show a broad
green-ish and red tartan, but it does not match any modern Grant
pattern.^[428] <#cite_note-484> ^[be] <#cite_note-486>

A case of general colour-matching: In 1703–04, the chief of Clan Grant
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Grant> ordered that his "fencible
<https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fencible>" men obtain clothing in red
and green tartan^[247] <#cite_note-StewartDW_26-28-281> (vaguely
described as "broad springed"^[247] <#cite_note-StewartDW_26-28-281> but
not specified in detail).^[165] <#cite_note-SCSC_history-188> The
material seems not to have been provided by Grant for them in a
centralised way, but left to each man to furnish by his own means (on
penalty of a fine).^[247] <#cite_note-StewartDW_26-28-281> "He did not
order them to wear the 'Clan Grant Tartan', as one would expect if such
a tartan existed at that time."^[430] <#cite_note-
TelferDunbar1979_62-487> Some of the modern Grant tartans also use red
and green; one was designed by Wilsons of Bannockburn in 1819 as "New
Bruce" and shortly adopted by both Grant of Redcastle <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Redcastle>^[431] <#cite_note-
TelferDunbar1979_146-488> and Clan Drummond <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Clan_Drummond>;^[432] <#cite_note-489> one was reconstructed from
an 1838 portrait;^[433] <#cite_note-490> another first appeared in the
dubious /Vestiarium Scoticum/ of 1842^[434] <#cite_note-491> ^[435]
<#cite_note-492> (see below); and so on – none with pre-19th-century
history. Nevertheless, D. W. Stewart (1893) proclaimed on this thin
material that here was "a complete chain of evidence ... of the
existence of a uniform clan pattern at the very start of the eighteenth
century" – despite his own observation that portraits of leading members
of the Grant family in this era do not show them wearing consistent
tartans,^[247] <#cite_note-StewartDW_26-28-281> ^[243] <#cite_note-
Scarlett1990_13-276> much less ones that agree with modern "official"
Grant tartans.^[396] <#cite_note-Ward_Edwards-444> ^[436]
<#cite_note-493> ^[bf] <#cite_note-494> Scarlett (1990), though thinking
this presaged "the Clan Tartan Idea", notes that "had the men of
Strathspey <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strathspey,_Scotland> been
accustomed to wearing uniform tartans it would not have been necessary
to order them to do so"^[243] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_13-276> (twice
over). He also observes that the lairds of Grant in this period were
unusually bent on uniformity, one of them even issuing moustache
regulations for clansmen;^[243] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_13-276> the
Grant red-and-green order cannot be taken as typical of everyday
Highland practice. Telfer Dunbar (1979) notes that Highland military
discipline hardly existed: "To these independent Highland chieftains
restraint of any kind was irksome and unbearable, and to impose any
rigid military discipline on their followers ... [was] found to be
impossible."^[437] <#cite_note-495> Nevertheless, Mackay (1924)
corroborates Grant militia wearing a livery tartan in 1715.^[248]
<#cite_note-Mackay_50-282>

In 1718, Allan Ramsay <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Allan_Ramsay_(poet)> (the writer, father of the artist by the same name)
published the poem /Tartana/, which combined colours with Latinised
family names: "... If shining red Campbella <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Clan_Campbell>'s cheeks adorn .... If lin'd with green Stuarta
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Stewart>'s Plaid we view ... Or
thine Ramseia <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Ramsay>, edg'd around
with blue ...." This has sometimes been taken as evidence of early clan
tartans, despite possibly just referring to the edging and lining of
garments (coloured facings were common on jackets of the time).^[438]
<#cite_note-496> ^[bg] <#cite_note-498> Worse for this hypothesis, the
Campbell tartans are predominantly green, Stuart/Stewart red, and Ramsay
red and green. The extant red Campbell tartans are all modern
reconstructions of patterns (that are unlike each other) from
portraits;^[440] <#cite_note-499> ^[441] <#cite_note-500> ^[442]
<#cite_note-501> Stewart/Stuart tartans with significant green date to
the early 19th century^[443] <#cite_note-502> ^[444] <#cite_note-503>
^[445] <#cite_note-504> ^[446] <#cite_note-505> ^[447] <#cite_note-506>
or much later;^[448] <#cite_note-507> ^[449] <#cite_note-508> ^[450]
<#cite_note-509> and the Ramsay blue hunting sett dates to 1950.^[451]
<#cite_note-510>

A Victorian volume, /Old and New Edinburgh/ (1884) by James Grant
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Grant_(1822%E2%80%931887)>,^[452]
<#cite_note-511> stated that one Rev. Joseph Robertson MacGregor
"attired himself in a full suit of the MacGregor tartan" in 1782, upon
repeal of the Dress Act. But it misquoted the original source (and
contained other errors). The original, /A Series of Original Portraits
and Caricature Etchings/ (1842) by John Kay <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/John_Kay_(caricaturist)>, read: "dressed himself in the Highland
costume peculiar to his clan", and says nothing of tartan, much less a
suit of clan tartan.^[453] <#cite_note-512> While 1782 is within the
late-18th-century range accepted by some researchers for some informal
early clan tartans, this is not clear evidence of one.


      Lack of further evidence of early adoption[edit <https://
      en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tartan&action=edit&section=18>]

John Lesley <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Lesley>, bishop of Ross,
in 1578 wrote a great deal about Highland customs, including dress, but
did not include clan tartans (despite later being claimed to have been
the original keeper of the /Vestiarium Scoticum/ clan-tartans
manuscript, now known to be a 19th-century forgery).^[454]
<#cite_note-513> In 1688, William Sacheverell, a Manx <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isle_of_Man> politician, described Hebrideans of
the Isle of Mull <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isle_of_Mull> all
wearing plaids, but the women in a different style of colour and
pattern – not a consistent "clan" tartan.^[240] <#cite_note-Stewart/
Dunbar_Sacheverell-273> Rev. Thomas Morer in 1689 described Highland
garb in some detail, including tartan plaids and hose (made from the
same cloth), but mentions no clan patterns.^[455] <#cite_note-514>
Daniel Defoe <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Daniel_Defoe> (c. 1720)
wrote also in considerable detail of Highland warriors of the prior
century, and noted that the men were organised into "companies, all of a
name", each led by "one of their own clan or family", yet he never
