mentions any distinction between tartans of these different groups,
instead describing them all as wearing tartan with red and yellow over-
checks,^[227] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_28-29-258> strongly implying
a regional style. This pattern of 17th- through 18th-century writings
providing specifics of tartan and Highland dress, but nothing about clan
tartans, is consistent.

Contemporary portraits show that although tartan is of an early date,
the pattern worn depended not on the wearer's clan, but rather regional
style and personal taste. They frequently depict subjects wearing
multiple tartans at once.^[421] <#cite_note-Innes_of_Learney_9-475> Nor
do the tartans shown match current clan tartans.^[456] <#cite_note-515>
^[457] <#cite_note-516> For example, the famous painting /The MacDonald
Boys Playing Golf/ <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Attributed_to_William_Mosman_-_Sir_James_Macdonald_1741_-
_1765_and_Sir_Alexander_Macdonald_1744_-_1810_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg>
(1740s), attributed usually to William Mosman <https://en.wikipedia.org/
w/index.php?title=William_Mosman&action=edit&redlink=1> but sometimes to
Jeremiah Davison <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jeremiah_Davison>, shows
them wearing five different tartans, and they are not surviving patterns
(except as later reconstructions from the painting).^[bh]
<#cite_note-518> Period tartans were also often of differing warp and
weft <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warp_and_weft> (giving more of a
striped than checked appearance), unlike modern symmetrical
patterns.^[459] <#cite_note-519> Sometimes the portraits were copied,
but with tartans that do not match, as if the designs were up to
artistic whim.^[460] <#cite_note-520> As Scarlett (1990) put it:^[461]
<#cite_note-521>

    "[T]hese portraits have one thing in common: in no case does the
    tartan shown bear any close resemblance to the modern 'Clan'
    tartan. ... There is a great lack of evidence to show that the
    pattern of a tartan had any important significance in the early
    eighteenth century

D. W. Stewart (1893) had also noted this, about both portrait tartans
and "examples of tartan fabrics which can be proved to date from the
risings of 1715 and 1745".^[412] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979-StewartDW-
Scott-464> Many of the portraits by Allan Ramsay <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allan_Ramsay_(artist)> the younger show the same
shoulder plaid but with colours changed, suggesting it was the artist's
own studio prop and used for modelling purposes by his clients who
apparently did not care about the tartan pattern.^[27] <#cite_note-
Scarlett1990_23-31> ^[166] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2016-189> ^[316]
<#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2010-11-a-361> According to Scottish National
Portrait Gallery <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scottish_National_Portrait_Gallery> keeper A. E. Haswell Miller
(1956):^[398] <#cite_note-HaswellMiller1956-446>

    Authentic documentation of the tartan previous to the 19th century
    is limited to a comparatively small number of contemporary
    portraits, and is negative so far as it provides any suggestion of
    heraldic <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heraldry> significance or
    "clan badge" intention.

<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:The_Battle_of_Culloden.jpg>David
Morier <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Morier>'s /An Incident in
the Rebellion of 1745 <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
An_Incident_in_the_Rebellion_of_1745>/. The tartans shown generally do
not resemble modern ones.

According to Trevor-Roper (1983):^[462] <#cite_note-522>

    contemporary evidence concerning the rebellion of 1745 <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jacobite_rising_of_1745> – whether pictorial,
    sartorial <https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sartorial>, or literary –
    shows no differentiation of clans, no continuity of setts....
    Tartans were a matter of private taste, or necessity, only.

David Morier <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Morier>'s well-known
mid-18th-century painting <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
An_Incident_in_the_Rebellion_of_1745> of the Highland charge at the 1745
Battle of Culloden <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_Culloden>
shows eight Highlanders wearing over twenty different tartans which have
been analysed in detail;^[463] <#cite_note-523> very few of the setts
painted resemble today's clan tartans,^[294] <#cite_note-
Campbell_of_Airds-336> ^[302] <#cite_note-BdLC_84-346> though they are
similar to existing samples of tartan cloth from the era.^[464]
<#cite_note-524> ^[bi] <#cite_note-528> The method of identifying
Highlander friend from foe was not through tartans but by the colour of
the bonnet <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bonnet_(headgear)#Men>'s
cockade <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cockade> or ribbon, or perhaps by
the different plant sprigs worn in the cockade of the bonnet.^[175]
<#cite_note-Trevor-Roper_23-199> ^[132] <#cite_note-Thompson1992_iv-150>
^[bj] <#cite_note-531> ^[bk] <#cite_note-533> In particular, the
government Highland militia forces wore a badge in the form of a black
cockade with red saltire <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saltire>;
according to Mackay Scobie (1946), "each individual wore his own
Highland dress with varied tartans, with the only uniform part being the
'Hanoverian <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/House_of_Hanover>' cockade
and large coloured cross on the bonnet."^[471] <#cite_note-534> A 1745
letter on the Scottish troops at Culloden describes "all ye Forces as
well Horse as foot were in Highland Dress except ye body Guards wh. wore
Blue bound wth Red"; i.e., only the bodyguards were wearing a uniform,
and it was not of Highland dress.^[472] <#cite_note-535>

One of many tartan legends has it that the Highland-dress ban of the
Dress Act <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dress_Act_1746> was enacted
because tartans were used as clan-identifying symbols or uniforms, but
not a trace of this idea can be found in period sources. To the
contrary, Burt (1727–37) was explicit that English objection to Highland
dress (since perhaps 1703–04)^[328] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_14-373>
was /general/, because the garb served to distinguish the Highlanders as
a people apart from the Lowlanders <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scottish_Lowlands> and other British (not distinguish Highlander from
Highlander).^[473] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_4-536> ^[bl]
<#cite_note-537> Defoe (c. 1720) likewise mocked Highland dress as what
he saw as a clownish costume that set Highlanders apart from everyone
else, not each other.^[227] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_28-29-258>
Similarly, in an account of Jacobite trials, it was asked whether
defendants had worn "Highland cloaths" in general, with no mention of
clan-identifying patterns.^[474] <#cite_note-538> Extant MacDonald
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Donald> tartan fragments from the
Battle of Culloden do not match each other or any current clan tartan
named MacDonald.^[470] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_20-532> Lord
President <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Lord_President_of_the_Court_of_Session> Duncan Forbes of Culloden
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Duncan_Forbes_of_Culloden_(judge,_born_1685)>, keen on punishing the
Jacobites with disarmament and other penalties, wrote a detailed letter
laying out /pro/ and /con/ points (mostly /con/) regarding the proposed
Highland-dress ban before Parliament passed it, yet never indicated
anything like clan tartans, something that would have been a key
argument to address.^[475] <#cite_note-539>

C. C. P. Lawson (1967) raised a point of logic: "Remembering the
continuous clan feuds and the consequent state of more or less perpetual
hostilities, a recognisable clan plaid would have been a positive danger
to the wearer outside his own territory."^[476] <#cite_note-
TelferDunbar1979_15_Lawson-540> This may explain why the handful of
early apparent examples of groups of men in similar tartan seem to have
the nature of militia uniforms and are mentioned in the context of
"fencible <https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fencible>" bodies or outright
battle (possibly aside from the 1618 case).^[bm] <#cite_note-541> Lawson
also states: "The '45 <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Jacobite_rising_of_1745> supplies no evidence that tartans were used as
clan insignia .... Relics of those tartans which were worn at Culloden
or of the pre-1745 period bear no resemblance to any known modern
tartan."^[476] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_15_Lawson-540> The Lord Lyon
King of Arms <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lord_Lyon_King_of_Arms> in
1948, Sir Francis James Grant <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Francis_James_Grant>, wrote that pre-1745 tartans were qualitatively
different from those of the 19th century and later.^[477]
<#cite_note-542> Scottish United Services Museum <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_War_Museum> curator Dunbar (1979) notes
this as well.^[478] <#cite_note-543>

The Jacobite poets wrote much about the rousing appeal of Highland clans
and Highland dress, even tartan specifically, but never mentioned clan
tartans.^[479] <#cite_note-DunbarJD1979_16-17-544> Similarly, multiple
large volumes of traditional Highland folklore were collected and
published by John Francis Campbell <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
John_Francis_Campbell> in 1860–1862 and Alexander Carmichael <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_Carmichael> (who also collected tartan
samples) in 1900, but they are devoid of any recorded references to clan
tartans^[479] <#cite_note-DunbarJD1979_16-17-544> (despite post-dating
the popularisation of the notion among city-dwellers and the upper class).

The idea of groups of men wearing the exact same tartan as an identifier
is thought to originate (aside, again, from the odd 1618 case) from
Highland regiment <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highland_regiment>
units in the 18th century, starting with the Black Watch in
1739/1749.^[294] <#cite_note-Campbell_of_Airds-336> ^[214] <#cite_note-
Newsome_Black_Watch-243> According to Trevor-Roper (1983):^[480]
<#cite_note-545>

    [I]t was probably their use of it which gave birth to the idea of
    differentiating tartan by clans; for as the Highland regiments were
    multiplied ... so their tartan uniforms were differentiated; and
    when the wearing of tartan by civilians was resumed, and the
    romantic movement encouraged the cult of the clan, the same
    principle of differentiation was easily transferred from regiment to
    clan.

Particular regiments were often dominated by men raised from the same
clan lands, and this may have blurred the line between regimental
uniform and clan-identifying tartan. (And several tartans of extinct
regiments survive today as clan tartans.) Newsome (2006) writes: "the
practice of clans wearing these regimental tartans may have in fact been
the inspiration for the 'clan tartan' system as we now know it."^[214]
<#cite_note-Newsome_Black_Watch-243> Telfer Dunbar (1979), on the idea
of the early Independent Highland Companies <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Independent_Highland_Companies> using distinct uniform tartans: "I
feel sure that here we have much of the 'clan tartan' origin."^[481]
<#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_159-546> The end of the 18th and beginning
of the 19th centuries brought an unprecedented level of influence of
military clothing styles, including Highland regimental, on civilian
attire (even for women), especially among the social elite connected to
regiments.^[340] <#cite_note-Tuckett_10-11-385> Some regimental tartans
appear to have been named after their commanding officers, and this may
be how they came to be associated with family/clan names over
time.^[482] <#cite_note-547> ^[368] <#cite_note-
Barnes_&_Allen_84-86-414> Banks & de La Chapelle (2007):^[323]
<#cite_note-BdLC_87-368>

    the notion of differentiation of tartan by clans might have evolved
    from this desire to distinguish on Highland regiment uniform from
    another. Certainly, its classification for military use laid the
    groundwork for many subsequent designs and the movement toward
    uniformity.

Scarlett (1990) also observed the connection to regional or "district"
tartans:^[245] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_34-278>

    [B]asic patterns prevailed over wide areas and were modified by
    local weavers for their own ends. It can easily be seen that a local
    pattern of this kind, made for a captive clientele, might have
    become identified with the people of that locality who were
    themselves predominantly of one Clan or family group and its
    adherents and, when the belief grew up that Clan tartans had been
    worn since the beginning of time, have become, by retrospection, the
    Clan tartan of that group. There is no evidence that the Highlanders
    themselves looked on tartan in that light, however ....

An young woman in a coat of predominantly-red tartan and white satin
with gold braid, and a white headdress, with a white rose and what is
probably a riding crop <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Unknown_Jacobite_Lady,_c._1740%E2%80%9350,_attributed_to_Cosmo_Alexander.jpg>Unknown Jacobite lady in Tullabardine tartan, c. 1740–1750, attributed to Cosmo Alexander <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmo_Alexander>

Haswell Miller (1956) similarly noted: "We can ... readily accept that
certain dyes would prevail in different regions and that traditional
types of pattern might be followed in various parts."^[398] <#cite_note-
HaswellMiller1956-446> Martin Martin <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Martin_Martin> in 1703 had described tartans as being identifiably
specific to particular regions, but not clans.^[244] <#cite_note-
Scarlett/Stewart/Dunbar_Martin-277> There are numerous cases of tartans
loosely associated with districts later becoming clan tartans. The best-
documented case^[8] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2010-11-b-8> is the
Tullibardine <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tullibardine> pattern, one
of the few modern clan tartans that can be traced (at all, not as a clan
tartan) to the pre-proscription period.^[316] <#cite_note-
EsleaMacDonald_2010-11-a-361> ^[8] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2010-11-
b-8> It was long associated with Perthshire <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Perthshire>, and later adopted as the Murray <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Murray> of Tullibardine clan tartan, but sold
by Wilsons as simply "Tullibardine" as late as c. 1830–40, and it was
found for sale in a market by W. & A. Smith around 1850, who also said
it was worn then by Charles Murray, Earl of Dunmore <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Murray,_7th_Earl_of_Dunmore>;^[316]
<#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2010-11-a-361> the first record of the
pattern as "Murray of Tullibardine" is in their 1850 book.^[8]
<#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2010-11-b-8> It appears in at least five
early portraits; four date to c. 1740–1750, the first of an unknown
female sitter attributed to Cosmo Alexander <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Cosmo_Alexander>,^[bn] <#cite_note-549> and three by Allan Ramsay
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allan_Ramsay_(artist)> (with the cloth
painting completed by Joseph van Aken <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Joseph_van_Aken>) which are not of any known Murrays (but of a Campbell
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Campbell,_4th_Earl_of_Loudoun>, a
MacLeod <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Norman_MacLeod_(The_Wicked_Man)>,
and a MacDonald <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flora_MacDonald>).^[316]
<#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2010-11-a-361> It is not until 1770 that a
known Murray is painted wearing it (John Murray, Earl of Dunmore
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Murray,_4th_Earl_of_Dunmore>, by
Joshua Reynolds <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joshua_Reynolds>),^[316]
<#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2010-11-a-361> which still does not
necessarily make it a "clan tartan" at that early a date ("evidence for
its historic use by that branch [of Clan Murray] is circumstantial at
best").^[8] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2010-11-b-8> The oldest version
of it differs slightly as to colours and sett from the modern clan
version.^[8] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2010-11-b-8> ^[bo] <#cite_note-550>

Similarly, according to the Scottish Register of Tartans <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_Register_of_Tartans>, the district tartan
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Huntly_district_tartan,_tileable.png> for Huntly <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Huntly>,^[304] <#cite_note-SRT_Huntly-348>
originating in more complex form as the personal tartan <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Marchioness_of_Huntly%27s_tartan,_tileable.png> of a Marchioness of
Huntly^[484] <#cite_note-SRT_Marchioness-551> (probably Henrietta
Mordaunt <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Alexander_Gordon,_2nd_Duke_of_Gordon>), was in use as a regional tartan
since at least "the '45", and worn at Culloden by clansmen of Brodie
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Brodie>, Forbes <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Forbes>, Gordon <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Clan_Gordon>, MacRae <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Macrae>,
Munro <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Munro>, and Ross <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Ross>, "which gives a strong indication of
the greater antiquity of the 'District' setts compared to the Clan
tartans."^[484] <#cite_note-SRT_Marchioness-551>

Some surviving early records of tartan manufacture are those of the
Orphan Hospital Manufactory and Paul's Work, in Edinburgh, for the
period 1734–37 and 1751–52; tartans were not named but given numeric
designations such as "No. 2nd".^[159] <#cite_note-SRT_NRS-181> In 1745,
the /Caledonian Mercury <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Caledonian_Mercury>/ of Edinburgh carried an advertisement for a "Great
Choice of Tartans, the newest Patterns" – not clan or even district
tartans, but newly devised ones, suggesting a fashion market driven by
novelty not supposed "heraldic" traditions. Even clan-tartans booster D.
W. Stewart (1893) conceded: "This advertisement, it may be urged, is a
stumbling-block in the way of those who argue for the antiquity of clan
patterns; for it seems peculiar that, when the city was filled with
Highlanders of all ranks and many clans, they should be offered not
their ancient setts ...."^[485] <#cite_note-StewartDW+Trevor-
Roper_on_1745_ad-552> Other advertisements for tartan from 1745 to the
early 19th century did not mention clans, or focus on the patterns at
all, but rather on the forms in which the cloth could be ordered.^[486]
<#cite_note-553> Even immediately after the repeal of the Dress Act in
1782, the demand was for "latest patterns and bright colours",^[487]
<#cite_note-554> with no hint of a family heraldry aspect.

William Wilson & Son of Bannockburn, just south of the dividing line
between the Highlands and Lowlands,^[488] <#cite_note-
EsleaMacDonald2012_3-4-555> were the first large-scale commercial tartan
producers;^[106] <#cite_note-Newsome_2006-123> founded c. 1765,^[1]
<#cite_note-Newsome_1994-1> they had become the foremost supplier of
tartan to the military by around 1770, and the dominant tartan weaver in
general.^[329] <#cite_note-Tuckett_19-374> It was an endeavor that
required the introduction of tartan recording, of standardisation of
setts and dyes, and of consistency and quality control.^[1] <#cite_note-
Newsome_1994-1> ^[489] <#cite_note-556> ^[488] <#cite_note-
EsleaMacDonald2012_3-4-555> Wilsons corresponded with their agents
(especially the son, James Wilson)^[103] <#cite_note-
TelferDunbar1979_145-120> in the Highlands to get information and
samples of cloth from the various districts to enable them to reproduce
"perfectly genuine patterns". Wilsons recorded over 200 setts in
addition to ones they designed in-house, collected in their 1819 /Key
Pattern Book/ of around 250 setts^[1] <#cite_note-Newsome_1994-1> (among
earlier in-house volumes to the 1770s). These tartans were numbered,
named after places, or given fanciful names such as "Rob Roy <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rob_Roy_MacGregor>", later sometimes family names
(after prominent members), sometimes foreign names like "Coburg", but
usually not those of clans,^[264] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_144-300>
^[388] <#cite_note-Newsome_2005-435> ^[490] <#cite_note-557> ^[132]
<#cite_note-Thompson1992_iv-150> nor, when they did, often matching
present clan patterns.^[491] <#cite_note-BdLC_104-558> ^[bp]
<#cite_note-559> A large proportion of the modern clan tartans,
however, /can/ be traced to this work – just often originally with
numbers or unrelated names.^[388] <#cite_note-Newsome_2005-435> The
evidence of direct adoption from Wilsons happening frequently completely
overwhelms "ancient clan tartans" sentiment.^[bq] <#cite_note-572>

The /Scottish National Dictionary/, in providing an unusually discursive
definition of /tartan/, includes: "[T]owards the end of the 18th century
and largely through the enterprise of Messrs Wilson, weavers in
Bannockburn, a series of tartans, each ascribed to a certain clan, was
devised and is now accepted as authoritative, though almost entirely
unhistorical."^[504] <#cite_note-573> Analysing the direct and strong
influence of Wilsons' /Key Pattern Book/ (/KPB/) on the later adoption
of clan tartans (see next section), Eslea MacDonald (2012)
concluded:^[505] <#cite_note-574>

    Some of the 1819 /KPB/ setts no longer retain their original names,
    others were altered or were the basis for a number of variations
    which were named or simply numbered .... Whatever their origins,
    these patterns gave rise to the idea of clan tartans as we know them
    today. In a very few cases a pattern's origins may have indeed been
    a lot older than the 1819 /KPB/ but their contemporary names were
    almost always the work of Wilsons or subsequent writers.

The Cockburn Collection of 56 tartan samples (some of them duplicates)
was put together between 1810 and c. 1825 (most likely 1816–25)^[506]
<#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2023-575> ^[388] <#cite_note-Newsome_2005-435>
by Lt.-Gen. Sir William Cockburn, and is now in the Mitchell Library
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mitchell_Library> in Glasgow <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glasgow>.^[388] <#cite_note-Newsome_2005-435>
^[507] <#cite_note-576> This collection does ascribe particular family
names to many of these setts (probably naming them after prominent
individuals),^[506] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2023-575> but only
sometimes corresponding to current clan tartan associations (indeed,
some patterns that are today associated with particular clans were given
multiple different names in the Cockburn Collection).^[br]
<#cite_note-578> There are many conflicts in name-to-pattern
associations between this collection and that of the Highland Society of
London <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highland_Society_of_London> around
the same time.^[506] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2023-575>

Even David Stewart of Garth <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
David_Stewart_of_Garth>, who was to become one of the chief proponents
of the idea of clan tartans, observed in 1814 only that various heads of
families seemed to have selected personal tartans and that there were
also district tartans.^[509] <#cite_note-579> When Garth and his
Highland Society of London solicited clan tartans from chiefs in 1815
(see below), Col. Alexander Robertson of Struan, Chief of Clan
Robertson/Donnachaidh/Duncan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Clan_Robertson>, wrote back:^[510] <#cite_note-580>

    It does not appear to be appertained, either by tradition or by
    authentick history, that the different Clans in the Highlands of
    Scotland, wore any distinctive pattern or tartan. It is well known
    that they all had particular Colours, or Standards <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Military_colours,_standards_and_guidons>,
    emblematical of some of their most honourable attachments, but as
    far as I have been able to discover, they wore no uniform Garb.

At the beginning of the 19th century, a letter from an Inverness tailor
to Wilsons of Bannockburn requested fine tartan cloth to be used for
women's clothing, because the fashion was to wear husbands' regimental
tartans (not clan tartans).^[348] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_102-393>
In 1829, responding negatively to the idea of Lowland and Borders
"clans" wearing their own tartans, Sir Walter Scott <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Walter_Scott> – who was instrumental in helping
start the clan-tartans fervour in the first place – wrote "where had
slept this universal custom that nowhere, unless in this MS. [the
draft /Vestiarium Scoticum/, published ultimately in 1842] is it even
heard of? ... I would rather suppose that the author had been some
tartan-weaver zealous for his craft, who wished to extend the use of
tartan over the whole kingdom."^[511] <#cite_note-581> Also in the same
year, he wrote: "The idea of distinguishing the clans by their tartans
is but a fashion of modern date in the Highlands themselves".^[512]
<#cite_note-582>

Another of the tartan legends has it that Alexander Gordon, 4th Duke of
Gordon <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Alexander_Gordon,_4th_Duke_of_Gordon>, commissioned the design of a clan
tartan based on Black Watch in 1793, kept one of three designs, then
passed the other two on to cadet branches <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Cadet_branch> of the family.^[513] <#cite_note-583> This tale can
be traced in unembellished form to 1793 records of weaver William
Forsyth of Huntly which do not say this at all, only that Forsyth
provided three potential designs for a /regiment/ tartan, with yellow
over-checks in various configurations, of which the Duke selected no. 2
for the unit, the 92nd Gordon Highlanders <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/92nd_(Gordon_Highlanders)_Regiment_of_Foot>.^[375] <#cite_note-
TelferDunbar1979_159-160-422>

Scarlett (1990) surmises that there must have been /some/ informal clan
tartans – a confluence of district tartans that had become associated
with particular families, and adoptions of regimental uniform tartans by
them – by the late 18th century, otherwise there is no explanation for
where Stewart of Garth got the idea.^[514] <#cite_note-584> Scottish
United Services Museum curator Maj. I. H. Mackay Scobie (1942),^[400]
<#cite_note-MackayScobie-448> Haswell Miller (1947),^[515]
<#cite_note-585> and Barnes & Allen (1956),^[94] <#cite_note-
Barnes_&_Allen_266-110> also zeroed in on this timeframe. Eslea
MacDonald (2010-11) observes, for example, the Murrays using the common
Tullibardine regional pattern in portraits and in bed hangings at their
clan seat, Blair Castle <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blair_Castle>,
1770 – c. 1780 and possibly earlier.^[316] <#cite_note-
EsleaMacDonald_2010-11-a-361> Telfer Dunbar (1979), considering the
1703–04 Grant proclamation and the early regiments, suggests that "any
uniformity of tartan was only to be found in an organised body of
troops, or the 'tail' or following of a chief."^[430] <#cite_note-
TelferDunbar1979_62-487> These possible comparatively early, informal
clan tartans of the late-18th-century simply cannot usually be
identified (when they survived) until the early 19th century.


      19th century broad adoption[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/
      index.php?title=Tartan&action=edit&section=19>]

It has been suggested by a modern chief of Clan Campbell <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Campbell> and another of the clan executives
that the clan had informally adopted what is now known as old Campbell
or Black Watch <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Campbell#Tartans>
tartan by the early 19th century, because so many of their men were
already wearing it as part of regimental uniform^[406] <#cite_note-
Ian_Campbell-454> (three of the Independent Highland Companies that
amalgamated into the Black Watch <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
42nd_Regiment_of_Foot> regiment in 1739–1751 were Campbell units).^[481]
<#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_159-546> Some time in or after 1806, when
he became clan chief, the city-dwelling politician George Campbell, 6th
Duke of Argyll <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
George_Campbell,_6th_Duke_of_Argyll>, created his own personal tartan,
of Black Watch with a thin over-check of white and yellow added,^[516]
<#cite_note-586> "to differentiate himself from the rest of the
Campbells", i.e. because they were already so often wearing Black
Watch.^[406] <#cite_note-Ian_Campbell-454> This essentially may have
been one of the earliest attested surviving clan tartans (and the duke's
variant was an early declared personal tartan of a noble).^[bs]
<#cite_note-587>

Elderly man in Highland dress with a green coat and various medals,
wearing spectacles (eyeglasses) and leaning on a basket-hilted sword
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:David_Stewart_of_Garth,_by_James_Montgomery_Scrymgeour,_c._1820.jpg>Maj.-Gen. David Stewart of Garth <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Stewart_of_Garth>, c. 1820, in royal Stewart tartan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Stewart_tartan>

The idea arose among Scottish expatriates (especially in the Celtic
societies <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celtic_society>, which
encouraged members to wear "appropriate" tartans),^[517] <#cite_note-
Tuckett_15-588> eager to "preserve" Highland culture,^[1] <#cite_note-
Newsome_1994-1> ^[517] <#cite_note-Tuckett_15-588> ^[518]
<#cite_note-589> that tartans had traditionally been named and that the
names represented clan affiliations.^[1] <#cite_note-Newsome_1994-1>
Among them was Maj.-Gen. David Stewart of Garth <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Stewart_of_Garth>, a Black Watch veteran and
vice-president of the Highland Society of London <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highland_Society_of_London>^[519] <#cite_note-
TBW_Garth-590> (founded 1778).^[388] <#cite_note-Newsome_2005-435> He
and fellow members Sir John Sinclair <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Sir_John_Sinclair,_1st_Baronet> and Andrew Robertson <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andrew_Robertson_(miniaturist)> were among the
first proponents of the idea of clans being identified by tartans,
despite the lack of evidence.^[519] <#cite_note-TBW_Garth-590> ^[520]
<#cite_note-591> ^[521] <#cite_note-Trevor-Roper_28-29-592> ^[506]
<#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2023-575> ^[bt] <#cite_note-594> The society
also counted among its members the Prince of Wales <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prince_of_Wales>^[523] <#cite_note-
PatersonMB_158-159-595> (the future George IV <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/George_IV>, who was to become instrumental to clan "tartanry" in
1822) and two dukes <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Dukes_in_the_United_Kingdom>, among various itinerant actual Scots^[524]
<#cite_note-596>  – including James Macpherson <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Macpherson> of "Ossian <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ossian>" fame (or infamy).^[525] <#cite_note-597>

Full length portrait of young woman wearing a white empire-waist dress
with a plaid of tartan loosely draped over her head and left shoulder,
seated in a park <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Elizabeth_Brodie,_Wife_of_the_5th_Duke_of_Gordon.jpg>Elizabeth
Gordon (/née/ Brodie), Duchess of Gordon <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Elizabeth_Gordon,_Duchess_of_Gordon>, c. 1813–1814 by Alfred Edward
Chalon <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alfred_Edward_Chalon>; she appears
to be wearing Black Watch (42nd regiment) tartan, as it lacks the yellow
over-check of 92nd Regiment, which became the Gordon clan tartan. This
was only about a year before the Highland Society solicited clan patterns.

On 8 April 1815, the society resolved that the clan chiefs each "be
respectfully solicited to furnish the Society with as much of the Tartan
of his Lordship's Clan as will serve to Show the Pattern and to
Authenticate the Same by Attaching Thereunto a Card bearing the
Impression of his Lordship's Arms."^[526] <#cite_note-Urquhart_18-598>
Many had no idea of what their tartan might be or whether they had
one,^[527] <#cite_note-599> some provided only a vague description, and
some claimed they had none.^[523] <#cite_note-PatersonMB_158-159-595>
But plenty were keen to comply and to provide authentic signed and
sealed <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seal_(emblem)> samples;^[526]
<#cite_note-Urquhart_18-598> ^[523] <#cite_note-PatersonMB_158-159-595>
^[528] <#cite_note-Armstrong_37-600> many (possibly most) turned to
Wilsons of Bannockburn for a design,^[517] <#cite_note-Tuckett_15-588>
^[493] <#cite_note-Trevor-Roper_30-561> while some directly adopted a
regimental tartan as their own,^[388] <#cite_note-Newsome_2005-435> ^[1]
<#cite_note-Newsome_1994-1> ^[bu] <#cite_note-602> and still others
adapted designs from old portraits of clan nobles.^[523] <#cite_note-
PatersonMB_158-159-595> ^[bv] <#cite_note-604> Alexander Wentworth
Macdonald <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Alexander_Macdonald,_2nd_Baron_Macdonald>, Baron Macdonald <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Donald>, wrote back to the society: "Being
really ignorant of what is exactly The Macdonald Tartan, I request you
will have the goodness to exert every Means in your power to Obtain a
perfectly genuine Pattern, Such as Will Warrant me in Authenticating it
with my Arms."^[526] <#cite_note-Urquhart_18-598> Finding no agreement
within his clan on a pattern, Robertson of Struan ended up adopting the
regimental tartan of the Loyal Clan Donnachie (Robertson) Volunteers;
being based on the Black Watch pattern, it could not pre-date the late
18th century.^[531] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2012_4-605> On the other
hand, Sir John Macgregor Murray <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Sir_John_Macgregor_Murray,_1st_Baronet> of Clan Gregor <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Gregor>, who had spent most of his life in
England and India, was writing instructions on the use of his clan's
tartan by December 1818.^[532] <#cite_note-606> In 1819, Wilsons were
engaged in correspondence to "send ... specimens of all coloured Tartans
used by these Clans ...said to exceed thirty in number", to a writer in
Italy preparing a book on clan tartans;^[533] <#cite_note-607> the same
year, they also produced their /Key Pattern Book/ of over 200 tartans
(representing only a fraction of their total tartan output, presumably
the most marketable designs, and not always under the same names as
found in contemporary collections of Wilsons' tartan samples such as the
Cockburn collection and that of the Highland Society).^[534]
<#cite_note-608>

According to Trevor-Roper (1983), Wilsons were in a direct "alliance"
with the Highland Society of London by 1819; the former saw a great
marketing opportunity, and the latter provided a veneer of
respectability as Wilsons helped the society pin tartans to clan
names.^[535] <#cite_note-609> ^[536] <#cite_note-610> Banks & de La
Chapelle (2007) concur: "The Wilson firm worked in tandem with the
Highland Society, preparing tartan samples for the latter to certify as
belonging to one clan or another."^[491] <#cite_note-BdLC_104-558> Clan
nobles (who sometimes contradicted each other, within the same clan, on
what their tartan was or should be)^[537] <#cite_note-611> were
apparently also "ready to adopt changes at the mere dictation of fancy"
to improve designs.^[538] <#cite_note-612> From the "authentications"
they received 1815–26, the society built up a clan-tartan collection
(now in the National Museum of Scotland <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
National_Museum_of_Scotland>), with 34 authenticated specimens and about
40 others.^[523] <#cite_note-PatersonMB_158-159-595> ^[539]
<#cite_note-613> ^[bw] <#cite_note-614> Other such societies generated
more interest, belief, and demand.^[540] <#cite_note-615> According to
the analysis by Eslea MacDonald (2012), "Most of the pieces sealed [by
clan chiefs] and deposited with the Society at that time were patterns
woven, and in the majority of cases appear to have been designed, by
Wilsons. This obviously means they could not have existed prior to c1765
when William Wilson started his business."^[531] <#cite_note-
EsleaMacDonald2012_4-605> So many of Wilsons' stock tartans from their /
Key Pattern Book/ of 1819 were being renamed for clans that J. C.
Thompson (1992) wrote: "Clearly the naming of tartans was just getting
started in 1819",^[132] <#cite_note-Thompson1992_iv-150> and: "There was
nothing people wanted more than an ancient clan tartan system, and they
were determined to have one."^[541] <#cite_note-Thompson1992_v-616> By
1821, advertisements for tartan cloth had shifted to include language
like "true", "warranted", and "original", and began to stress antiquity
and family connections.^[542] <#cite_note-617>

The 1822 visit of George IV to Scotland <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Visit_of_George_IV_to_Scotland>, in Highland garb and with a great deal
of tartan-festooned public ceremony (arranged by Stewart of Garth and
romanticist <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Romanticism> writer Sir
Walter Scott <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Walter_Scott> of the Celtic
Society of Edinburgh <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
title=Celtic_Society_of_Edinburgh&action=edit&redlink=1>), had a
profound tartan-boosting effect, including the invention of new clan-
specific tartans to suit^[543] <#cite_note-Moncreiffe_24-618> ^[388]
<#cite_note-Newsome_2005-435> ^[544] <#cite_note-619> (or renaming of
old tartans to have clan names),^[1] <#cite_note-Newsome_1994-1> ^[545]
<#cite_note-Scarlett1990_36-620> as clan chiefs had been asked to attend
in clan tartans.^[1] <#cite_note-Newsome_1994-1> It caused a boom in the
tartan-weaving business,^[492] <#cite_note-Tuckett_17-560> and a broader
public notion that tartans should be named for families.^[388]
<#cite_note-Newsome_2005-435> ^[1] <#cite_note-Newsome_1994-1> "When
these two [Scott and Stewart of Garth] stage-managed the King's
visit ... they fixed the Clan Tartan idea in the public mind."^[545]
<#cite_note-Scarlett1990_36-620> Wilsons' pattern book in 1822 had
expanded significantly with tartans named for clans, in addition to all
their numbered setts.^[503] <#cite_note-DunbarJD1979_149-571> According
to R. Martin (1988), Wilsons and other weavers were made aware of the
king's planned visit three or four years in advance, and had all that
time to pad their catalogues with additional designs and to assign clan
names to patterns often "probably picked entirely out of the air."^[546]
<#cite_note-MartinR_54-621> He added that "anyone looking at the tartan
pattern books of 1819 to 1822 would have realized the cacophony of
different names for the same [pattern], the chaos of clan attributions,
and the complete capriciousness of that association."^[547]
<#cite_note-622> A telling letter from a tailor, archived among the
Wilsons papers, to the company in 1822 asked: "Please send me a piece of
Rose <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Rose> tartan, and if there
isn't one, please send me a different pattern and call it Rose."^[494]
<#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2012_5-562>

By 1824, an invitation to the Atholl Gathering <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atholl_Gathering>, one of the earliest of the
modern Highland games <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highland_games>
festivals, made it clear that participants should arrive "in the plaids
or Tartans of their Clans".^[548] <#cite_note-Armstrong_148-623> In
1829, Sir Thomas Dick Lauder <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Thomas_Dick_Lauder> complained to Walter Scott about all the "uncouth,
spurious, modern [tartans] which are every day manufactured, christened
after particular names, and worn as genuine",^[549] <#cite_note-624> and
also of "clans ... at this moment ignorantly disputing for the right to
the same tartans which in fact belong to none of them but are merely
modern inventions for clothing Regimental Highlanders".^[550]
<#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_116-625> Scott himself was backpedalling
away from what he had helped create, and was suspicious of the recent
claims about "ancient" clan tartans: "it has been the bane of Scottish
literature and disgrace of her antiquities, that we have manifested an
eager propensity to believe without inquiry and propagate the errors
which we adopt too hastily ourselves."^[551] <#cite_note-626>

A "busy" tartan of broad red bands, medium green ones, and thin white
ones <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Scott_tartan_(Vestiarium_Scoticum).png>The Scott <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Scott> tartan invented by the "Sobieski
Stuarts" around 1829, eventually published in the 1842 /Vestiarium/.
Based on the c. 1819 MacGregor <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Clan_Gregor>, the tartan was rejected (along with other Lowland family
tartans) by Walter Scott <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Walter_Scott>,
but remains the most popular Scott tartan.^[552] <#cite_note-627>

A wave of highly dubious books were published, all purporting to reveal
true clan histories and tartans; they presented little in the way of
evidence, but they caused enthusiastic adoption of clan tartans. The
first of these, in 1831, was /The Scottish Gaël or Celtic Manners, as
Preserved Among the Highlanders/ by James Logan <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Logan_(writer)>, containing 54 tartans
(based on Wilsons' collection, that of the Highland Society of London,
and other sources he alleged but did not name, plus some he collected or
devised himself); the author ignored advice from Wilsons on which were
actually old tartans, and included some erroneous, fictitious, and
incomplete setts.^[553] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_153-628> ^[554]
<#cite_note-629> ^[bx] <#cite_note-630> He also included untenable
assertions about the designs' antiquity; "Logan took the line that
everything Highland was rooted impossibly far in the past", and was
mocked in /The Pall Mall Gazette <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
The_Pall_Mall_Gazette>/ for it.^[545] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_36-620>
Meanwhile, Wilsons and other weavers simply adopted some patterns from
his book due to demand,^[555] <#cite_note-BdLC_106-108-631> ^[492]
<#cite_note-Tuckett_17-560> ^[556] <#cite_note-Armstrong_60-61-632> and
also took to inventing all-new "clan tartans" to keep up with the
growing market for patterns associated with names.^[557]
<#cite_note-633> The archived correspondence of Wilsons in the 1830s
shows that the company was frequently pressured by merchants for the
"truest" and "real" clan patterns.^[558] <#cite_note-
TelferDunbar1979_151-634> Logan, despite himself being involved in sham
clan tartanry, observed that "fanciful varieties of tartan ... were
being passed off as genuine" by Wilsons and other weavers.^[494]
<#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2012_5-562>

Logan was followed in 1842 by /Vestiarium Scoticum <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vestiarium_Scoticum>/ by the so-called Sobieski
Stuarts <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sobieski_Stuarts>, purporting to
contain 75 centuries-old clan tartans, illustrated in great detail but
from vague textual descriptions.^[559] <#cite_note-635> Although it is
now known to have been largely a forgery,^[560] <#cite_note-636> ^[561]
<#cite_note-Armstrong_61-637> ^[by] <#cite_note-641> many of the visual
tartan designs in this "final – and fantastic – codification"^[561]
<#cite_note-Armstrong_61-637> of clan tartans were nevertheless adopted
and still survive as accepted tartans of clans,^[565] <#cite_note-
EsleaMacDonald_history-642> ^[566] <#cite_note-BdLC_108-643> especially
for Lowland clan names (which had hitherto never been associated with
tartan or Highland garb at all).^[388] <#cite_note-Newsome_2005-435>
^[173] <#cite_note-Spinning-197> ^[567] <#cite_note-644> ^[568]
<#cite_note-645> Starting in 1822, Borders <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Scottish_Borders> families had been redefining themselves as clans,
and the book encouraged more of them to take on clan tartans and open
clan societies.^[569] <#cite_note-646> Modern critics have even praised
the lasting socio-cultural accomplishement of the Sobieski-Stuarts'
works in helping establish a systemic clan-tartans legend while
recognizing the bogus nature of their material.^[bz] <#cite_note-648>

(The socio-political background of these events and their overall impact
on tartan in general are presented at § Late Georgian <#Late_Georgian>
and § Victorian <#Victorian>, below.)

Trevor-Roper (1983) believed that the Sobieski Stuarts had been in
direct communication with manufacturers like Wilsons, and were advising
clan chiefs on which tartans to choose, from as early as 1819;^[571]
<#cite_note-Trevor-Roper_39-649> J. C. Thompson (1992) agreed.^[541]
<#cite_note-Thompson1992_v-616> Dick Lauder certainly said they were
doing so by 1829, and that Wilsons were already weaving many Sobieski
Stuart samples by that year;^[572] <#cite_note-650> the company's own
records the same year confirm orders for designs from the Sobieski
Stuarts.^[573] <#cite_note-651> /Vestiarium/ was followed soon after
by /The Costume of the Clans/ published by the Sobieski Stuarts in
1845;^[555] <#cite_note-BdLC_106-108-631> the illustrations it provided,
allegedly based on portraits, have proven to be largely a mixture of
error and invention.^[574] <#cite_note-652> By 1849, John Sobieski
Stuart was in discussion with a publisher to produce a new, cheaper
edition of /Vestiarium/, in a series of small volumes "so that it might
be rendered as available as possible to manufacturers and the trades in
general concerned in Tartan ... and it was for the[ir] advantage and
use ... that I consented to the publication." The same letter also
proposed binding the manufacturers by contract to produce tartans that
conformed exactly to the Sobieski Stuarts' specifications.^[575]
<#cite_note-653>

Weavers like Wilsons were complicit, not passive, in the tartan boom.
They had lost much of their military and export markets after major wars
ended and colonies in the Americas and elsewhere had become more self-
sufficient.^[498] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_64-566> "The concept of
differentiated clan tartans, newly popularized, was codified and
developed by canny manufacturers .... Since the repeal of the [Dress
Act], these tartan makers saw the prospect of a vast new market."^[265]
<#cite_note-BanksdeLaChapell_100-301> According to Alastair Campbell of
Airds <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Alastair_Lorne_Campbell_of_Airds>:^[405] <#cite_note-PatersonMB_Airds-453>

    One factor which has been decisive throughout the history of the
    development of the modern system [of clan tartans] has been the
    influence of the tartan manufacturers .... As with any marketing
    organisation it was important to maintain a steady flow of "new
    products", and every year new patterns were produced .... The idea
    of individual tartans providing a clan or family identity was a most
    attractive one, which was adopted enthusiastically by both wearer
    and seller alike.

Romanticised Highland warrior with red-and-blue tartan small kilt, a
targe, a basket-hilt broadsword, a fur vest, and a blue bonnet <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Maclachlan_(R._R._McIan).jpg>"Maclachlan
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Maclachlan>", a romanticised
Highland warrior image from Logan and McIan's /The Clans of the Scottish
Highlands/, 1843

This heavy promotion for decades of the clan-tartans idea has been
described as "inciting a rush to lay claim to the tartan to which one's
family was 'entitled'".^[576] <#cite_note-BdLC_26-654> Other 19th-
century clan-tartan works followed.^[565] <#cite_note-
EsleaMacDonald_history-642> Logan (by then president of the Highland
Society of London)^[571] <#cite_note-Trevor-Roper_39-649> returned, with
illustrator Robert Ranald McIan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
R._R._McIan>, with /The Clans of the Scottish Highlands/ in several
volumes 1843–1849, which had inconsistently hand-coloured portraits of
chiefs in clan tartans, which he stated were "acknowledged by the
present chiefs and clans".^[388] <#cite_note-Newsome_2005-435> /The
Clans of the Highlands of Scotland/ in 1850 by Thomas Smibert drew
heavily on Wilsons' patterns and on Logan.^[577] <#cite_note-
Scarlett1990_196-655> In the same year, /Authenticated Tartans of the
Clans and Families of Scotland/ by William & Andrew Smith was based on
trade sources such as Wilsons, competing mill Romanes & Paterson of
Edinburgh, and army clothier George Hunter's pre-1822 collection of
setts (and some consultation with historian W. F. Skene <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Forbes_Skene>).^[577] <#cite_note-
Scarlett1990_196-655> ^[410] <#cite_note-Thompson1992_iii-462> Also in
1850, Gen. James Browne published /History of the Highlands and the
Highland Clans/, another /Vestiarium/ knock-off.^[571] <#cite_note-
Trevor-Roper_39-649>

In 1871, Gaelic folklorist and Highland dress fancier John Francis
Campbell <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Francis_Campbell> of Islay
wrote in /Clan Tartans/:^[578] <#cite_note-656>

    I have come to the conclusion that Sir Walter Scott and my friends
    the Editors of the /Vestiarium Scoticum/ and Scotch Manufacturers of
    tartans are together responsible for the present flourishing and
    luxuriant crop of brilliant clan tartans .... I do not believe that
    the distinctions which are now made as to Clan Tartans ever
    prevailed at all, till Tartan became an important manufacture in
    Scotland in the reign of George the 4th

J. Claude produced the tartan pattern sample book /Clans Originaux/ in
Paris c. 1880, and some tartans were adopted from it,^[ca]
<#cite_note-659> though its 185 samples were mostly of already-known
tartans.^[19] <#cite_note-Newsome_Wilkinson-21> A second edition of /The
Costume of the Clans/ was published in 1892.^[581] <#cite_note-660>
Another influential book was Donald W. Stewart's /Old & Rare Scottish
Tartans/ (1893), which included swatches of fabric; several accepted
clan tartans date to this work.^[582] <#cite_note-661>

Books of this era also introduced lists of alleged clan septs <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sept#Scotland>, families of different surnames
(often of English, Norman <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anglo-Normans>,
or other non-Gaelic derivation) supposedly linked to particular clans as
"extended family". It was a means of greatly increasing tartan sales by
attaching many more names to extant tartan designs, but not well-
grounded in any historical reality.^[583] <#cite_note-
Duncan_of_Sketraw-662> ^[584] <#cite_note-663> ^[401] <#cite_note-
TelferDunbar1979_15-16_Haldane-449> Two such works, both published by
W. & A. K. Johnston were: /Tartans of the Clans and Septs of Scotland/
by James Grant in 1886, revised by Henry Whyte in 1906 in more of a
picture-book format (three tartans make their first appearance in the
1886 edition,^[585] <#cite_note-664> and various more in the 1906
version, with no provenance);^[586] <#cite_note-
Scarlett1990_20,_197-198-665> and /What Is My Tartan? or, The Clans of
Scotland, with Their Septs and Dependents/ by Adam Frank in 1896.^[388]
<#cite_note-Newsome_2005-435>

The romanticised notion of clan tartans had become deeply embedded in
the Scottish imagination and further afield.^[173] <#cite_note-
Spinning-197> ^[587] <#cite_note-666> "[I]t all got mixed up in the
public mind and the myth of tartan as a kind of heraldry <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heraldry> became established, not only in the eyes
of outsiders, even the Clansfolk believed it".^[588] <#cite_note-
Scarlett1990_37-667> On the cusp of the Scottish Renaissance <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_Renaissance> and Gaelic Revival <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaelic_Revival>, most clans (including major
Lowland families) had been assigned and had generally accepted one or
more tartans by the late 19th century.


      20th century consolidation[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/
      index.php?title=Tartan&action=edit&section=20>]

<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Charles_E._N._Leith_Hay_by_John_Ernest_Breun,_1905.jpg>Charles
E. N. Leith Hay, 1905 portrait by John Ernest Breun <https://
en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
title=John_Ernest_Breun&action=edit&redlink=1>, in Edwardian daywear
Highland dress, kilt in a dark rendition of the Hay and Leith <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Hay> tartan. Most clan tartans were settled
by the turn of the 19th and 20th centuries.

The first Edwardian <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edwardian_era> book
on the subject (aside from a larger 1906 "library edition" of Whyte as /
The Scottish Clans and Their Tartans with Notes/),^[586] <#cite_note-
Scarlett1990_20,_197-198-665> was Frank Adam's 1908 /The Clans, Septs &
Regiments of the Scottish Highlands/, which remains in print today
(though in drastically edited form, by Sir Thomas Innes of Learney
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Innes_of_Learney>).^[388]
<#cite_note-Newsome_2005-435> ^[589] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_198-668> A
variety of books, with colour plates, had been affordably and widely
published about clan tartans by the mid-20th century. Three popular ones
were /The Clans and Tartans of Scotland/ by Robert Bain, 1938 (the first
to use photographic halftone <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Halftone>
prints; revised and updated many times through 1983);^[590]
<#cite_note-669> /The Tartans of the Clans and Families of Scotland/ by
Innes of Learney (later to become the Lord Lyon King of Arms <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lord_Lyon_King_of_Arms> as well as a founder of
the Scottish Tartans Society <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scottish_Tartans_Society>),^[591] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_ix-670> 1938,
advancing some clan-tartanry ideas his Lord Lyon predecessor Sir Francis
James Grant <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Francis_James_Grant>
considered "humbug";^[589] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_198-668> and /The
Scottish Clans & Their Tartans/ published by W. & A. K. Johnston, 1945
(later editions re-titled /The Scottish Tartans with Historical
Sketches/, edited by Innes of Learney), and based on previous works by
Grant and Whyte. Many others followed in successive decades.^[388]
<#cite_note-Newsome_2005-435>

A wall covered with a bewildering array of different samples of tartan
cloth <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Lochcarron_-_Scotland.jpg>400
clan and district tartan samples at the headquarters of the weaver
Lochcarron of Scotland

The mass-market books (some with over 200 tartans illustrated) did much
to cement the idea of clan tartans in the public imagination, as well as
to consistently anchor particular tartans to particular clans. And the
works were in more general agreement with one another than had been the
Victorian "authorities".^[cb] <#cite_note-672> They also simultaneously
increased the number of clans with their own assigned tartans, and
reduced the number of tartans claimed to be those of certain clans to a
more manageable number, probably after consultation with clan chiefs and
clan society officers. They did, however, typically include sept lists,
which today are widely regarded as bogus^[583] <#cite_note-
Duncan_of_Sketraw-662> (though many present-day clan associations still
use them, as a means of attracting larger membership).

Almost every extant clan (with or without a chief) had at least one
tartan associated with it by this era. Many clans have several well-
accepted tartans. Sometimes they represent different branches of the
family; e.g., there are separate tartans for Campbell <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Campbell> of Breadalbane, Campbell of Cawdor,
and Campbell of Loudoun, in addition to the general "old" Campbell
tartan. In other cases, they are (at least ostensibly) for specific
purposes such as hunting, mourning, formal dress occasions, or Highland
dance competition; e.g., the MacFarlane <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Clan_MacFarlane> dress^[593] <#cite_note-673> and hunting tartans^[594]
<#cite_note-674> are different. (See § Tartans for specific purposes
<#Tartans_for_specific_purposes>, below.)

An important, more scholarly work was 1950's /The Setts of the Scottish
Tartans/ by Donald C. Stewart^[595] <#cite_note-675> ^[cc]
<#cite_note-677> (son of the aforementioned D. W. Stewart).^[388]
<#cite_note-Newsome_2005-435> The younger Stewart has been hailed as
"the founder of serious tartan research"; originated now-standard
methods for indexing tartans; and would go on to help expose the /
Vestiarium Scoticum/ as a fraud, in /Scotland's Forged Tartans/, co-
authored with J. Charles Thompson in 1980.^[388] <#cite_note-
Newsome_2005-435> ^[597] <#cite_note-678>

In the late 20th century to present, clan and other tartans also have
been catalogued in databases <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Database>.
(See § Registration <#Registration>, below.) A small number of new
official clan tartans (mostly specific-purpose "side" tartans, like
dance tartans) were registered in tartan databases in the 21st
century.^[cd] <#cite_note-680>

Regarding modern misrepresentations of clan tartans on historical
figures in films and even museums, Scarlett (1990) wrote: "so widely
have the tartan myths been spread that any script- or guide-book writer
will, in complete ignorance, write the most arrant nonsense and never
think that it might not be true. ... Once false information has been
disseminated by a supposedly authoritative body it is virtually
impossible to correct it."^[599] <#cite_note-681>


      Recognition by clan chiefs[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/
      index.php?title=Tartan&action=edit&section=21>]

The "officialness" of clan tartans has varied widely, and still does
today. Although it is possible for anyone to create a tartan and assign
it any name they wish, the only person with the authority to make a
clan's tartan "official" is the chief.^[388] <#cite_note-Newsome_2005-435>

Some clans have had no chiefs <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Armigerous_clan> for some time, while only a majority subset of those
with living chiefs in the modern era made direct proclamations as to
their clan tartans and registered them with the Lord Lyon <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lord_Lyon_King_of_Arms>.^[ce] <#cite_note-683>
Some time after the launch of the Scottish Register of Tartans <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_Register_of_Tartans> (SRT) in 2009, the
Lord Lyon stopped recording clan tartans, deferring to SRT for this
purpose. (See § Registration <#Registration>, below.) Some of the clan
tartans were simply adopted by custom,^[cf] <#cite_note-685> and have
remained rather consistent into the 21st century. A clan booth at a
Highland games <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highland_games> event is
likely to proudly display at least their best-known clan tartan,
regardless whether a chief has declared it official.

However, some chiefs have been quite adamant about what their clan's
legitimate tartans are. Ian Campbell, 12th Duke of Argyll <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ian_Campbell,_12th_Duke_of_Argyll>, chief of Clan
Campbell <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Campbell> in the late 20th
century, excoriated attempts to claim there were other than the four
aforementioned particular Campbell tartans (and specifically rejected
the personal-variant tartan of the 6th Duke).^[406] <#cite_note-
Ian_Campbell-454> Similarly, Sir Malcolm MacGregor <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MacGregor_baronets>, chief of Clan Gregor
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Gregor>, has written that only four
MacGregor tartans (plus a newer dance tartan) are legitimate, out of 10
or more alleged ones found in a tartan database, which he blamed on
"indiscriminate commercialisation ... disingenuous and lead[ing] to
confusion".^[602] <#cite_note-Malcolm_MacGregor-686>

In at least one instance, a clan tartan appears in the coat of arms
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coat_of_arms> of a clan chief and is
considered by the Lord Lyon as the "proper" tartan of the clan: The
crest of the chief of Clan MacLennan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Clan_MacLennan> is /A demi-piper all Proper <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Tincture_(heraldry)#Proper>, garbed in the proper tartan of the
Clan Maclennan/.^[603] <#cite_note-687> ^[cg] <#cite_note-688>

Some chief-authenticated clan tartans are quite late arrivals. In 1961,
the Clan Davidson <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Davidson> main
tartan was replaced (and registered with the Lord Lyon) by one of
multiple disputed chiefs, Sir David Davidson of Allt Dinnie, with a
design dating to 1893, in place of an older white-striped version.^[604]
<#cite_note-689> Chief Charles Shaw of Tordarroch <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Shaw_of_Tordarroch> in 1971 replaced the
old Shaw <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Shaw> tartan (a Black Watch
variant based on a misprinted image in Logan & McIan (1847))^[605]
<#cite_note-SRT_Shaw-690> with a new pair (dress^[606] <#cite_note-
SRT_Shaw_dress-691> and hunting)^[607] <#cite_note-SRT_Shaw_hunting-692>
designed in 1969 by D. C. Stewart based on more historical
sources.^[608] <#cite_note-693> Clan Mar <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Clan_Mar> had no approved tartan until Chief Margaret of Mar <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Margaret_of_Mar,_31st_Countess_of_Mar> registered
one in 1978 (from a design that may pre-date 1850);^[609]
<#cite_note-694> their dress/red tartan was not adopted until 1992 (from
a design dating to the 18th century).^[83] <#cite_note-SRT_Mar-96> The
MacLeod <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_MacLeod> red tartan was
approved by Chief John MacLeod of MacLeod <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/John_MacLeod_of_MacLeod> in 1982, to join much longer-standing
yellow and blue tartans of the clan; it was based loosely on what
appears in a 1748 portrait of Chief Norman MacLeod <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Norman_MacLeod_of_MacLeod> by Allan Ramsay
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allan_Ramsay_(artist)> and Joseph van
Aken <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph_van_Aken>.^[610]
<#cite_note-695> Baron David Lumsden of Cushnie-Lumsden <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Gordon_Allen_d%27Aldecamb_Lumsden> in 1996
approved the Clan Lumsden <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Lumsden>
hunting sett by Peter Eslea MacDonald^[611] <#cite_note-696> (though
technically the baron was just the chieftain of the Cushnie-Lumsden
branch). In 1998, Chief Dugald MacTavish of Dunardry <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_MacTavish#Revival> approved a 1958 design as
the MacTavish <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_MacTavish> dress
tartan.^[612] <#cite_note-SRT_MacTavish-697> In 2005, Chief Gillem
Lumsden of that Ilk registered a new main Lumsden <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Lumsden> tartan with the Lord Lyon,^[613]
<#cite_note-698> based closely on that of a c. 1790 Lumsden family
waistcoat.^[614] <#cite_note-699> Also in 2005, a pattern for Duncan of
Sketraw <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Duncan_of_Sketraw> was approved
by Chieftain John Duncan of Sketraw, based on a 1930s design.^[615]
<#cite_note-700> In 2007, Chief Fergus D. H. Macdowall of Garthland
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_MacDowall#Clan_chief> designed the
Clan MacDowall <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_MacDowall> tartan
(the clan previously used MacDougall <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Clan_MacDougall> or Galloway <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galloway>
district); he registered it with the Lord Lyon and Scottish Tartans
Authority <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_Tartans_Authority> in
2008.^[616] <#cite_note-701> ^[617] <#cite_note-702> The Cochrane
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Cochrane> hunting tartan was
designed personally by Chief Iain A. D. B. Cochrane, Earl of Dundonald
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earl_of_Dundonald>, in 2008.^[618]
<#cite_note-703> The Clan Carruthers <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Clan_Carruthers> tartan was approved by Chief Simon Peter Carruthers of
Holmains in 2017.^[619] <#cite_note-704>


    Modern general use[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
    title=Tartan&action=edit&section=22>]

Aside from regimental and clan usage, tartan has seen broad (and
sometimes highly politicised) use by the general public in the modern
era. By the 19th century, the Highland romantic revival <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celtic_Twilight>, inspired by James Macpherson
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Macpherson>'s "Ossian <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ossian>" poems and the writings of Sir Walter
Scott <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Walter_Scott>, led to wider
interest in tartan and other things felt to be Gaelic <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaels> and Celtic <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Celts_(modern)>. Clubs like the Celtic societies <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celtic_society> welcomed Lowlanders <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_Lowlands>, and tartan was rapidly
appropriated <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Cultural_appropriation>^[546] <#cite_note-MartinR_54-621> as part of the
Scottish national identity <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scottish_national_identity>^[620] <#cite_note-Milne_138-705> ^[621]
<#cite_note-706> (and part of broader British dress as a familiar
exoticism <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exoticism>).^[622]
<#cite_note-707> ^[623] <#cite_note-708>


      Late Georgian[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
      title=Tartan&action=edit&section=23>]

Illustration of Scottish regimental solidier in green tartan kilt, red
coat, and feather bonnet, with a lady in a blue tartan dress over a
white slip <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:The_New_Fashion,_or_The_Scotsman_in_Paris,_1815.jpg>"The New
Fashion, or The Scotsman in Paris", from a series of Parisian fashion
prints, 1815

The period of widened public interest in tartan and Highland dress after
the repeal of the Dress Act in 1782 has been called the *Highland
Revival*.^[33] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2022-37> ^[ch] <#cite_note-710>
While tartan had already seen more nationwide use from 1707, as a
Scottish nationalism <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scottish_nationalism> symbol against union with England <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707>,^[270] <#cite_note-
BdLC_75-309> it was turned on its ear to become a romanticised symbol of
union loyalism in the early 19th century,^[173] <#cite_note-
Spinning-197> ^[625] <#cite_note-711> an era in which prominent
conflicts caused a patriotic influence of military (including Highland)
style on civilian clothing,^[ci] <#cite_note-714> even among women^[340]
<#cite_note-Tuckett_10-11-385> ^[628] <#cite_note-715> despite its
overtly masculine focus.^[629] <#cite_note-McNeil_83-84-716> ^[630]
<#cite_note-717> ^[166] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2016-189> First among
the northern gentry and later among the common people more broadly,
there was a renewed interest in tartan and Highland dress, despite the
long period of prohibition – largely due to the glory associated with
the Highland regiments' exemplary service in various military
campaigns.^[631] <#cite_note-718> "Highlandism"^[632] <#cite_note-719>
became a romantic, mythologised (even fictionalised) and colourful
escapism <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Escapism>^[633] <#cite_note-720>
^[338] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_35-383> even as Lowland Scotland itself
was becoming one of the most industrialised <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Industrialisation> places on earth, and the entire nation was
undergoing the social upheavals of union and empire <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Empire>, of large-scale warfare, of
urbanisation, and of modernisation during the Scottish Enlightenment
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_Enlightenment>.^[634]
<#cite_note-721> The bloody French Revolution <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/French_Revolution> of 1789–1799 had also helped inspire a British
setting aside of old Stuart <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
House_of_Stuart> and Hanoverian <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
House_of_Hanover> rivalry.^[635] <#cite_note-722>

Before the clan tartans rush began in 1815, tartan was already being
aggressively marketed to the general public as "fancy" cloth with names
that commemorated famous events and people, even fictional characters
from books and songs, e.g. "Waterloo <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Battle_of_Waterloo>", "Flora MacDonald <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Flora_MacDonald>", "Sir Walter Scott", "Wellington <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arthur_Wellesley,_1st_Duke_of_Wellington>",
"Maggie Lauder <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Francis_Sempill>", and
"Meg Merrilees <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Guy_Mannering#Characters>". This inspired a novel perception that
tartans should be named.^[636] <#cite_note-723> Some of the designs by
leading weaver Wilsons of Bannockburn by this period were considered
recognisable on sight.^[637] <#cite_note-724>

In 1822, Maj.-Gen. David Stewart of Garth <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/David_Stewart_of_Garth>, who was with both the Highland Society of
London <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highland_Society_of_London> and
the Celtic Society of Edinburgh <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
title=Celtic_Society_of_Edinburgh&action=edit&redlink=1>,^[638]
<#cite_note-725> ^[521] <#cite_note-Trevor-Roper_28-29-592> published /
Sketches of the Character, Manners, and Present State of the Highlanders
of Scotland/, the first of a number of 19th-century books lionising the
Highlanders, the clans, and the tartaned regiments.^[629] <#cite_note-
McNeil_83-84-716> ^[528] <#cite_note-Armstrong_37-600> The various
Celtic/Highland societies throughout Britain had already been driving a
rise in tartan demand since the late 18th century.^[639]
<#cite_note-726> ^[364] <#cite_note-Hinderks_Dziennik_8-409> The
societies liked wearing Highland dress – in their own assimilated, urban
idiom,^[cj] <#cite_note-727> such as tartan frock coats <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frock_coat>^[344] <#cite_note-
EsleaMacDonald_2020-10-389>  – and devising new tartans; it has been
suggested that they were engaging in a sort of "internal colonisation",
imposing what they wanted to see rather than simply recording what was
traditionally Highland.^[640] <#cite_note-728> Aside from tartan
fabric's increasing use in non-Highland styles of clothing, Highland
dress itself had already become highly stylized, quite removed from the
simplicity of its peasant origins;^[641] <#cite_note-BdLC_99-100-729>
this was a trend that would continue throughout the later Victorian period.


        The King's jaunt in tartan[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/
        index.php?title=Tartan&action=edit&section=24>]

<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:George_IV_in_kilt,_by_Wilkie.jpg>David Wilkie <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Wilkie_(artist)>'s idealised depiction of
George IV <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_IV>, in full Highland
dress <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highland_dress>, during the visit
to Scotland in 1822^[ck] <#cite_note-731>

The popularity of tartan was greatly increased by the royal visit of
King George IV <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Visit_of_George_IV_to_Scotland> of the United Kingdom to Edinburgh in
1822, with other nobles including Lord Mayor of London <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lord_Mayor_of_London> Sir William Curtis <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sir_William_Curtis,_1st_Baronet>,^[643]
<#cite_note-732> in Highland garb. George was the first reigning monarch
to visit Scotland in 171 years.^[543] <#cite_note-Moncreiffe_24-618> The
pageantry invented for the event, which was nicknamed "the King's
Jaunt", brought a sudden consumer-driven demand for tartan cloth^[492]
<#cite_note-Tuckett_17-560> and made it the national dress <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_dress> of the whole of Scotland.^[543]
<#cite_note-Moncreiffe_24-618> ^[644] <#cite_note-Tuckett_18-733> ^[620]
<#cite_note-Milne_138-705> ^[645] <#cite_note-734> The 21 days of
festivities were organised by the Jacobitism-romanticising but staunchly
unionist^[173] <#cite_note-Spinning-197> Walter Scott, who was another
co-founder of the Celtic Society of Edinburgh, and British military man
Stewart of Garth.^[519] <#cite_note-TBW_Garth-590> They urged Scots
(most of whom were Lowlanders) to attend "all plaided and plumed in
their tartan array"^[646] <#cite_note-Magnusson_653-654-735> in
"complete national costume".^[644] <#cite_note-Tuckett_18-733> One
contemporary writer sarcastically described the pomp that surrounded the
celebrations as "Sir Walter's Celtified Pageantry",^[646] <#cite_note-
Magnusson_653-654-735> ^[647] <#cite_note-736> and another as a "plaided
panorama".^[644] <#cite_note-Tuckett_18-733> Clan chiefs, expected to be
kilted, had little choice but to take the event seriously, and arrived
to show their loyalty in something of a panic, with tartaned retinues of
half a dozen^[265] <#cite_note-BanksdeLaChapell_100-301> to up to 50 per
clan^[648] <#cite_note-BBC_Brown-737> (equipped at great expense, and
with only about a month's official notice), in a city overflowing with
Highlanders, Lowlanders, and English spectators decked in tartan,^[649]
<#cite_note-738> ^[493] <#cite_note-Trevor-Roper_30-561> a sight that
Scott's own son-in-law and biographer John Gibson Lockhart <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Gibson_Lockhart> called a "Celtic
hallucination".^[491] <#cite_note-BdLC_104-558> Thousands of spectators
attended the many events arranged for the visit.^[265] <#cite_note-
BanksdeLaChapell_100-301> The formal ball, reserved for the gentry,
required Highland dress for admittance, and some 300 tailors were
employed to supply it.^[648] <#cite_note-BBC_Brown-737>

The royal endorsement of tartan and Highland-wear did much to erase any
lingering association of them with the servile peasant class of the
Highlands^[650] <#cite_note-739> (or the region's bands of mountain
bandits, for that matter).^[648] <#cite_note-BBC_Brown-737> Because
Scott had become "the acknowledged preserver of Scotland's past" through
his historical novels, the legend he helped create of tartan and
Highland dress as a Scotland-wide tradition rooted in antiquity was
widely and quickly accepted, despite its ignoring and erasing of
cultural diversity within the country^[641] <#cite_note-BdLC_99-100-729>
(of Gaels, Norse–Gaels <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Norse%E2%80%93Gaels>, Scoto-Normans <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scoto-Norman>, and Lowlanders of largely Anglo-Saxon <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northumbrian_Old_English> extraction). "A bogus
tartan caricature of [Scotland] had been drawn and accepted, even by
those who mocked it, and it would develop in perspective and
colour."^[651] <#cite_note-740> George IV's visit – which was not just
theatrical but thoroughly political, in marrying Hanoverian power and
loyalty to Stuart ideology and pride^[652] <#cite_note-741>  – has been
described in by Angus Calder <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Angus_Calder> (1994) as the catalyst by which "a Union of practical
convenience became a Union of irrational love and fears, sublimated in
militarism, tartanry, royalism and, eventually imperialism".^[653]
<#cite_note-742> R. Martin (1988) added: "it would seem that this visit
presages the acts of orchestrated political propaganda that we have come
to know very well in the 20th century."^[546] <#cite_note-MartinR_54-621>

Young man in a black coat, trousers, and boots, a white cravat, and a
voluminous tartan cloak with a red lining, holding a gentleman's cane
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:John_Crichton-
Stuart_(1793%E2%80%931848),_2nd_Marquess_of_Bute,_by_Henry_Raeburn,_c._1829.jpg>Portrait of John Crichton-Stuart, 2nd Marquess of Bute <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Crichton-Stuart,_2nd_Marquess_of_Bute>, by Henry Raeburn <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Henry_Raeburn>, c. 1829, showing adaptation of tartan to Regency-era <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regency_era> clothing styles, like this red-lined cloak

Following the royal visit, the tartan industry boomed,^[654]
<#cite_note-743> and the number of available tartans increased
tenfold;^[655] <#cite_note-Armstrong_54-744> in 1822, Wilsons' pattern
book had numbered setts in the hundreds, and introduced many more with
proper names.^[503] <#cite_note-DunbarJD1979_149-571> Scarlett (1990)
writes that "Tartan was no longer the dress of northern barbarians or
political dissidents; it had become respectable and the garb of loyal
subjects."^[656] <#cite_note-745> Books which documented tartans began
to appear and added to the "tartanry <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Tartanry>" craze. James Logan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
James_Logan_(writer)>'s romanticised work^[556] <#cite_note-
Armstrong_60-61-632> /The Scottish Gaël/ (1831) was the first such
publication, and led the weaving industry to adopt new patterns, even
Logan's invented or erroneous ones.^[555] <#cite_note-BdLC_106-108-631>

The result of these flurries of attention has been described as an
"astonishing frenzy of excitement into which [patronage of tartanry]
threw the citizens of Edinburgh and much of the rest of Scotland".^[657]
<#cite_note-746>


        Civilian spread[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
        title=Tartan&action=edit&section=25>]

From the 1820s, Georgian and then Victorian portraiture of clan nobles
continued the earlier theme of regimentally re-styled Highland dress,
with jewels, gold, and other symbols of aristocracy – a "synthetic
Gaelicism".^[658] <#cite_note-747> The funerals of Sir John Macgregor
Murray <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Sir_John_Macgregor_Murray,_1st_Baronet> and Alasdair Ranaldson Macdonell
of Glengarry <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Alexander_Ranaldson_Macdonell>, in 1822 and 1823 respectively, were
marked by tartan, bagpipes <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Great_Highland_bagpipe>, and "wailing" of clansmen – "a feudal sight in
an increasingly industrial age".^[659] <#cite_note-748> A large public
tartan affair was the 1824 Atholl Gathering <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Atholl_Gathering>^[548] <#cite_note-Armstrong_148-623> (an annual
event that, after a period of abeyance, continues to the present). From
the end of proscription through the Georgian promotion, "distrust of the
Highlands became fascination",^[660] <#cite_note-749> and tartan and
Highland garb "moved from the periphery to the very center, accompanied
by all the processes of forgetting and imaginative re-creation".^[661]
<#cite_note-750> Tartan, no longer the everyday traditional dress of
Highland "barbarians", had become, in altered form, all the rage among
the Scottish upper and even middle classes as formal attire.^[662]
<#cite_note-751> This popularisation of tartan increased its
marketability in the Lowlands, in England, and in the colonies, and
provided a boost to the Scottish textile industry.^[331] <#cite_note-
Hinderks_Dziennik_127-129-376>

Illustration of tartan dresses in French style of the era, frilly and
with no relation to Scottish garb <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Costumes_Parisiens_1826.jpg>French tartan fashions from /Costumes
Parisiens/, 1826

Tartan had begun making appearances in civilian Georgian fashion
throughout Britain and into continental Europe, as illustrated in
publications such as London's /Gallery of Fashion <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gallery_of_Fashion>/ (1787) and /La Belle
Assemblée <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/La_Belle_Assembl%C3%A9e>/
(1808), and (after Paris was famously occupied by Highland regiments
during the Waterloo campaign <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Waterloo_campaign#Invasion_of_France_and_the_occupation_of_Paris_(18_June_%E2%80%93_7_July)> and the fall of Napoleon <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Napoleon> in 1815)^[663] <#cite_note-752> ^[629] <#cite_note-McNeil_83-84-716> ^[664] <#cite_note-753> in the French periodicals /Le Prétexte/ (1815)^[665] <#cite_note-754> and /Costumes Parisiens/ (1826); tartan was in vogue in Paris in particular
