in this period,^[666] <#cite_note-755> ^[667] <#cite_note-756> and approximations of Highland soldiers even appeared in Parisian plays at the time.^[668] <#cite_note-757> Tartans associated with family names became popular, but there was also a brisk trade in new tartans commissioned for societies, to commemorate events, in honour of famous persons, and designed simply to personal aesthetic taste.^[669] <#cite_note-758> Manufacturers struggled to keep up with demand.^[670] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_150-759> ^[620] <#cite_note-Milne_138-705> By 1819, dominant tartan weaver Wilsons of Bannockburn^[329] <#cite_note-Tuckett_19-374> (also a carpet and ribbon weaver)^[671] <#cite_note-760> was keenly interested in exploiting the civilian market, due to a reduction in regimental demand, and introduced many more patterns, providing cloth in various grades.^[672] <#cite_note-761> By 1820, the company had access to 132 looms <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Loom>;^[673] <#cite_note-Tuckett_7-9-762> they experienced a four-fold increase in output in 1821, leading up to George IV's visit,^[644] <#cite_note-Tuckett_18-733> after which they acquired 40 more looms^[494] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2012_5-562> ^[655] <#cite_note-Armstrong_54-744> in an add-on building,^[670] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_150-759> named the Royal George after the king,^[494] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2012_5-562> and expanded into a new mill in 1822, mechanising <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Power_loom> more and more to keep up with demand.^[673] <#cite_note-Tuckett_7-9-762> They stopped weaving muslin <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muslin> to focus on tartan,^[670] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_150-759> and produced it in a range of qualities from finest merino <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Merino> wool to cheap linsey-woolsey <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linsey-woolsey> blends, demonstrating that whatever high-class associations tartan had taken on, there was significant working-class demand.^[674] <#cite_note-Tuckett_20-763> In 1829, a merchant wrote to Wilsons that "We are like to be torn to pieces for tartan; the demand is so great we cannot supply our customers", and there was great demand for the newest patterns.^[558] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_151-634>

Colour illustration of women in Victorian dresses working power looms in
a large textile factory <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:V%C3%A6vesal_i_Rubens_Dampv%C3%A6veri.jpg>Illustration of Victorian
women weaving at power looms in a textile factory (this one in Denmark,
but the scene in Wilsons of Bannockburn at its peak would have been very
similar).

Georgian and later Victorian entrepreneurs not only created new tartans,
but new tartan objects called /tartanware/, starting as far back as the
proscription period in the form of wine glasses decorated with tartan
and enamel Jacobite portraits.^[302] <#cite_note-BdLC_84-346> Tartan
decorated an assortment of common household objects, such as snuffboxes
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decorative_box#Snuff_box>, jewellery
cases, tableware, sewing accessories, desk items, and even doorknobs and
furniture – a tartan knick-knack market for tourists that continues
through the present in the Highlands.^[675] <#cite_note-764> Visitors to
the Highlands went home with tartanware, and Scotland-based businesses
sent tartanware out as gifts to customers. Some of the more popular
tartans used were the Stewart <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Clan_Stewart>, MacDonald <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Donald>,
MacGregor <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Gregor>, MacDuff <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_MacDuff>, MacBeth <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Clan_MacBeth>, and one fancifully named "Prince Charlie".^[676]
<#cite_note-765> ^[677] <#cite_note-766> ^[678] <#cite_note-767> Today,
tartanware is widely collected in England and Scotland.^[679]
<#cite_note-768> There was a symbiotic relationship between tartanware
production and interest in tartans generated by books on the subject: a
tartanware manufacturer from 1820 onward was W. & A. Smith, of Mauchline
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mauchline>, also incidentally the
publishers of /Authenticated Tartans of the Clans and Families of
Scotland/ (1850).;^[680] <#cite_note-769> tartanware was sometimes more
specifically called Mauchlinware.^[681] <#cite_note-MartinR_57-770>


      Victorian[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
      title=Tartan&action=edit&section=26>]

Leading up to the beginning of Queen Victoria <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Queen_Victoria>'s reign in 1837, tartan was a brisk trade in
London, Manchester, and other English cities and towns.^[682]
<#cite_note-771> In 1839, the Eglinton Tournament <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eglinton_Tournament>, a medieval re-enactment
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Historical_reenactment> featuring
jousting <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jousting> and a ball <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ball_(dance_party)>, was organised in North
Ayrshire <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_Ayrshire> by Archibald
Montgomerie, Earl of Eglinton <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Archibald_Montgomerie,_13th_Earl_of_Eglinton>; it drew some 100,000
spectators, who had been asked to attend in plaids, and included George
Murray, Duke of Atholl <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
George_Murray,_6th_Duke_of_Atholl>, arriving with an entire regiment in
tartan, his newly re-formed Atholl Highlanders <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atholl_Highlanders>^[683] <#cite_note-772> (which
still exists as Europe's last remaining private military force).

A Scottish Highlands hunting scene with a lord, a lady, a child, a
gillie (all in tartan), two dogs, a horse, and two slain deer <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Scene_in_the_Highlands_with_Portraits_of_the_Duchess_of_Bedford_and_Duke_of_Gordon,_by_Edwin_Landseer_1825.jpg>/Scene in the Highlands with Portraits of the Duchess of Bedford and Duke of Gordon/ (in various tartans), by Edwin Landseer <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edwin_Landseer>, 1825. The Highlands were being cleared <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highland_Clearances> of native people, for deer hunting preserves and sheep pastures


        /Vestiarium Scoticum/[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/
        index.php?title=Tartan&action=edit&section=27>]

The first publication showing colour plates of an array of tartans was
the /Vestiarium Scoticum <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Vestiarium_Scoticum>/ (meaning 'wardrobe of the Scots'), published in
1842,^[565] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_history-642> and it included a
first: tartans for Lowland families. It was the work of two brothers:
John Carter Allen and Charles Manning Allen, from Surrey <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surrey>, England, who used a variety of assumed
names. The two implied they were grandsons of Prince Charles Edward
Stuart <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Edward_Stuart> and
Princess Louise of Stolberg-Gedern <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Princess_Louise_of_Stolberg-Gedern>, and consequently later became known
as the "Sobieski Stuarts <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Sobieski_Stuarts>". They claimed further that the /Vestiarium/ was based
on a 1571 manuscript on clan tartans – a manuscript which they never
managed to produce. It was not known at the time, but many of the
tartans were simply invented by the brothers, and others were taken from
early-19th-century sources like the Cockburn and Wilson
collections.^[684] <#cite_note-773> ^[388] <#cite_note-Newsome_2005-435>
The brothers heavily favoured basic checks, or crudely divided checks,
with thin over-checks added; they had an identifiable style of tartans,
assessment of which has varied from "few can be called inspired"^[51]
<#cite_note-Scarlett1990_47-62> to "quite novel and singularly
gorgeous".^[685] <#cite_note-774> The /Vestiarium/ was followed by their
equally dubious /The Costume of the Clans/ in 1845.^[555] <#cite_note-
BdLC_106-108-631> The books, which "added mystery, romance and some
spurious historical documentation to the subject",^[561] <#cite_note-
Armstrong_61-637> triggered another wave of interest in tartans, and the
enthusiasm generated by these publications led the way for numerous
tartan books in the later 19th century.^[646] <#cite_note-
Magnusson_653-654-735> ^[565] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_history-642>
^[cl] <#cite_note-775>

The sudden availability (and almost unquestioning acceptance) of Lowland
tartans helped spread tartan further in popularity. "The [tartan] cult
was gathering strength and tartan was no longer 'Highland', it had
become 'Scottish'."^[588] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_37-667>


        The Queen and "Balmorality"[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/
        index.php?title=Tartan&action=edit&section=28>]

Blue and yellow tartan dress with a trailing bustle, high collar, and a
long series of buttons down the entire front <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/File:Young_Woman%27s_Dress_LACMA_M.2000.178.16.jpg>A silk and
velvet late-Victorian young woman's tartan dress, 1878, probably made in
England

Twenty years after her uncle's royal visit to Scotland, Victoria and her
husband Prince Albert <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Albert,_Prince_Consort> made their first trip to the Scottish Highlands
in 1842; she was the first monarch to set foot in the Highlands since
Mary, Queen of Scots <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Mary,_Queen_of_Scots>, in the 16th century.^[686] <#cite_note-776> The
visit involved her large royal party being met with several theatrical
tartan-kilted welcomes by Highland nobility and their retinues, with
much sycophantic newspaper fanfare (while the common people were
experiencing considerable misery); the Queen wrote: "It seemed as if a
great chieftain in olden feudal times was receiving his
sovereign".^[687] <#cite_note-777> The monarch's early trips to Scotland
were seen as a royal endorsement and had a transformative effect on the
image of the country, as a now-loyal land of tartan, pipers <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Highland_bagpipe>, and kilted martial
display.^[688] <#cite_note-778>

Victoria and Albert leased Balmoral Castle <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Balmoral_Castle>, in Aberdeenshire <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Aberdeenshire>, in 1848 (and bought it in 1852)^[689] <#cite_note-
EsleaMacDonald_2019-06-779> as a private royal demesne <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Demesne> and hired a local architect to re-model
the estate in feudalised Scots baronial <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scottish_baronial_architecture> style, starting a "sham-castles"
trend.^[690] <#cite_note-780> Prince Albert personally took care of the
interior design, where he made great use of tartan. He used the royal
Stewart (red) and the hunting Stewart (green) tartans for carpets, while
using the dress Stewart (red and white) for curtains and
upholstery.^[566] <#cite_note-BdLC_108-643> Prince Albert (who often
wore the kilt at Balmoral) is said to have created the Balmoral tartan,
still used as a royal tartan today.^[691] <#cite_note-781> (See
illustration at § Family and individual <#Family_and_individual>,
below.) They even decorated their carriage <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Carriage> with tartan.^[692] <#cite_note-782> ^[693]
<#cite_note-783> Their adoption of a showy form of Highland dress
inspired adoption by subject "who would have previously left Highland
dress to the festivals of the Scots."^[681] <#cite_note-MartinR_57-770>

The royal couple spent a considerable amount of time at their Scottish
estate (nearly 7 years in total),^[694] <#cite_note-784> ^[cm]
<#cite_note-786> and in doing so hosted "Highland" activities. Victoria
was attended by pipers,^[696] <#cite_note-Armstrong_207-787> and her
children were attired in Highland dress.^[697] <#cite_note-
Armstrong_101-788> Prince Albert himself loved watching the Highland
games <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highland_games>^[698] <#cite_note-
BdLC_108-109-789> and the pair became patrons of the Braemar Gathering
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Braemar#Braemar_Gathering>.^[699]
<#cite_note-790> (Support from and attendance by various nobles may have
helped preserve such events to the present, but it also "tartanised"
them permanently, all the way into the 21st century.)^[700]
<#cite_note-791> The royal enthusiasm for and patronage of Highland
things generated more early tourism <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Tourism#Emergence_of_leisure_travel> to the Highlands,^[576]
<#cite_note-BdLC_26-654> ^[701] <#cite_note-792> ^[cn] <#cite_note-794>
and a boost to business in the region as far as Perth <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perth,_Scotland> and Edinburgh <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edinburgh>.^[703] <#cite_note-795> It also spread
tartan-wearing to other northern British lords and ladies, who began to
invent complicated etiquette rules of dress for Highland garb, which had
the effect of increasing the sense that it was upper-class attire.^[173]
<#cite_note-Spinning-197> ^[704] <#cite_note-796> ^[588] <#cite_note-
Scarlett1990_37-667> (See § Etiquette <#Etiquette>, below.) Adoption of
tartan continued to spread into England; Thomas Osborne, Duke of Leeds
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Osborne,_4th_Duke_of_Leeds>, in
West Yorkshire <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Yorkshire>, devised a
livery <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Livery> tartan for his men in
1848.^[705] <#cite_note-797> Tartan, though a "pseudo-Caledonian
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caledonia> masquerade",^[706]
<#cite_note-798> had become "the stuff of loyalty to the crown",^[707]
<#cite_note-799> with "a spurious royal and aristocratic cachet".^[708]
<#cite_note-800> This royal promotion was also noted abroad, with the
effect that tartan became one of the widest-recognised cultural-identity
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cultural_identity> symbols for the entire
British country.^[709] <#cite_note-801>

Despite their considerable devotion to charity (up to 20% of their Privy
Purse <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Privy_Purse> income),^[710]
<#cite_note-802> Victoria and Albert, along with their friends in the
northern gentry, have been accused of using their "Balmorality" – a term
coined by George Scott-Moncrieff <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
George_Scott-Moncrieff> (1932) to refer to upper-class appropriation of
Highland cultural trappings, marked by "hypocrisy" and "false
sentiment" – to trivialise and even fictionalise history.^[711]
<#cite_note-Armstrong_Balmorality-803> According to Fiona K. Armstrong
(2017), they engaged in long-term, tartan-blanketed escapism from the
uncertainties of modernising, industrialised society and from pressing
British societal problems, while worsening those problems in the actual
Highlands.^[711] <#cite_note-Armstrong_Balmorality-803> The queen's
Balmoral residency also had a colonising effect, leading other English
nobility to follow her north and buy property, resulting in land-
ownership disparities that persist to the present.^[712]
<#cite_note-804> The Highlands during Victoria's reign also became more
accessible by road, rail, and boat.^[698] <#cite_note-BdLC_108-109-789>

Mannequin modelling a green and blue tartan suit, with a bowler derby
hat and black shoes <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Man%27s_two-
piece_lounge_suit_1875-1880.jpg>A late Victorian style, this two-piece
tartan suit dates to about 1875–1880

As the tartan and "romantic Highlands" craze swept over Scotland, the
real Highland population suffered grievously from the Hungry Forties
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hungry_Forties> as well as the Highland
Clearances <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highland_Clearances>, when
thousands of Gaelic-speaking Scots from the Highlands and Isles were
evicted by landlords (often the very men who would have been their clan
chiefs) to make way for sheep^[646] <#cite_note-Magnusson_653-654-735>
^[713] <#cite_note-805> and for expansive deer-hunting preserves.^[714]
<#cite_note-806> Scots were also largely disenfranchised from voting,
and the Highlands were running out of young men, in great regimental
demand to fight and die in foreign wars for the empire, and many
emigrating otherwise,^[715] <#cite_note-807> with Victoria and Albert
directly patronising emigration societies <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Highland_and_Island_Emigration_Society>.^[716] <#cite_note-808>
Nearly 2 million Scots moved to non-European destinations during the
Victorian era (more than half the native-born Scottish people of the
period), and took a measure of Highlandism with them^[717]
<#cite_note-809>  – "many of the generally understood images of the
Highlands were held to be 'real' by people at the time".^[718]
<#cite_note-810> This would have strong tartan-promoting results among
the Scottish diaspora <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_diaspora>
later;^[719] <#cite_note-811> Scarlett (1990) calls it a "tartan hunger
that has been abroad from late Victorian times to the present
day".^[720] <#cite_note-812>


        Ripple effects[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
        title=Tartan&action=edit&section=29>]

Thomas Babington Macaulay <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Thomas_Babington_Macaulay> wrote in 1848 of the romantic reinvention of
Highland customs as somehow /generally/ Scottish: "Soon the vulgar
imagination was so completely occupied by plaids <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Belted_plaid>, targets <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Targe>, and claymores <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Claymore>,
that, by most Englishmen, /Scotchman/ and /Highlander/ were regarded as
synonymous words."^[721] <#cite_note-813> In 1849, Sir John Graham
Dalyell <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Graham_Dalyell> asserted
that "forty years ago no reputable gentleman would have appeared in a
kilt in the streets of Edinburgh."^[722] <#cite_note-814> Scott-
Moncrieff (1932) likewise wrote of tartans being "misconceived" and worn
all over Scotland (and even England) in the Victorian era as a part of
the Queen's influence.^[723] <#cite_note-815> Increasingly-urban
Scotland was putting on a "rural face"^[724] <#cite_note-816> (a trend
that would continue with "kailyard <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Kailyard_school>" literature). Tartanry and Highlandism were popular in
part as a counter to a sense (especially among the aristocracy) that
Scotland was losing its separate national identity in the Georgian to
Victorian era, being ever more Anglicised <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Anglicisation> as just "North Britain" amid empire-wide
modernisation.^[725] <#cite_note-817>

Four men in kilts with widely divergent tartans, sporting a variety of
headgear, jackets, plaids, sporrans, and other accessories <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Kenneth_MacLeay_-
_John_MacLachlan,_Hugh_Graham,_James_MacFarlane_and_Angus_Colquhoun_(1866).jpg>Kenneth MacLeay <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kenneth_MacLeay>'s 1866 portrait of a MacLachlan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Maclachlan>, a Graham <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Graham>, a MacFarlane <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_MacFarlane>, and a Colquhoun <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Colquhoun>, for Victoria's /Highlanders of Scotland/ book project.

In an 1849 letter to a publisher about a planned second edition of /
Vestiarium Scoticum/, John Sobieski Stuart noted that tartan had become
"extensively worn and manufactured" on the continent, as far away as
France, Germany, Bohemia <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bohemia>, and
Hungary; he also expressed an interest in working directly with
tartanware and tartan book makers W. & A. Smith of Mauchline.^[726]
<#cite_note-818> The same year, the Duke and Duchess of Atholl <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anne_Murray,_Duchess_of_Atholl> (whose entire
estate was prescribed tartan livery)^[727] <#cite_note-819> hosted a
Highland-dress affair in London, the Royal Caledonian Ball <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Caledonian_Ball>, the first known charity
ball (still a sold-out annual event today).^[728] <#cite_note-820> The
1859 opening of the massive Loch Katrine <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Loch_Katrine> waterworks (to pump fresh water to Glasgow <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glasgow>, running out of well <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Well> water) was attended by Queen Victoria, with
the Atholl Highlanders (cannon in tow), the Celtic Society of Glasgow,
and an honour-guard <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guard_of_honour> unit
called the Glasgow Volunteers putting on a tartan- and piper-laden
display for the newspapers; it was a confluence of modern engineering
and romantic–patriotic tartanry.^[729] <#cite_note-821> When the Prince
Consort died in 1861, Victoria commissioned a tartan-kilted statue of
Albert at Balmoral by William Theed <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
William_Theed>.^[730] <#cite_note-822>

According to Jonathan Faiers (2008), Victoria had actually intentionally
made tartan more popular for the benefit of the British textile
industry.^[731] <#cite_note-823> By the 1860s, tartan was not only as
popular in London as in Scotland,^[732] <#cite_note-MartinR_58-824>
leading weaver Wilsons of Bannockburn produced £80,000 of product per
year, and employed 500–600 people. (It amalgamated with another of the
family businesses, a carpet-weaving operation, in 1867, which continued
to 1924.)^[264] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_144-300> Around 1860, new
synthetic aniline dyes <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Synthetic_colorant> allowed for production of tartans in vivid colours
at more affordable prices, and their lower cost translated into more
consumption of tartan by the middle class.^[696] <#cite_note-
Armstrong_207-787>

<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Tartan_Ribbon.jpg>The first
permanent colour photograph, by Thomas Sutton <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Thomas_Sutton_(photographer)> in 1861, was of a tartan ribbon.

As modernisation marched on, the world's first permanent colour
photograph <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Color_photography>, taken by
Thomas Sutton <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Thomas_Sutton_(photographer)> (using the three-colour process developed
by Scottish physicist James Clerk Maxwell <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell>) in 1861, was of a tartan ribbon.^[733]
<#cite_note-825> It was created by using red, blue, and yellow filters
to create three photographs which were then combined into a composite.
R. Martin (1988) notes that there was a confluence of unrelated
technological "junctions and serendipities" in the mid-19th century that
together broadly promoted tartan, including photography, consistently
bright and more economical artificial dyes, affordable colour book
printing <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Color_printing#Europe>, mass-
production of soft but durable fine textiles, and applicability of
printed patterns to middle-class products like tartanware – all "far-
removed from the true peasant history of tartan."^[734] <#cite_note-826>
Ian Brown (2012), a professor with a focus on Scottish literature and
culture, has written that while George IV and Victoria (not to mention
business interests in their wake, like the Wilsons of Bannockburn and
the Smiths of Mauchline) seemed to have been "the winner taking over the
loser's tokens", the renewed public interest in tartan within and beyond
Scotland was not entirely owing to them, especially given the
international interest in Highland-romantic works of Walter Scott and
"Ossian". The acceptance of and even enthusiasm for tartan among the
post-proscription upper class can be seen as a necessary attempt at
reconciliation within a culturally diverse country, and the influence
ran both ways, with old Scottish nationalism transmuting into a new
unionism that demanded recognition of Scottish interests and
institutions. "In short, it is an open question whether George IV in a
kilt and Victoria and Albert at Balmoral are appropriating and
subverting a set of values, or whether they are being appropriated and
subverted."^[735] <#cite_note-827> Even the 1822 "King's Jaunt" had been
stage-managed by two Scots with a keen interest in romanticising and
promoting Gaelic and broader Scottish culture (historico-traditional
accuracy notwithstanding),^[641] <#cite_note-BdLC_99-100-729> and the
Atholls' deep and tartan-arrayed involvement in Victoria's activities in
the north can be viewed in the same light.^[736] <#cite_note-828> Both
George IV^[737] <#cite_note-829> ^[738] <#cite_note-830> ^[739]
<#cite_note-831> and Victoria,^[740] <#cite_note-832> primarily of
German House of Hanover <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/House_of_Hanover>
stock, came to identify strongly with their quite thin Scottish House of
Stuart <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/House_of_Stuart> genealogy.

Prince Arthur with his hand on a chair, and dressed in an elaborate
tartan costume <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Prince_Arthur_as_Bonnie_Prince_Charlie.jpg>Prince Arthur <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prince_Arthur,_Duke_of_Connaught_and_Strathearn>
dressed up as Bonnie Prince Charlie <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Charles_Edward_Stuart> for the 1871 Waverley Ball

The 1863 funeral of the Duke of Atholl was another anachronistically
feudal, tartan-and-pipers pageant.^[741] <#cite_note-833> In 1866–1870,
Victoria and the Duchess of Atholl commissioned artist Kenneth MacLeay
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kenneth_MacLeay> in Edinburgh to produce
a series of watercolours <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Watercolour> of
statuesque men in tartan Highland gear, representing common people from
ghillies <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghillie> to shepherds and
fishermen, "as they /now/ are". Prints were published in 1870 as /
Highlanders of Scotland: Portraits Illustrative of the Principal Clans
and Followings, and the Retainers of the Royal Household at Balmoral/,
with text by Amelia (Emily) Murray MacGregor, an attendant of Victoria
as well as a Clan Gregor <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Gregor>
historian and the first female Gaelic lecturer. A tartanistical fantasy,
as well as another exercise in "Highlander as noble savage <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Noble_savage>", the art book necessitated
canvassing Scottish aristocrats for outfits and suitable models
("specimens"), as the everyday people did not look the hyper-masculine
part, were not able to afford such Highland-dress extravagances as were
to be illustrated, and were more likely to be wearing trousers than
kilts.^[742] <#cite_note-834> The resulting book is the most detailed
record of the "proper", codified Victorian-era Highland dress and
accessories, which "removed tartan from its blustery nonchalance to an
ordered set of adornments"^[732] <#cite_note-MartinR_58-824>  – most of
which survive to the present, Highland dress being remarkably resistant
to further major stylistic changes, Victorian styles having become
"traditional". Tartan had also become more established throughout the
1850s and 1860s as a textile for European-fashionable rather than
Highland women's clothing, from bodices <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Bodice> and dresses to sashes <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sash> and
shawls (the never-extinguished ladies' plaids).^[732] <#cite_note-
MartinR_58-824> The tartan sash in particular was a favourite of the
Queen,^[732] <#cite_note-MartinR_58-824> and remains a common womenswear
option, worn several different ways in modern Highland dress,^[743]
<#cite_note-835> though it has little to do with original Highland
clothing before the 19th century; it is an adaptation of the plaid to a
style of the European nobility.

In 1871, at the Waverley Ball, a fancy dress <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Costume_party#Nineteenth_century> affair in London, the Prince of
Wales (the future King Edward VII <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Edward_VII>) and his brother Prince Arthur <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Prince_Arthur,_Duke_of_Connaught_and_Strathearn>, long accustomed
to Highland dress, arrived tartaned out as an old-time Lord of the Isles
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lord_of_the_Isles> and as Bonnie Prince
Charlie <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Edward_Stuart>,
respectively.^[744] <#cite_note-836> In 1872, ethnologist <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethnology> Jacob Falke wrote that "In Scotland
indeed the plaid has still some importance, but it is an object of
manufacture, and ... its motives have long ago become the common
property of fashion, and indeed have become so permeated by it that what
is genuine and old in it is scarcely to be recognised".^[745]
<#cite_note-837> Since its 1880 re-opening, the Gaelic Society of Perth
in the Lowlands held festivities that involved much piping and tartan-
wear, into the early 20th century, despite the language-preservation
organisation having nothing to do with Highland dress or /pibroch
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pibroch>/; being swathed in tartan had
somehow become vital to such events.^[746] <#cite_note-838> By 1883,
Highland dress as proper courtly <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Royal_court> attire had become highly regulated, aristocratic, and
formal, but "inclusive" in one sense – the tartan-wear was permitted at
court for essentially anyone claiming Highland origins or land-ownership
(even if natively English), not just the gentles of the well-established
clans.^[747] <#cite_note-839>

In the Victorian era, tartan garments for women as well as men continued
to be featured in fashion catalogues, in styles not derived from
Highland costume, such as everyday suits and dresses.^[576] <#cite_note-
BdLC_26-654> Tartan had also become popular for children's clothing in
continental Europe,^[748] <#cite_note-840> inspired by the royal
children of Victoria.^[749] <#cite_note-841> In the United States,
tartan was similarly worked into school uniforms, especially at Catholic
schools <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catholic_school_uniform>.^[750]
<#cite_note-842> The late 19th century saw tartan (sometimes in silk) in
fashion throughout Europe, including in France (e.g. Paris, Lyon, and
Alsace) and Italy,^[751] <#cite_note-843> and as far from Britain as
Russia.^[752] <#cite_note-Kirsanova-844> (See c. 1855 French master
weaver's illustration <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Master_Weaver%27s_Thesis_Book,_Montage_a_Planchette,_1850%E2%80%9360_(CH_18423041-26).jpg> of complex tartan-making.) Founded in 1898, Walker's Shortbread <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Walker%27s_Shortbread> has long been sold in royal Stewart tartan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Stewart_tartan> packaging around the world (especially for Christmas <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christmas> and Hogmanay <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hogmanay>).^[753] <#cite_note-845>


      20th century to present[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/
      index.php?title=Tartan&action=edit&section=30>]

Head and shoulders view, in black and white, of Edward in a grey suit,
white shirt, and starkly tartaned tie <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:The_Duke_of_Windsor_(1945).jpg>Edward, Duke of Windsor <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_VIII>, in a tartan necktie, 1945

In the Edwardian era <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edwardian_era>,
tartan had become less a component of men's clothing (with the decline
in kilt-wearing) but more an important part of women's fashion,^[77]
<#cite_note-Scarlett1990_40-89> including fanciful /haute couture
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haute_couture>/ designs from Paris
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_fashion#Paris> that had no
connection to Highland style,^[576] <#cite_note-BdLC_26-654> and many
accessories such as petticoats <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Petticoat>, stockings <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stockings>, and
blouses <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blouse>; masculine accessories
included braces (suspenders) <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suspenders>,
neckties <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Necktie>, cummerbunds <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cummerbund>, and socks.^[77] <#cite_note-
Scarlett1990_40-89>

Edward VII <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_VII> himself had grown
up wearing Highland dress frequently.^[697] <#cite_note-
Armstrong_101-788> There was also in this period into the 1920s a market
for Highland-dress etiquette booklets, which tied into the era's "dress
sense" of decorum and class^[415] <#cite_note-PatersonMB_162-467> (see
also § Etiquette <#Etiquette>, below). Because of its associations with
the British aristocracy, Scottish clans, and Highland military, tartan
had developed an air of dignity and exclusivity.^[754] <#cite_note-
BdLC_26-27-846> Because of this, tartan was to make periodic resurgences
in the world of fashion. The tartan uniforms of the Scottish Regiments
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_Regiments> were an important
recruiting tool during World War I; as Archibald Primrose, Lord Rosebery
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Archibald_Primrose,_5th_Earl_of_Rosebery>, put it: "there is nothing so
magnificent in our army as the swing of a kilted regiment".^[755]
<#cite_note-847> Tartan's Georgian re-orientation as a symbol
representing unionism and empire continued well into the first half of
the 20th century,^[756] <#cite_note-848> though outright tartanry and
Highlandism on the part of the upper class waned, especially after about
1920.^[757] <#cite_note-849> Nevertheless, Edward VIII <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_VIII>, later Duke of Windsor, was a life-
long devotee of tartan, often wearing more than one at a time.^[576]
<#cite_note-BdLC_26-654>

Close up of five skirts in a wide variety of tartans <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Catholic_school_uniforms.jpg>Catholic school
uniform <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catholic_school_uniform> skirts,
using a wide variety of tartans

Tartan patterns (often simple, unnamed ones) remained commonly used for
skirts and pinafore dresses <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Jumper_(dress)> (jumper dresses) in Catholic and other private school
uniform <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/School_uniform> codes in North
America and also in public and private schools in New Zealand. The style
spread to many other places, including South America, Japan <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/School_uniforms_in_Japan>^[674] <#cite_note-
Tuckett_20-763> (which sometimes imports tartan directly from
Scotland),^[758] <#cite_note-850> and Hong Kong.

Black and white image of Harry Lauder in a kilt, tartan hose and tie,
tweed jacket, and a Balmoral bonnet <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Harry_Lauder_1922.jpg>Harry Lauder <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Harry_Lauder> in one of his Highland outfits, 1922

Harry Lauder <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harry_Lauder> (properly Sir
Henry – he was knighted for his war-effort <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/War_effort> fundraising during World War I) became world-famous in
the 1910s and 1920s, on a dance hall <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Dance_hall> and vaudeville <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vaudeville>
entertainment platform of tartan Highland dress, a thick Scots <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scots_language> accent, and folksy songs about an
idealised, rural Scotland, like his hit "Roamin' in the Gloamin'
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roamin%27_in_the_Gloamin%27>". At one
point, he was the highest-paid performer in the world, and toured the
United States, Australia, South Africa, and of course the UK to sold-out
audiences. A Lowlander himself, Lauder has been credited with (and
blamed for) keeping alive a tartanry-and-Highlandism image of Scotland,
with critics calling him a "kilted clown" who promoted the idea of
Scotsmen "clothed like the chieftain of Clan McCrazy".^[759]
<#cite_note-851>


        Disaspora and globalisation[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/
        index.php?title=Tartan&action=edit&section=31>]

By the mid-20th century,^[760] <#cite_note-852> annual Highland games
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highland_games> events, modelled on the
traditional events in Scotland, had been established not just in
Scotland but throughout the United States, Canada, Australia, New
Zealand, and South Africa, among other places with a notable Scottish
diaspora <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_diaspora>, which totals
about 50 million people worldwide.^[761] <#cite_note-853> There are
dozens of such events in Scotland,^[762] <#cite_note-Armstrong_264-854>
and at least 260 annual Highland games events worldwide as of
2000,^[763] <#cite_note-855> more than 100 of them in the US alone, and
dozens more in Canada.^[762] <#cite_note-Armstrong_264-854> They are
closely intertwined with bagpipe <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Great_Highland_bagpipe> band competitions (which date to 1781), a
lasting source of tartan imagery in their regiment-inspired Highland
uniforms.^[764] <#cite_note-856>

A hundred or more pipers and drummers in an array of kilts at a Scottish
games event <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Massed_bands.jpg>Massed
bands <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Massed_bands> at the Glengarry
Highland Games <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glengarry_Highland_Games>,
Maxville, Ontario, Canada, 2006

The games' rather flamboyantly^[765] <#cite_note-857> tartaned
subculture <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subculture> is sustained
outside Scotland primarily by multi-generational Scottish descendants
rather than by direct Scottish expatriates.^[766] <#cite_note-858>
^[767] <#cite_note-859>

Red-kilted bagpipers in feather bonnets on an urban city street
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:MHPB_New_York.jpg>Mystic Highland
Pipe Band at Tartan Day <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tartan_Day>
parade, New York City, 2002

Tartan Day <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tartan_Day>, an annual
symbolic ethnicity <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symbolic_ethnicity>
holiday among the Scottish diaspora, is a growing affair celebrated on 6
April, the date on which the Declaration of Arbroath <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Declaration_of_Arbroath> was signed in 1320.
Tartan Day was first declared in Nova Scotia <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Nova_Scotia> in 1987, and was essentially nation-wide in Canada by
the 1990s. It has since spread to Australia (with varying levels of
official recognition, 1989–1996), the US (1998), and other places
including New Zealand,^[768] <#cite_note-860> and even Argentina^[769]
<#cite_note-861> and Paris, France.^[770] <#cite_note-
SRT_Brittany_National-862> In New York City <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/New_York_City>, it has turned into an entire Tartan Week since
1999, with honorary "grand marshals" that are usually Scottish
celebrities.^[771] <#cite_note-863>

The term /tartanism/ (as distinct from /tartanry/) has been coined by
Ian Brown (2012) for this international tokenisation of tartan as an
ethnic-identity <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethnicity> symbol,
evolving to some degree independently to suit diasporic cultural needs
and unrestrained by the views of the originating Scottish "home"
culture.^[772] <#cite_note-864> According to Ian Maitland Hume (2001),
tartan and the kilt are powerful symbols that "encapsulate many facets
of a heritage which people aspire to access ... a part-mythical family
origin for those seeking roots".^[773] <#cite_note-865>

The Scottish Tartans Museum and Heritage Center <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_Tartans_Museum_and_Heritage_Center> was
opened by the Scottish Tartans Society <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scottish_Tartans_Society> in 1988 in Highlands, North Carolina <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highlands,_North_Carolina>; in 1994, it moved to
nearby Franklin <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Franklin,_North_Carolina>. The museum, which runs independently of STS,
features over 600 tartans on display, including specimens dating to
c. 1725, and Highland dress examples to ca. 1800.^[774] <#cite_note-866>
(STS also operated a Scottish Tartans Museum in Edinburgh,^[775]
<#cite_note-867> but it closed when STS did in 2000.) A major exhibition
on tartan was produced by the Fashion Institute of Technology <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fashion_Institute_of_Technology> in New York 1988–
89, and another was created for the Edinburgh Festival <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edinburgh_Festival> in 1989.^[776]
<#cite_note-868> Others followed in Italy in 2003, and Japan in
2018.^[777] <#cite_note-869> In April 2023, the Victoria and Albert
Museum of Dundee (V&A Dundee <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
V%26A_Dundee>) opened a design exhibit (running until January 2024)
about tartan and its "shifting context", with goals of "challenging
preconceptions of what tartan is, whether that be from a historical
sense or fashion sense".^[778] <#cite_note-870> ^[779] <#cite_note-871>

D. Gordon Teall of Teallach, of the Scottish Tartans Society, observed
in 1994:^[780] <#cite_note-872>

    Tartans have always formed part of Scotland's historic heritage and
    it is a compliment to their country that they have become so
    widespread throughout the English and Gaelic speaking world. They
    are probably more popular now than they have ever been because they
    have come to symbolise the spirit of families, clans and districts
    and, more recently, corporate institutions.

Even as tartan has been bent to the cultural needs of the diaspora, as
"the most straightforward and outward sign of ... affinity with
Scottishness", and bent to the commercial intents of fashion, tourism,
entertainment, and other industries, tartan's reception by native Scots
in Scotland has been less favourable for decades, even the last century
or so. Reasons include a feeling that it is not really a symbol of broad
Scottish national identity because of its specifically Gaelic and
Highland origin; the "Highlandist" and imperialist foisting of it on the
entire country as national costume in the late Georgian through
Victorian eras; distorted views of Scottish people promulgated by Lauder
and other tartaned entertainers of a century ago; an academic view of
tartary and Lowland kailyard literature <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Kailyard_literature> as two halves of a low-brow, romanticising
vulgarity (reinforced in recent decades by the "Tartan Army <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tartan_Army>" fandom of the Scotland national
football team reinvigorating a working-class attachment to kilts and
tartan); and historically inaccurate portrayal of Scotland by tartan-
heavy Hollywood productions like /Brigadoon/ <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Brigadoon_(film)> (1954) and /Braveheart <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Braveheart>/ (1995).^[781] <#cite_note-873> Brancaz (2016) argues
that "looking at tartan through the lens of the intelligentsia fails to
account for its enduring appeal and resilience. ... [T]he wearing of
kilts and tartans at weddings, funerals, and /cèilidhs <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/C%C3%A8ilidh>/ in Scotland has increasingly been
interpreted as a form of cultural reappropriation."^[782] <#cite_note-874>


        Industry and politics[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/
        index.php?title=Tartan&action=edit&section=32>]

In 2006, the British Ministry of Defence <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Ministry_of_Defence_(United_Kingdom)> sparked controversy when it
allowed foreign woollen mills to bid for the government contracts to
provide the tartans used by the Scottish troops (newly amalgamated as
battalions into the Royal Regiment of Scotland <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Regiment_of_Scotland>), and lowered the
formerly very high standards for the cloth.^[214] <#cite_note-
Newsome_Black_Watch-243>

Following a bill <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Private_member%27s_bill>
submitted in the Scottish Parliament <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scottish_Parliament> in February 2007,^[783] <#cite_note-whatsNew-875>
Scotland's enterprise minister <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Deputy_Minister_for_Enterprise_and_Lifelong_Learning> announced in July
2007 that the National Archives of Scotland <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/National_Archives_of_Scotland> would set up a national register of
tartans.^[784] <#cite_note-Mather_MacKenzie-876> The announcement stated
that "Tartan's importance to Scotland cannot be overestimated. It is
deeply embedded in Scottish culture and is an internationally recognised
symbol of Scotland."^[784] <#cite_note-Mather_MacKenzie-876> This was
later reiterated in 2013 through the BBC.^[785] <#cite_note-877> The
ministry cited an industry report indicating that "the tartan industry
is a significant contributor to the overall Scottish economy <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economy_of_Scotland>; and larger ... than
suggested by previous industry estimates", and is the basis for some 200
businesses, 4,000 jobs, and £350 million in annual GDP <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gross_domestic_product> in Scotland.^[784]
<#cite_note-Mather_MacKenzie-876> The bill passed in October 2008, and
the Scottish Register of Tartans <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scottish_Register_of_Tartans> launched in February 2009.^[783]
<#cite_note-whatsNew-875> (See § Registration <#Registration>, below.)

Clothing rack with very wide array of tartan flannel shirts <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Too_Much_Plaid_(4394365739).jpg>General
tartan-pattern clothing shot up in popularity again starting around 2010

/The Observer <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Observer>/ reported in
2010 that tartan clothing had become more popular than ever before,
crossing subcultural, social-class, and age-group lines, and showing in
that year a 540% sales increase in Britain from only two years
earlier.^[786] <#cite_note-878> Around the same time, there began a
resurgence in tartan kilt wearing among Scottish young people "as a mark
of a vibrant, modern Scotland".^[787] <#cite_note-879> ^[788]
<#cite_note-PatersonMB_172-880> ^[789] <#cite_note-Armstrong_274-881>
^[790] <#cite_note-Cheape2012_14-882> This has interrupted a
generations-long trend of native Scottish disaffection toward tartan as
stereotyping kitsch <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kitsch>.^[791]
<#cite_note-883> ^[792] <#cite_note-884> An online survey by BBC
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BBC> in 2012 found that 52% of
respondents strongly or very strongly disagreed with the premise "Walter
Scott's re-branding of all Scots as tartan-wearing Highlanders has been
a hindrance to Scotland's cultural development", and only a third
agreed.^[793] <#cite_note-885> Tartan in mainstream, international
fashion experienced another resurgence starting in 2019.^[794]
<#cite_note-886>

Contemporary Scottish nationalism <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scottish_nationalism> has been said to be "fed, in part, by tartan and
Jacobite nostalgia".^[795] <#cite_note-887> After avoidance of tartan
since the 1970s (especially by Scottish liberals <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liberalism>),^[796] <#cite_note-888> the cloth has
been politicised again as a nationalist symbol (as it was in the early
18th century), especially during the 2014 Scottish independence
referendum <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
2014_Scottish_independence_referendum> and in the Scottish National
Party <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_National_Party>'s 2015
campaign.^[789] <#cite_note-Armstrong_274-881> (Perhaps owing to this
messaging shift, the VisitScotland agency around the same time changed
its tourism advertising to minimise, though not eliminate, tartan
imagery.)^[797] <#cite_note-889> Murray Pittock <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Murray_Pittock> (2002) writes that the neo-
Jacobitism <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neo-Jacobite_Revival> is "both
irritating kitsch and a language of identity" for modern Scots.^[798]
<#cite_note-890> After several decades of intellectual hostility toward
tartan (e.g. in Tom Nairn <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tom_Nairn>'s
1977 /The Break-up of Britain: Crisis and Neo-nationalism/, and Hugh
Trevor-Roper <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hugh_Trevor-Roper>'s
posthumous 2008 /The Invention of Scotland/), an "academic re-assessment
of tartan" began in the early 21st century, relying on a wider range of
early and modern source material,^[799] <#cite_note-891> in
historiographical <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Historiography>,
multidisciplinary edited volumes <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Edited_volume> including /Scottish History: The Power of the Past/ (eds.
Edward J. Cowan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_J._Cowan> and
Richard J. Finlay <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_J._Finlay>,
2002) and /From Tartan to Tartany/ (ed. Ian Brown, 2010).

Major commercial weavers (tartan mills) of traditional tartan cloth that
are operating today include Lochcarron of Scotland^[800] <#cite_note-
BdLC_33-892> ^[801] <#cite_note-Armstrong_259-893> in Lochcarron
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lochcarron> and Selkirk <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Selkirk,_Scottish_Borders>; Ingles Buchan in
Glasgow <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glasgow>;^[388] <#cite_note-
Newsome_2005-435> ^[801] <#cite_note-Armstrong_259-893> House of Edgar
(also a Highland dress vendor, and a subsidiary of Macnaughton Holdings)
in Perth <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perth,_Scotland>;^[388]
<#cite_note-Newsome_2005-435> Johnstons of Elgin <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elgin,_Moray> (also a wool clothing maker),^[388]
<#cite_note-Newsome_2005-435> Strathmore Woollen in Forfar <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Forfar>,^[388] <#cite_note-Newsome_2005-435> and
D. C. Dalgliesh in Selkirk,^[106] <#cite_note-Newsome_2006-123> all
three of which are now part of the Edinburgh-based Scotweb, under the
trade name <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trade_name> Clan;^[802]
<#cite_note-894> Prickly Thistle (also a women's clothing maker) in
Evanton <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evanton> and Edinburgh <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edinburgh>;^[803] <#cite_note-895> The Tartan
Weaving Mill (also a weaving museum, and a subsidiary of Gold Brothers)
in Edinburgh;^[804] <#cite_note-896> Andrew Elliot Ltd in Selkirk
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Selkirk,_Scottish_Borders>; Stevens &
Graham (specialising mostly in tartan rugs and carpet) in Rutherglen
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rutherglen>; Marton Mills in West
Yorkshire <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Yorkshire>, England;
Cambrian Woollen Mill <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Cambrian_Woollen_Mill>, in Powys, Wales; West Coast Woollen Mills in
Vancouver <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vancouver>, British Columbia,
Canada;^[805] <#cite_note-PatersonMB_175-897> GK Textiles in Port Moody
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Port_Moody>, BC (formerly Fraser &
Kirkbright, Vancouver);^[806] <#cite_note-898> and Pendleton Woolen
Mills <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pendleton_Woolen_Mills> in
Portland, Oregon, US.^[205] <#cite_note-Newsome_disguise-233> The modern
trade in wool tartan fabric has three principal markets: Highland dress,
high fashion (with significant business from France and Italy), and
furnishing.^[807] <#cite_note-899> ^[19] <#cite_note-Newsome_Wilkinson-21>

Popular tartans (including for kilts and other Highland dress, as well
as for school uniforms) have increasingly been manufactured, primarily
in the UK, in poly-viscose <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
title=Poly-viscose&action=edit&redlink=1> (PV),^[808] <#cite_note-900> a
blend of the artificial materials polyester <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Polyester> and viscose <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Viscose>
(rayon), typically in a 65% polyester to 35% viscose ratio.^[809]
<#cite_note-901> ^[810] <#cite_note-902> PV is promoted as washable,
durable, crease-resistant but heat-settable for permanent pleating,
shrinkage-resistant, stain-resistant, colour-fast, low-pilling <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pill_(textile)>, hypoallergenic, not attractive to
clothes moths <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tineola_bisselliella>, more
"breatheable" than polyester (thus good for athletics), lower cost than
wool, and lighter weight than wool, but said to have a wool-like
texture.^[811] <#cite_note-Moloney-903> ^[812] <#cite_note-Fiddes-904>
^[813] <#cite_note-905> ^[814] <#cite_note-906> ^[815] <#cite_note-907>
It also does not rely on animal industry, so it appeals to vegans
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Veganism>.^[811] <#cite_note-Moloney-903>
^[812] <#cite_note-Fiddes-904> Large-scale global manufacturers of
tartan-patterned cloth in a variety of cotton, polyester, viscose, nylon
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nylon>, etc., materials and blends
include Başkan Tekstil in Istanbul <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Istanbul> and Bursa <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bursa>, Turkey; and
Jeen Wei Enterprises in Taichung <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Taichung>, Taiwan; while a leading maker of tartan ribbon <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ribbon> is Satab in Saint-Just-Malmont <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saint-Just-Malmont>, France.^[816]
<#cite_note-908> Tartan designs have long been produced in low-cost
cotton <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cotton> in large quantities in
China.^[805] <#cite_note-PatersonMB_175-897>

Carol Craig (2003) writes: "Like it or not, tartan is a very
sophisticated branding and marketing tool for Scotland."^[817]
<#cite_note-909> In a tartan-as-marketing analysis, Paterson (2001)
observed that continued internationalisation of tartan manufacture,
design, and consumption has diluted the associative "Scottishness" of
tartan and its value as a national identifier. He blames this in part on
Scottish weavers' failure to adapt to market demands for a wider range
of fabric applications, as well as the businesses' own complicity in
broadening tartan's perceived cultural identity, e.g. in creating
tartans for non-Scottish families, places, and organisations.^[818]
<#cite_note-910>

(For particular 20th-century to present-day tartans, see also
§ Corporate and commercial <#Corporate_and_commercial> and § Fashion
<#Fashion>, below.)


        In popular culture[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
        title=Tartan&action=edit&section=33>]

A cast of dozens singing and dancing in kilts, tartan trews, and other
outfits <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Brigadoon_(1954)_trailer_3.jpg>Scene from 1954 /Brigadoon <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brigadoon_(film)>/ film, with kilts and tartan trews

In 1947, the tartan-laden Broadway musical /Brigadoon <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brigadoon>/ (followed by a film version <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brigadoon_(film)> in 1954 and a television
adaptation in 1966) renewed an excessively romanticised notion of the
Highlands and Highland dress. A critical review called it a "whimsical
dream-world" that was "overloaded with Hollywood-Scottish
trappings".^[819] <#cite_note-911> (The production is generally not well
received by actual Scots.)^[820] <#cite_note-912> ^[821] <#cite_note-
Herald_Hollywood-913>

Tartan suits were popular in the mod subculture <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mod_(subculture)> of Great Britain of the early to
mid-1960s and its late 1970s revival <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Mod_revival>.

Football fans in kilts and other tartan garb, mixed with informal
everyday clothing, on steps outside an arena <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/File:Tartan_Army.png>"Tartan Army <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Tartan_Army>" Scottish football fans at a match in Milan, Italy, in 2005

Since the 1970s, the fandom of the Scotland men's national football
(soccer) team <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scotland_men%27s_national_football_team> have been collectively referred
to by the nickname "Tartan Army <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Tartan_Army>", with fans often sporting tartan clothing (including
kilts) at matches.

Five young men, two in tartan shirts, all wearing Dutch clogs <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:BayCityRollers1976RobBogaerts.jpg>The Bay
City Rollers <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bay_City_Rollers> in the
Netherlands in 1976, sporting some tartan shirts and a tartan-trimmed jacket

Popular in the mid-1970s, Scottish teeny-bopper band the Bay City
Rollers <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bay_City_Rollers> were described
by the /British Hit Singles & Albums <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
British_Hit_Singles_%26_Albums>/ reference book as "tartan teen
sensations from Edinburgh <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Edinburgh>".^[822] <#cite_note-914>

<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Punkertreffen_1984_-
_Ausschnitt.jpg>A German punk <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Punk_subculture> wearing a piece of the royal Stewart tartan <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Stewart_tartan>, 1984

Tartan became a common element of punk subculture <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Punk_subculture> starting in the late 1970s. Punk
music <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Punk_rock> was a way for youth in
the British Isles to voice their discontent with the ruling class
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ruling_class> and with modern society.
The unorthodox use of tartan (especially the royal Stewart <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Stewart_tartan>), which had long been
associated with authority and gentility <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Gentry>, was then seen as an expression of that discontent. In this way,
tartan – worn unconventionally – became an anti-establishment <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-establishment> symbol. This was entirely on
purpose according to Vivienne Westwood <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Vivienne_Westwood>, a designer deeply involved in early punk
fashion;^[754] <#cite_note-BdLC_26-27-846> ^[823] <#cite_note-915> the
idea was "to seize the very fabric of the Establishment in order to
reverse its meaning and perhaps to challenge society's design."^[824]
<#cite_note-MartinR_59-916> American punks often wore tartan skirts, a
"subversion" of the Catholic school-girl uniform, and kilts have also
been worn in the punk scene since the late 1970s, especially in the
UK.^[790] <#cite_note-Cheape2012_14-882> Baggy tartan pants later proved
popular among pop-punks <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pop-punk> and
skate punks <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Skate_punk>, and tartan-lined
jackets among ska punks <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ska_punk>. (For
further information, see Punk fashion <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Punk_fashion>.) From the late 1990s, kilts (mostly modernised "utility
kilts <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Utility_kilt>" but sometimes
traditional ones) have become relatively popular even in North American
post-punk subculture (e.g. the goth <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Goth_subculture>–industrial <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Industrial_music>, emo <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emo>, and
steampunk <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steampunk> scenes), though
often in black rather than tartan.

After the 1970s, Westwood, who continued to work extenstively with
tartan, was joined by other big-name /couturiers/. These included Ralph
Lauren <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ralph_Lauren> and Laura Ashley
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laura_Ashley>, whose designs promoted
tartan as a mainstream modern clothing option "with traditional grace
and style" for both women and men;^[800] <#cite_note-BdLC_33-892> ^[824]
<#cite_note-MartinR_59-916> Stephen Sprouse <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Stephen_Sprouse>,^[824] <#cite_note-MartinR_59-916> credited with a
1980s combination of "uptown sophistication in clothing with a downtown
punk and pop sensibility";^[825] <#cite_note-917> and later Alexander
McQueen <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_McQueen>,^[826]
<#cite_note-918> who was "consciously repoliticising the cloth".^[308]
<#cite_note-NatGeo-352> Others have included Jean Paul Gaultier
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jean_Paul_Gaultier>, Tommy Hilfiger
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tommy_Hilfiger> (who made tartan central
to his fall 2000 collection), Christian Lacroix <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christian_Lacroix>, Yves Saint Laurent <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yves_Saint_Laurent_(designer)>, Giorgio Armani
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Giorgio_Armani>, and Gianfranco Ferré
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gianfranco_Ferr%C3%A9>.^[827]
<#cite_note-919> ^[824] <#cite_note-MartinR_59-916> A tartan outfit
designed by Westwood featured on a commemorative UK postage stamp
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
United_Kingdom_commemorative_stamps_2010%E2%80%932019#2012> issued by
the Royal Mail <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Mail> in 2012
celebrating "Great British Fashion".^[828] <#cite_note-920>

Tartan/plaid flannel <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flannel> shirts,
emblematic of the working class, re-entered mainstream fashion through a
series of subcultural <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subculture>
adoptions, originating primarily in the western United States. First,
the style became a staple of cholo <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Cholo_(subculture)> style in and around Los Angeles, from the 1970s.
From there, the style later became adopted by hip hop fashion <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hip_hop_fashion> in the 1990s, especially the West
Coast hip hop <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Coast_hip_hop>
lifestyle.^[829] <#cite_note-921> Tartan flannel shirts also became
quintessentially part of (and androgynous within) the grunge <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grunge> scene (starting in Seattle) of the late
1980s to 2000s.^[830] <#cite_note-922> There was fashion cross-
pollination between these youth-culture movements,^[831]
<#cite_note-923> and the fashion industry has found this confluence very
marketable.^[832] <#cite_note-924>

A resurgence of interest in tartan and kilts (and even Scottish
tourism)^[821] <#cite_note-Herald_Hollywood-913> ^[833] <#cite_note-925>
has been generated in recent times by major Hollywood productions^[834]
<#cite_note-Armstrong_262-926> like the /Highlander/ franchise <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highlander_(franchise)> (1986–2007),^[835]
<#cite_note-Doran-927> ^[834] <#cite_note-Armstrong_262-926> /Four
Weddings and a Funeral <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Four_Weddings_and_a_Funeral>/ (1994),^[836] <#cite_note-928> /Braveheart
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Braveheart>/ (1995),^[837] <#cite_note-
PatersonMB_285-287,_304-929> ^[838] <#cite_note-930> ^[839] <#cite_note-
NYT_film-931> /Rob Roy <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Rob_Roy_(1995_film)>/ (1995),^[837] <#cite_note-
PatersonMB_285-287,_304-929> ^[839] <#cite_note-NYT_film-931> ^[840]
<#cite_note-932> /Brave <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Brave_(2012_film)>/ (2012),^[841] <#cite_note-933> and the television
series /Outlander <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Outlander_(TV_series)>/
(2014–, with a follow-on travelogue documentary <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Travel_documentary> series, /Men in Kilts
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Men_in_Kilts>/).^[842] <#cite_note-934>
Many of these featured custom-designed tartans.^[835] <#cite_note-
Doran-927> ^[843] <#cite_note-Martin_film-935>

Tartan clothing has appeared frequently in /Doctor Who <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doctor_Who>/. The Fourth Doctor <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fourth_Doctor> (Tom Baker <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tom_Baker>) wore a Wallace <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Wallace> tartan scarf on /Terror of the
Zygons <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terror_of_the_Zygons>/,^[844]
<#cite_note-936> and his robot-dog companion K9 <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/K9_(Doctor_Who)> had a tartan collar.^[843]
<#cite_note-Martin_film-935> The Sixth Doctor <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Sixth_Doctor> (Colin Baker <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Colin_Baker>) had a signature patchwork frock coat that included
segments in three different tartans, and also typically wore a tartan
waistcoat in a fourth sett under it.^[845] <#cite_note-937> The Seventh
Doctor <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seventh_Doctor> (Sylvester McCoy
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sylvester_McCoy>) wore a crimson and
black tartan scarf on /Time and the Rani <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Time_and_the_Rani>/. Clara Oswald <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Clara_Oswald> (Jenna Coleman <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Jenna_Coleman>), the companion of the Eleventh Doctor <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eleventh_Doctor> (Matt Smith <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Matt_Smith>) and the Twelfth Doctor <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Twelfth_Doctor> (Peter Capaldi <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peter_Capaldi>), wore a Campbell <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Campbell> tartan dress on "The Name of the
Doctor <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Name_of_the_Doctor>" and a
Wallace skirt on "The Time of the Doctor <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
The_Time_of_the_Doctor>" and "Deep Breath <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Deep_Breath_(Doctor_Who)>".^[846] <#cite_note-938> Annabel Scholey
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Annabel_Scholey> as Claire Brown, in the
Thirteenth Doctor <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thirteenth_Doctor>
(Jodie Whittaker <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jodie_Whittaker>)
serial /Flux <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doctor_Who_(series_13)>/,
wears a 1960s-style muted tartan dress.^[847] <#cite_note-939> The
Fourteenth Doctor <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fourteenth_Doctor>
(David Tennant <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Tennant>) wore a
brown tartan suit in the 60th anniversary specials <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doctor_Who_(2023_specials)>.^[848] <#cite_note-940>

    made of at least five different setts <https://en.wikipedia.org/

    Rita Ora <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rita_Ora> performing in
    Glasgow in 2018, wearing a tartan trench coat made of at least five
    different setts
  *
    Grunge fashion still alive and well in 2019, featuring a lot of
    tartan/plaid shirts <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
