    Popular designs[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
    title=Tartan&action=edit&section=34>]

One of the most popular tartans is the royal Stewart <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Stewart_tartan>, ostensibly the personal
tartan of the British monarch <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
List_of_British_monarchs>, since George IV declared it his own (though
it was probably designed by the Sobieski Stuarts <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sobieski_Stuarts>,^[689] <#cite_note-
EsleaMacDonald_2019-06-779> albeit based on mid-18th-century pattern
called "Prince Charles Edward Stuart").^[112] <#cite_note-SRT_PCES-129>
^[64] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2016-01-76> The "royal" sett was first
published in 1831 in the book /The Scottish Gaël/ by James Logan. In
addition to its use in clothing, such as skirts and scarves, royal
Stewart tartan has also appeared on biscuit tins for Scottish shortbread
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shortbread>,^[849] <#cite_note-941> and
it has also long been favoured by the British punk <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Punk_subculture> scene.

Another tartan in very common use by the general public is Black Watch
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_Watch> (also known as old Campbell
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Campbell>, Grant <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Grant> hunting, and Government).^[128]
<#cite_note-MacDonald1995_48-145> This tartan, a dark variant (and
ancestor) of the main Clan Campbell tartan, has long been used by
military units in the British Army <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
British_Army> and other Commonwealth <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Commonwealth_of_Nations> forces.

Early manufacturer Wilsons of Bannockburn made many "fashion", "fancy",
or "national" tartans with catalogue numbers or fanciful names, without
any association with particular families, districts, or organisations;
two popular ones still in use are both usually called "Caledonia".
Wilsons No. 3 is found in their 1819 /Key Pattern Book/ and is
comparatively simple,^[850] <#cite_note-SRT_468-942> while No. 144 is
more complex, though of a similar colour scheme, and seems to date to
the late 18th century.^[851] <#cite_note-943> (The numbering suggests
the other does as well.) Some other tartans in this "Caledonia" group
were later claimed by clans; e.g. Caledonia No. 43 or "Kidd" became one
of the MacPherson <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_MacPherson>
tartans.^[852] <#cite_note-944> ^[850] <#cite_note-SRT_468-942>

  *
    Royal Stewart tartan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:Royal_Stewart_tartan.png>
    Royal Stewart tartan
  *
    Black Watch tartan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:Black_Watch_(Old_Campbell)_tartan,_tileable.png>
    Black Watch tartan
  *
    Wilsons' No. 3 tartan, named Caledonia <https://en.wikipedia.org/
    wiki/File:Caledonia_(No._3)_tartan,_tileable.png>
    Wilsons' No. 3 tartan, named Caledonia
  *
    Wilsons' No. 155, also often called Caledonia <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Caledonia_(No._155)_tartan,_tileable.png>
    Wilsons' No. 155, also often called Caledonia

In the general fashion industry, various patterns are technically tartan
but are not treated as tartan/s/ in the clan or district sense. The very
basic red-and-black Rob Roy or Robert Roy MacGregor pattern, the oldest
of the Clan Gregor <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Gregor> setts
(though named after Rob Roy <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Rob_Roy_MacGregor> in the Victorian period),^[853] <#cite_note-945> is
also in broad use (often with changed colours) as one of the most common
patterns used in flannel <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flannel> cloth
for clothing and bedding; in the US, it is often called "buffalo
plaid",^[854] <#cite_note-946> a term of uncertain derivation.^[855]
<#cite_note-947> When the Rob Roy sett is changed to a white ground with
any other colour this forms the most common gingham <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gingham> cloth style. Gingham is often given a
wider setting, to form a lattice appearance (sometimes called
"windowpane plaid" or "windowpane check").^[856] <#cite_note-
Schlueter-948> When that pattern is given one or more additional over-
check colours, the result is the pattern known as tattersall <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tattersall_(cloth)>.^[856] <#cite_note-Schlueter-948>

  *
    One of the most common flannel patterns, "buffalo plaid" is just Rob
    Roy MacGregor tartan (originally red and black) rendered in any of
    various colours <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:Green_and_blue_flannel_pattern,_tileable.png>
    One of the most common flannel <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Flannel> patterns, "buffalo plaid" is just Rob Roy MacGregor tartan
    (originally red and black) rendered in any of various colours
  *
    Rob Roy changed to white and any other colour becomes gingham
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:Gingham_(blue),_offset,_zoomed_out.png>
    Rob Roy changed to white and any other colour becomes gingham
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gingham>
  *
    Windowpane gingham <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:Gingham,_wide-set_(orange),_offset,_zoomed_out.png>
    Windowpane gingham
  *
    Windowpane gingham with two or more over-checks becomes tattersall
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:Tattersall_(red_and_green),_offset,_zoomed_out.png>
    Windowpane gingham with two or more over-checks becomes tattersall
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tattersall_(cloth)>


    Tartans for specific purposes[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/
    index.php?title=Tartan&action=edit&section=35>]

A a fairly traditional tartan pattern, but rendered in a rainbow
selection of blue, purple, red, orange, yellow, and green. <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Tartan_of_Pride,_2008,_centred.png>"Tartan of
Pride", designed in 2008;^[857] <#cite_note-949> one of over a dozen
LGBT <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/LGBT>-themed modern "fashion" tartans

In addition to clan tartans, many tartan patterns have been developed
for individuals, families, districts and towns, institutions,
corporations, and events.^[1] <#cite_note-Newsome_1994-1> They have even
been created for particular religious and ethnic groups <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethnic_group>,^[co] <#cite_note-954> and for
sociological groups like the LGBT <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/LGBT>
community.^[788] <#cite_note-PatersonMB_172-880> ^[862] <#cite_note-955>
Tartan has had a long history with the military, and today some military
units – particularly those within the Commonwealth <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations> – have tartan dress
uniforms.^[863] <#cite_note-956> (See List of tartans § UK military or
government tartans <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
List_of_tartans#UK_military_or_government_tartans>.)


      Regional[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
      title=Tartan&action=edit&section=36>]

Many districts, cities, and towns in Scotland have their own tartans,
mostly dating to the 20th century (though some few district tartans are
quite old),^[cp] <#cite_note-957> and not always official; many were
just created for marketing to tourists,^[864] <#cite_note-958> and some
are copyrighted works tied to specific vendors.^[865] <#cite_note-
Newsome_2008-959> They are intended primarily for those to whom a clan
tartan does not seem to apply (see § Etiquette <#Etiquette>, below). At
least two local government councils in Scotland <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_government_in_Scotland> have official
tartans.^[866] <#cite_note-960>

A tartan with wide bands of green and red, and narrower bands of grey
and yellow <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Maple_leaf_tartan.png>The
Maple Leaf tartan, designed in 1964,^[867] <#cite_note-961> has been an
official symbol of Canada since 2011.^[868] <#cite_note-DCH-962>

In addition to the traditional district and modern geographic tartans of
Scotland, new designs have been created for places in other countries.
Only some regional tartans are officially recognised by the government
bodies of the places the designs represent.

The pan-Celticism <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pan-Celticism> movement
has inspired the creation of "national" (in the sense of Celtic nations
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celtic_nations>) and sometimes regional
tartans "to emphasise the ... bonds with other Celtic countries"^[869]
<#cite_note-SRT_Welsh_National-963> outside of Scotland; none of these
appear to have any official recognition. There are tartans of Cornwall
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Cornish_kilts_and_tartans#National_tartans>, long a part of Devonshire
in England (designed from 1963 to the 1980s);^[cq] <#cite_note-970>
Wales <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wales> (from 1967 onward^[cr]
<#cite_note-975>  – sometimes with false claims of antiquity by
marketers);^[880] <#cite_note-Newsome_2005-04-976> the Isle of Man
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isle_of_Man> (from 1946, many by D. G.
Teall of the Scottish Tartans Society, and several asymmetric);^[cs]
<#cite_note-988> Brittany <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brittany> in
France (from 2002);^[ct] <#cite_note-991> Galicia <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galicia,_Spain> in Spain (from 1990);^[cu]
<#cite_note-995> and especially Ireland <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Ireland> (from 1956).

After the discovery of the "Dungiven tartan" (see § 16th century
<#16th_century>, above) and its marketing as a district tartan for
Ulster, Scottish weavers (and in two cases English, and in another
American) decided to tap an Irish and especially Irish-American market
by introducing a profusion of national, province, and county tartans for
Ireland and Northern Ireland <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Northern_Ireland>, generally based on established Scottish tartans with
some colour changes.^[19] <#cite_note-Newsome_Wilkinson-21> ^[205]
<#cite_note-Newsome_disguise-233> These geographical tartans, which
(aside from the Dungiven/Ulster reconstruction of 1956) date to 1970 and
later,^[209] <#cite_note-Newsome_rethinking-237> do not have any
official recognition, and are purely a product of the industry.^[19]
<#cite_note-Newsome_Wilkinson-21> ^[203] <#cite_note-PatersonMB_182-231>
One weaver even introduced a competing set of Irish national and county
tartans in 1996, different from the previous offerings.^[203]
<#cite_note-PatersonMB_182-231> "The influence of native Irish people,
either as suppliers or consumers of Irish tartans, would appear to be
minimal."^[19] <#cite_note-Newsome_Wilkinson-21>

Further afield, all but two Canadian provinces and territories <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Provinces_and_territories_of_Canada> have official
tartans <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regional_tartans_of_Canada>, with
the first dating from 1956. Neither Quebec <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Quebec> nor Nunavut <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nunavut>,
Canada's newest territory, have formally adopted patterns. Alberta
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alberta>, meanwhile, has two official
tartans, including a dress one. All but Quebec's were registered with
the Court of the Lord Lyon <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Court_of_the_Lord_Lyon> in Scotland.^[897] <#cite_note-996> Canada has
an official national tartan that was originally designed to commemorate
the introduction of its new maple leaf flag, and was made an official
national emblem in 2011.^[868] <#cite_note-DCH-962> Various Canadian
regions (like Labrador <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Labrador> and Cape
Breton Island <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cape_Breton_Island>),
counties, municipalities, and institutions also have official
tartans.^[cv] <#cite_note-999>

Tartans have been created for Australia <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
District_tartans_of_Australia>; its capital city, Canberra <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canberra>; each of its states <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/States_of_Australia>; and some of its local
government areas <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Local_government_in_Australia>; but only some of those tartans have been
officially adopted or recognised by the relevant governments in
Australia. US states <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/US_states> have
official tartans <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
List_of_U.S._state_tartans>, with the first dating from 1988.


      Hunting, mourning, dress, and dance[edit <https://
      en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tartan&action=edit&section=37>]

<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Highland-dance-2008-3-
girls.JPG>Highland dancing <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Highland_dancing>, at a 2008 Highland games <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Highland_games> event, in Aboyne dresses <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Aboyne_dress> with dance tartans that feature a lot of white

A tartan is sometimes differentiated from another with the same name by
a label: /hunting/, /mourning/, /dress/, or /dance/. The first three of
these ideas are the result of Victorian fondness for dress etiquette and
show^[900] <#cite_note-1000> (and weaver marketing);^[67] <#cite_note-
Scarlett1990_42-79> the last is more recent.

*Hunting tartans* tend to be made up of subdued colours, such as dark
blues, greens, and browns.^[901] <#cite_note-1001> Although there is
some evidence of early tartans with camouflage colours going back to the
16th century, hunting tartans, despite the name, have very little to do
with actual hunting.^[12] <#cite_note-STM_faq-12>

*Mourning tartans*, though quite rare, are associated with death and
funerals. They are usually designed using combinations of black and
white, or by replacing bright colours such as reds and yellows in a
traditional tartan with black, white, or grey.^[902] <#cite_note-1002>

*Dress tartans* are usually special tartans for formal-dress <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/White_tie> occasions^[903] <#cite_note-1003> (e.g.
dress Stewart^[904] <#cite_note-1004> is distinct from both the main
royal Stewart tartan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Royal_Stewart_tartan> and the hunting Stewart,^[905] <#cite_note-1005>
among several other tartans attributed to Stewart/Stuart <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Stewart>). In a few cases, a dress tartan is
simply the main tartan of the clan.^[cw] <#cite_note-1010> Dress tartans
that do differ from main clan tartans are sometimes entirely different
(e.g. MacMillan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_MacMillan>^[37]
<#cite_note-SRT_MacMillan-44> and MacMillan dress^[910]
<#cite_note-1011> are unrelated designs), while in most cases they are
based on the main tartan but with colour differences (e.g. Stewart).
Some dress tartans are very modern,^[83] <#cite_note-SRT_Mar-96> ^[612]
<#cite_note-SRT_MacTavish-697> but some date back to the era of the /
Vestiarium Scoticum/.^[911] <#cite_note-1012>

*Dance tartans*, intended for Highland dance <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Highland_dance> outfits, for either sex, are inspired (like most
dress tartans before them) by the arisaid <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Arisaid> (/earasaid/ tartans thought to have been worn by Highland
women in the 17th and 18th centuries, which often featured white as a
major colour, as do typical dance tartans today (most or all of which
date to the 20th century or later). Some dance tartans are named
"arisaid" rather than "dance", e.g. Fraser <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Clan_Fraser> arisaid.^[912] <#cite_note-1013> ^[cx] <#cite_note-1015>

There has been some confusion between dress and dance tartans,
especially since the idea of the latter developed from the former.^[cy]
<#cite_note-1017> Most dress tartans, including some of the oldest, also
have white in them, and have been used for dance competition in lieu of
a dance-specific tartan, so are easy to mistake for dance tartans, which
almost invariably have white in them.^[915] <#cite_note-SRT_dance-1018>
^[cz] <#cite_note-1019>


      Family and individual[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
      title=Tartan&action=edit&section=38>]

A large proportion of non-clan tartans in all of the modern tartan
databases have always been family tartans, promulgated mostly from the
late 20th century for family names that are not clans or listed as septs
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sept#Scotland> of clans. These are
usually Scottish surnames, but the /Scottish Register of Tartans
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_Register_of_Tartans>/ (/SRT/)
database increasingly includes new family tartans for names that are not
Scottish or even British. Most family tartans have no copyright claim,
since they are intended for use by anyone with the surname or an
extended-family connection. The /SRT/ classifies them together with clan
tartans in a "clan/family" category if they have history that pre-
dates /SRT/ or if they are newer and are approved by a legally
recognized clan chief or family head, but in a "name" category if they
are newer and lack such imprimatur.

A tartan that is predominantly two-tone grey with thin black and red
stripes <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Balmoral_tartan.jpg>The
British royal family <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
British_royal_family>'s own Balmoral tartan (designed c. 1852). It is
incidentally one of the few long-established tartans with multiple hues
of the same colour (two greys, in this case).

A few non-clan family tartans have an older pedigree. The best known is
Balmoral tartan, reserved for the British royal family <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_royal_family> and personal pipers
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Highland_bagpipe> thereof, since
its creation by Prince Albert <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Albert,_Prince_Consort> c. 1852.^[da] <#cite_note-1021> (See also
further discussion under § Etiquette <#Etiquette>, below.) Some clans
recognise tartans for specific family branches and septs that are not
themselves generally regarded as clans. For example, Clan Robertson/
Donnachaidh/Duncan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Robertson>
acknowledges separate, established tartans (some of them quite old) for
Inches, MacGlashan, MacInroy, MacLagan, MacPhee, MacWilliam, Reid, and
Robinson,^[917] <#cite_note-1022> and they are all registered in the /SRT/.

Since the late 1960s, various weavers have marketed (primarily to Irish
Americans) some tartans with Irish family names, without any involvement
by family members.^[205] <#cite_note-Newsome_disguise-233> There had
also been a legend that the rare /Clans Originaux/ (1880) contained
Irish family tartans, but this was finally disproven in 2003.^[19]
<#cite_note-Newsome_Wilkinson-21> ^[db] <#cite_note-1024> There is one
case of a formal Irish clan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irish_clan>/
family tartan, however: The Clan Cian <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Clan_Cian> Society commissioned a tartan for Cian of Ely <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C3%89ile>, and registered it with the Chief
Herald of Ireland <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Chief_Herald_of_Ireland> in 1983.^[19] <#cite_note-Newsome_Wilkinson-21>
^[205] <#cite_note-Newsome_disguise-233> (Even this has an Irish-
American connection, as the chief resided in California, and the society
is US-headquartered.)^[919] <#cite_note-1025> Similarly, a commercial
operation in Cardiff <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiff> named Wales
Tartan Centre (supplied by Cambrian Woollen Mill <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cambrian_Woollen_Mill>) has since the early 2000s
promoted a long series of tartans named for common or prominent Welsh
family names; they are unusual in often having odd-numbered thread
counts, and having a different warp and weft <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Warp_and_weft> (producing rectangular rather than square patterns),
probably to distinguish them from the Scottish style.^[880] <#cite_note-
Newsome_2005-04-976> ^[920] <#cite_note-1026>

For the much narrower sense of /family/ <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Nuclear_family>, the /SRT/ registers also as "name" tartans those that
are created by individuals for only themselves and their immediate-
family members, often for weddings; these usually have a copyright
claim. One of the earliest tartans named for a specific person^[dc]
<#cite_note-1027> is the "Janet Wilson sett", entered into the late
1770s records of Wilsons of Bannockburn and believed to refer to the
company founder's wife or daughter-in-law, though made as one of their
publicly available patterns.^[306] <#cite_note-Tuckett_6-350> ^[dd]
<#cite_note-1030>


      Corporate and commercial[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/
      index.php?title=Tartan&action=edit&section=39>]

Numerous Scottish brands use tartan, and some have unique tartans.
Various not-for-profit organisations also have corporate tartans.
Probably the earliest case was that of the Ancient Caledonian Society of
London (founded in 1786 and defunct since 1837), which used what is
believed to have been a consistent tartan^[923] <#cite_note-1031> for
its members' frock coats <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frock_coat>
(which, unusually, featured brocade <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Brocade> woven into the tartan, of Jacobite <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Jacobitism> white roses – it may be what 1767 advertisements called
"flowered tartan"); only one known example of the coat survives.^[344]
<#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2020-10-389> ^[924] <#cite_note-1032>

<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Saab_340B_%E2%80%98G-
LGNU%E2%80%99_Loganair_(44050058802).jpg>Scottish airline Loganair
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Loganair> in its tartan livery

As an example of a modern commercial tartan, Irn-Bru <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irn-Bru> (introduced in 1901), the best-selling
soft drink <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soft_drink> in Scotland,^[925]
<#cite_note-1033> has its own tartan.^[926] <#cite_note-1034> Scottish
regional airline Loganair <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Loganair> uses
tartan livery, including on the tails of its planes, and has two
registered corporate tartans.^[927] <#cite_note-1035> "Racing
Stewart"^[928] <#cite_note-1036> is a pattern created in 1995 for the
Jackie Stewart <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jackie_Stewart> Formula
One car-racing team.^[929] <#cite_note-PatersonMB_168-1037>

A fairly complex divided-check tartan primarily of azure, maroon, and
green with white and black over-checks <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:DunBroch_tartan.jpg>"DunBroch", a tartan devised by Disney/Pixar
for fictional characters in the animated film /Brave <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brave_(2012_film)>/

The "corporate" category is one of the fastest-growing in the official /
Scottish Register of Tartans <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scottish_Register_of_Tartans>/ (/SRT/) database, with a large number of
Scottish (and American and other) companies and societies registering
organisational tartans. These are generally protected by copyright
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copyright> and sometimes trademark
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trademark> law. These tartans vary in
purpose from general corporate livery, to special event tartans, to
tartans for fictional characters.

Two examples of the latter are Sanrio <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Sanrio>'s 2004 creation of a predominantly pink tartan for Hello Kitty
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hello_Kitty>;^[92] <#cite_note-
SRT_Hello_Kitty-107> and the 2011 creation by Disney <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Disney>/Pixar <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Pixar> of the DunBroch tartan for the family of the main character,
Mérida, of the animated Highland fantasy/adventure film /Brave <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brave_(2012_film)>/.^[930] <#cite_note-1038>


        Fashion[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
        title=Tartan&action=edit&section=40>]

A purse in the tan, black, and white Burberry check pattern, with
rearing-horseman logos superimposed <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Burberry_handbag.jpg>Handbag in Burberry <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Burberry> check

An early example of a tartan created by and for the fashion industry,
and surely the most famous, is "Burberry check". It was introduced in
the 1920s for the lining of trench coats made by Burberry <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Burberry> of London, but has been used for all
manner of clothing and accessories since 1967^[931] <#cite_note-1039>
(with another major marketing push in 2001) and is emblematic of the
company and its upscale product line.^[932] <#cite_note-1040> (For
additional information, including a legal dispute, see § Legal
protection <#Legal_protection>, below.)

A fast-growing category in the /SRT/ is that of "fashion" tartans,
created by companies and individual designers simply for aesthetic
reasons, without any association with a particular clan, family, region,
etc. Like organisational tartans, most of these have a copyright claim
attached to them.

A prominent example: In 2017, Scottish fashion designer Charles Jeffrey
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Jeffrey_(fashion_designer)>
designed a signature tartan for his Loverboy label, registering it in
the /SRT/.^[126] <#cite_note-SRT_Loverboy-143>


    Regulation[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
    title=Tartan&action=edit&section=41>]

Manufacture and use of tartan (at least in the Scottish context) is
regulated, formally and informally, in three ways: /registration/
(recording of a tartan and its association, if any, with a particular
family, organisation, person, event, etc.); /legal protection/ of a
tartan as intellectual property (trademark, copyright); and /etiquette/
(socio-cultural norms regarding the use of tartan and Highland dress).


      Registration[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
      title=Tartan&action=edit&section=42>]

A heraldict design featuring book above a curve-horned ram holding a
weaving shuttle in its mouth. <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Coat_of_Arms_of_the_Scottish_Register_of_Tartans.svg>Coat of arms
of the Scottish Register of Tartans <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scottish_Register_of_Tartans>

The naming and registration of "official" clan tartans began in 1815,
when the Highland Society of London <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Highland_Society_of_London> solicited clan tartans from clan chiefs.

Following recognition by a clan chief of a tartan as a clan tartan, the
chief was formerly able to petition the Lord Lyon King of Arms <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lord_Lyon_King_of_Arms>, the Scottish heraldic
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heraldry> authority, to register it as a
formal clan tartan.^[de] <#cite_note-1041> Once approved by the Lord
Lyon, after recommendation by the Advisory Committee on Tartan, the clan
tartan was then recorded in the Lyon Court Books.^[294] <#cite_note-
Campbell_of_Airds-336> However, leading up to the launch of the Scottish
Register of Tartans <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scottish_Register_of_Tartans> in 2009 (see below for details), the
office of the Lord Lyon stopped providing this tartan-recording process
(though its statutory authority was not changed by the Tartans Bill).

Modern-day tartans can be created and registered by anyone, with the
Scottish Register of Tartans. Modern registered tartans include ones for
Scottish and other districts, cities, and towns; for Irish counties
(devised since the 1990s)^[106] <#cite_note-Newsome_2006-123> and
families (for example, the surname Fitzpatrick <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fitzpatrick_(surname)> has two registered
tartans^[933] <#cite_note-1042> ); for organisations and companies; and
even for specific events or individuals. Tartans are also being created
in record numbers among the Scottish diaspora <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Scottish_diaspora> in the United States, Canada, Australia, New
Zealand, etc., especially for places, military divisions, pipe bands,
and individuals and their immediate families.

Until the late 20th century, instead of a central official tartan
registry, independent organisations located in Scotland, Canada, and the
United States documented and recorded tartans.^[934] <#cite_note-1043>
In 1963, an organisation called the Scottish Tartans Society <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_Tartans_Society> (now defunct, and
originally named Scottish Tartans Information Centre)^[591] <#cite_note-
Scarlett1990_ix-670> was created to record and preserve every known
tartan design.^[935] <#cite_note-1044> The society's /Register of All
Publicly Known Tartans/ (/RAPKT/) contained about 2,700 different
designs of tartan.^[936] <#cite_note-Newsome_2004-1045> Registration of
new designs was not free of charge. The society, however, ran into
financial troubles in 2000, and folded.^[937] <#cite_note-1046> ^[388]
<#cite_note-Newsome_2005-435>

Former members of that society formed two new Scotland-based entities –
the Scottish Tartans Authority <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scottish_Tartans_Authority> (STA, 1996 – before STS closed) and the
Scottish Tartans World Register <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scottish_Tartans_Society#Scottish_Tartans_World_Register> (STWR, 2000 –
the trade name <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trade_name> of a private
company, Tartan Registration Ltd).^[388] <#cite_note-Newsome_2005-435>
Both of these organisations initially based their databases on the /
RAPKT/. STA's database, the /International Tartan Index/ (/ITI/)
consisted of about 3,500 different tartans (with over 7,000, counting
variants) as of 2004.^[936] <#cite_note-Newsome_2004-1045> The online /
ITI/ was later rebranded /The Tartan Ferret/. STWR's self-titled /
Scottish Tartans World Register/ database was made up of about 3,000
different designs as of 2004.^[936] <#cite_note-Newsome_2004-1045> Both
organisations were registered as Scottish charities <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charitable_organization#Scotland> and recorded new
tartans (free in the case of STA and for a fee in the case of STWR) on
request.^[938] <#cite_note-1047> ^[939] <#cite_note-1048>

In the interim, a jointly Scotland- and US-based organisation,
International Association of Tartan Studies and Tartan Educational &
Cultural Association (IATS/TECA) emerged in 1984^[388] <#cite_note-
Newsome_2005-435> and published its own /TartanArt/ database in the
early 1990s as Microsoft Windows software which was much used in the
North American kilt-making trade. IATS/TECA was absorbed by STA by
2005.^[388] <#cite_note-Newsome_2005-435>

The Scottish Register of Tartans <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scottish_Register_of_Tartans> (SRT) is Scotland's official tartan
register, and was established in 2009.^[940] <#cite_note-SRT_home-1049>
SRT is maintained and administered by the National Archives of Scotland
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Archives_of_Scotland> (NAS), a
statutory body based in Edinburgh <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Edinburgh>.^[941] <#cite_note-1050> The aim of the register is to
provide a definitive and accessible resource to promote and preserve
tartans. It is also intended to be the definitive source for the
registration of new tartans (if they pass criteria for inclusion and a
registration fee is paid). The database itself – also named simply /
Scottish Register of Tartans/, and sometimes called /TartanRegister/
from its domain name – is made up of the pre-existing registers of STA
and STWR as they were at the time of SRT's launch (preserving the STA's
and STWR's registration numbers, dates, and other details in the SRT
data), plus new registrations from 5 February 2009 onward. On the
register's website, users can register new tartans, search for existing
tartans and request their thread counts, and receive notifications of
newly registered tartans.^[940] <#cite_note-SRT_home-1049> ^[942]
<#cite_note-1051>

STWR became defunct some time after 2008. STA later closed the /ITI/
Tartan Ferret/ to new registrations, and in late 2022 removed the search
feature from the STA website (pending a site redesign), deferring to the
Scottish Register of Tartans, which now appears to be the only operating
tartan registry. STA continues offline work on the /ITI/ database,
correcting errors, importing new /SRT/ additions, and recording
historical patterns newly discovered in museum holdings, etc.


      Legal protection[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
      title=Tartan&action=edit&section=43>]

Some modern tartans are protected by trademark <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trademark> law, and the trademark proprietor can,
in certain circumstances, prevent others from selling that tartan.^[128]
<#cite_note-MacDonald1995_48-145> An example is the "Burberry <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Burberry> check" of the English fashion house, an
instantly recognisable tartan that is very well known around the
world.^[943] <#cite_note-1052> ^[df] <#cite_note-1056>

Unlike trademark registration <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Trademark_registration> and copyright registration <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copyright_registration>, the Scottish Register of
Tartans (SRT) and its authorising Tartans Bill do not create any new or
enhanced intellectual property <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Intellectual_property> rights through the act of registration (nor
provide any enforcement mechanism other than removal of infringing
entries from the registry).^[947] <#cite_note-1057>

SRT, however, permits registrants optionally to assert and record
copyright and/or trademark claims over their new tartans, for designs
that are eligible for such protection under other established law^[948]
<#cite_note-1058> (such as the Copyright, Designs and Patents Act 1988
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copyright,_Designs_and_Patents_Act_1988>;
and the Scotland Act 1998 <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Scotland_Act_1998>, which took over copyright and trademark registration
and enforcement in Scotland)^[949] <#cite_note-1059> and lists such
tartans as restricted. An SRT registration "provides evidence of the
existence and date of [the] design",^[950] <#cite_note-
SRT_copyright-1060> which helps establish the copyright date under the
Berne Copyright Convention <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Berne_Copyright_Convention>. Such legal protections apply only to
comparatively recently created tartans; old clan, regimental, and
district tartans are outside the protection periods of such intellectual
property laws.^[44] <#cite_note-Blakely_13-53>

SRT also permits the listing of intended /use and manufacture/
restriction preferences, but has no enforcement capability,^[951]
<#cite_note-1061> and also includes a statement that "No other rights
can be conferred."^[950] <#cite_note-SRT_copyright-1060> British tartan
weavers, such as Lochcarron and D. C. Dalgliesh, generally will not
produce material in an SRT "restricted" tartan without written evidence
of permission from the copyright/trademark claimant. In additional
furtherance of intellectual property concerns, the SRT also refuses to
register a new tartan that is confusingly similar to any existing one
(as determined by an SRT review process).^[952] <#cite_note-1062>

The application of copyright law to tartans is not well tested. The
leading British legal case on textile copyright, concerned with designs
printed on fabric, is /Designer Guild Ltd v Russell Williams (Textiles)
Ltd <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Designer_Guild_Ltd_v_Russell_Williams_(Textiles)_Ltd>/ (2000), finding
for fairly broad copyright protection in textile works that involve
creative originality.^[953] <#cite_note-1063> In 2008, two tartan
pattern copyright holders, Rosemary Nicolson Samios and weaver
Lochcarron of Scotland, took legal action for infringement of an Isle of
Skye <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isle_of_Skye> district sett
(designed 1993) and the Princess Diana <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Princess_Diana> Memorial sett (designed 1997), respectively, against the
Gold Brothers firm of Surinder, Galab, Malap, and Dildar Singh, who
operate dozens of stores in Scotland and online that sell primarily
Chinese-made tartan objects or "tartan-tat <https://en.wiktionary.org/
wiki/tat#English>", including cheap Highland-dress outfits, for the
tourist market.^[865] <#cite_note-Newsome_2008-959> The Isle of Skye
tartan was considerably profitable for Samios, after the pattern was
popularised by Queen Elizabeth II <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Elizabeth_II> wearing it in 1999. The Princess Diana sett was designed
by Alistair Buchan of Lochcanrron and of the Scottish Tartans Authority
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_Tartans_Authority> as a charity
fundraiser. A British court on 2 July 2008 issued an interim interdict
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interdicts_in_Scots_law> (preliminary
injunction) against Gold Brothers' sale of Isle of Skye goods, after a
police search found hundreds of metres of the pattern in Chinese-made
cloth in the company's warehouse.^[865] <#cite_note-Newsome_2008-959>
^[954] <#cite_note-1064> ^[955] <#cite_note-1065> ^[956]
<#cite_note-1066> Both cases may have been settled out-of-court
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Settlement_(litigation)> because
published news regarding them ceases in 2008. A more recent case, /
Abraham Moon & Sons Ltd v. Thornber & Others/ (2012), actually involved
tartan. It held that the textual /ticket stamp/ (a detailed set of
weaving instructions, i.e. a thread count with additional information on
precise colours, etc.) used to produce a tartan designed in-house by the
claimant had been infringed, was protected as a literary work, and /
also/ constituted a "recording" of the graphical work of the tartan and
thus was independently protected as a work of artistic
craftsmanship.^[44] <#cite_note-Blakely_13-53> ^[957] <#cite_note-1067>
As of 2020,^[update] <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
title=Tartan&action=edit> the decision was being appealed, as it
conflicted with previous caselaw, e.g. /Hensher v Restawile/ (1976),
holding such instructions to be uncopyrightable.^[958] <#cite_note-1068>
^[44] <#cite_note-Blakely_13-53>

While tartan arguably could be classified as a form of intangible
cultural heritage <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Intangible_cultural_heritage>,^[959] <#cite_note-1069> and its value to
identifying Scottish products both in Scotland and internationally has
been recognised and exploited for a long time,^[929] <#cite_note-
PatersonMB_168-1037> tartan is not protected by either geographical
indication (protected designation of origin) <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/
Geographical_indications_and_traditional_specialities_in_the_European_Union> law, nor /sui generis <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sui_generis#Intellectual_property_law>/ legislation specific to that kind of product.^[960] <#cite_note-1070> Harris tweed <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harris_tweed>, another textile associated more narrowly with Scotland, does have such protection. In 1998, Keith Lumsden, research officer of the Scottish Tartans Society <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottish_Tartans_Society>, proposed that the word /tartan/ be prohibited for use to market a textile, unless the design was accepted in an official governmental tartan registry (which did not then exist).^[929] <#cite_note-PatersonMB_168-1037> When the Scottish Parliament finally authorised the Scottish Register of Tartans in 2008, it did not include anything like this sort of trade protection. According to Michael B. Paterson (2001): "No mechanism exists to protect [traditional Scottish] tartan from 'misuse' by interests having nothing to do with Scotland or Scotland's interests", though the tartan registries "play an important, if weak, role in asserting Scotland's cultural rights <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cultural_rights> in relation to tartan."^[961] <#cite_note-1071>


      Etiquette[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
      title=Tartan&action=edit&section=44>]

<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ConneryKilt.jpg>Scottish actor Sean
Connery <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sean_Connery> at a Tartan Day
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tartan_Day> celebration in Washington DC.
When knighted <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knight> by Queen Elizabeth
II in 2000, he wore this green-and-black hunting-tartan kilt of his
mother's Clan Maclean <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Maclean>.

Since the Victorian era <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Victorian_era>,
authorities on tartan have claimed that there is an etiquette <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Etiquette> to wearing tartan, specifically tartan
attributed to clans or families. In the same line of opinion, some
tartans attributed to the British royal family <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_royal_family> have been claimed to be "off
limits" to non-royalty.^[962] <#cite_note-1072> ^[963] <#cite_note-1073>
Even so, there are no laws or universally accepted rules on who can or
cannot wear a particular tartan. The concept of the entitlement to
certain tartans has led to the term /universal tartan/, or /free
tartan/, which describes tartan which can be worn by anyone. Traditional
examples of such are the Black Watch, Caledonia, hunting Stewart, and
Jacobite tartans, shepherds' check <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Shepherds%27_check>, and district tartans.^[964] <#cite_note-1074>
^[788] <#cite_note-PatersonMB_172-880> ^[965] <#cite_note-1075> The
published marketing of tartans for simple fashion purposes without any
association to a place or body dates back to at least 1745,^[485]
<#cite_note-StewartDW+Trevor-Roper_on_1745_ad-552> and much of Wilsons'
output through the 19th century consisted of "fancy" patterns for the
general public.^[966] <#cite_note-1076> Some recently created designs
intended for everyone (though some are exclusive to particular weavers
or Highland dress outfitters) have names including Braveheart, Clansman,
European Union, Highlander, Independence, Pride of Scotland, Rainbow,
Scotland 2000, Scotland the Brave, Scottish National, Scottish
Parliament, Spirit of Scotland, Stone of Destiny, and Twenty First
Century.^[967] <#cite_note-1077>

Books on Scottish clans list such rules and guidelines.^[128]
<#cite_note-MacDonald1995_48-145> One such opinion is that people not
bearing a clan surname, or surname claimed as a sept of a clan, should
not wear the tartan of their mother's clan.^[968] <#cite_note-1078> This
opinion is reinforced by the fact that in the Scottish clan system, the
Lord Lyon states that membership to a clan technically passes through
the surname. This means that children who bear their father's surname
belong to the father's clan (if any), and that children who bear their
mother's surname (her maiden name <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Maiden_and_married_names>) belong to their mother's clan (if any).^[969]
<#cite_note-1079> Also, the Lord Lyon states that a clan tartan should
only be worn by those who profess allegiance to that clan's chief.^[970]
<#cite_note-1080>

Some clan societies even claim that certain tartans are the personal
property of a chief or chieftain, and in some cases they allow or deny
their clansfolk "permission" to wear that tartan.^[dg] <#cite_note-1082>
According to the Scottish Tartans Authority <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Scottish_Tartans_Authority> – which is an establishment of the
Scottish tartan industry – the Balmoral tartan should not be worn by
anyone who is not part of the British royal family. Even so, some
weavers outside of the United Kingdom ignore the "longstanding
convention" of the British royal family's "right" to this tartan. The
society also claims that non-royals who wear this tartan are treated
with "great disdain" by the Scottish tartan industry.^[972]
<#cite_note-1083> ^[dh] <#cite_note-1086>

Generally, a more liberal attitude had been taken by those in the
business of selling tartan, holding that anyone may wear any tartan they
like. Under the liberal view, claimed "rules" are mere conventions (some
of which are recent creations), with different levels of importance
depending on the symbolic meaning of the tartan on some particular
occasion.

The Standing Council of Scottish Chiefs <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Standing_Council_of_Scottish_Chiefs> has also taken a fairly flexible
position (organisationally; some specific individual chiefs may have a
narrower or looser take, and not all chiefs are members). Aside from
opposing the creation of a new tartan using a clan's name without the
chief's permission, their website states:^[975] <#cite_note-1087>

    There are no strict rules on who has the right to wear a particular
    tartan. People normally wear only the tartan (if any) of their
    surname, or a "district tartan" connected with where they live or
    where their family come from. Wearing a particular clan tartan
    indicates that the wearer bears an allegiance to the chief of that clan.

Some Highland dress historians have taken a dim view of regulatory
intents and proclamations with regard to tartans; Scottish National
Portrait Gallery curator A. E. Haswell Miller wrote that "to claim
special entitlement to a tartan in the same manner as heraldic arms is
certainly absurd", because evidence suggests that the idea was just
invented by writers of the late 18th to mid-19th centuries.^[976]
<#cite_note-1088> Sir Thomas Dick Lauder <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Thomas_Dick_Lauder> expressed similar views as far back as 1829, right
in the middle of the "clan tartanry" rush, dismissing both the then-new
adoption of "official" clan tartans and attempts by clans to claim
regimental ones.^[550] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_116-625>


    In other cultures[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
    title=Tartan&action=edit&section=45>]

While tartan has been most closely associated with Scotland, and dating
back to the Roman period was perhaps associated with Northwestern Europe
in general, it is likely that the idea of using patterns of rectangles
and lines has independently occurred many times, in any cultures with
weaving.^[977] <#cite_note-CampbellJF_366-1089> Basic tartan "is almost
as primitive a weave as it is possible to make ... probably the earliest
form of patterened fabric anywhere."^[3] <#cite_note-BdLC_57-3>
Surviving pre-modern historical examples seem sparse, however.

Modern tartan-style cloth in a wide variety of materials and patterns
from simple to complex is available and used today around the world,
often simply as a style of cloth and without any association with Scotland.


      Africa[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
      title=Tartan&action=edit&section=46>]


        Maasai /shúkà/[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
        title=Tartan&action=edit&section=47>]

Further information: Maasai people § Clothing <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Maasai_people#Clothing>
Black-and-white photo of three Maasai men in shúkà, two plain and
probably red, one tartan. <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0805,_Deutsch-
Ostafrika,_Gruppe_von_Massai_-_Detail.jpg>Maasai men c. 1906–1918, one
wearing a tartan /shúkà/; photo by Walther Dobbertin <https://
en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Walther_Dobbertin&action=edit&redlink=1>

Among the Maasai people <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maasai_people> of
Kenya and Tanzania, the /shúkà/ is a cotton <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Cotton> blanket-like garment (what Scots would call a plaid) worn
as a wrap, and very commonly in a tartan pattern, though sometimes
linearly striped or of one colour.^[978] <#cite_note-Oyange-
Ngando_5-1090> /Shúkà/ are predominantly red, though sometimes seen in
blue and other colours.

12 Maasai men in shúkà, mostly red, and mostly tartan <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:The_Maasai_line_up.jpg>Maasai <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maasai_people> men in /shúkà/; Narok County
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Narok_County>, Kenya, 2018

/Shúkà/ were originally of painted (typically red) leather, but Maasai
have had access to plain-weave <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plain-
weave> cotton fabric for some time, imported to the region by Americans
since the 1860s.^[978] <#cite_note-Oyange-Ngando_5-1090> Joseph Thomas
Last <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph_Thomas_Last>, a British
missionary <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Missionary>, in 1883 described
the Maasai as particularly fond of red and white cloth, to be worn by
higher-status men (though he did not mention tartan in
particular);^[979] <#cite_note-Last-1091> a 1903 report also had them
typically wearing red blanket-like garments, after a time of favouring
blue.^[978] <#cite_note-Oyange-Ngando_5-1090> The Maasai were loosely
allied with the British, 1895–1904,^[980] <#cite_note-1092> and the
latter made heavy use of Scottish regiments <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Scottish_regiment> in African conflicts, bringing tartan with them.
However, "Guinea <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guinea> cloth" (mostly
produced in India), sometimes red and blue checked, was a common
commodity in 18th-century western Africa, pre-dating British West Africa
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_West_Africa>; whether it relates
at all to /shúkà/ is unknown.^[981] <#cite_note-Huang-1093> /Shúkà/
patterns usually lack the thin black lines common in Scottish tartans.

A nomadic cattle-pastoralist <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pastoralism>
culture, without their own weaving tradition, the Maasai have been
described as unusually culturally conservative and resistant to
modernisation.^[982] <#cite_note-1094> Nevertheless, they have always
engaged in trade to get goods they do not make themsevles,^[979]
<#cite_note-Last-1091> and have made local traditional use of modern
materials.^[983] <#cite_note-1095> The Maasai approach has been to
resist yet assimilate colonial <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Colonisation_of_Africa> and post-colonial influences.^[984] <#cite_note-
Mastamet-Mason_et_al.-1096>

Although there is evidence of tartan usage among the Maasai to at least
the period 1906–1918, when Walther Dobbertin <https://en.wikipedia.org/
w/index.php?title=Walther_Dobbertin&action=edit&redlink=1> photographed
a tartan /shúkà/ in what was then German East Africa <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German_East_Africa>, the current bright tartan and
striped style of /shúkà/ appears to have been adopted primarily in the
1960s^[981] <#cite_note-Huang-1093> ^[985] <#cite_note-1097> (partly in
response to national-level clothing modernisation pressure), supplanting
leather but keeping the same form-factor.^[984] <#cite_note-Mastamet-
Mason_et_al.-1096> The shift in outward form without affecting function
led one writer to quip that Maasai dress "has undergone dramatic changes
while not changing at all".^[986] <#cite_note-1098> Tartan-patterned
cloth is not typically used for other Maasai garments besides /shúkà/.

The /shúkà/ has become so emblematic of the Maasai that there is some
discussion (driven by the Maasai themselves) at the national and
regional level about protecting it as a form of cultural property
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cultural_property>.^[987]
<#cite_note-1099> While it has been claimed that /shúkà/ patterns, at
least at one time, conveyed particular meanings,^[di] <#cite_note-1100>
and there historically have long been weaving operations in various
African areas,^[988] <#cite_note-1101> most /shúkà/ today that are not
mass-manufactured in Dar es Salaam <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Dar_es_Salaam> actually come from China, not Africa.^[981] <#cite_note-
Huang-1093>


      East and South Asia[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
      title=Tartan&action=edit&section=48>]

The earliest-discovered tartan fabric in the world was discovered in
Western China, dated to c. 2100 BC through the first centuries BC (See
§ Pre-medieval origins <#Pre-medieval_origins>, above). Today, tartan is
still woven in China, both as a traditional fabric and in large
commercial quantities for export.^[805] <#cite_note-PatersonMB_175-897>

        Bhutanese /mathra/[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
        title=Tartan&action=edit&section=49>]

Four men outdoors in ruddy tartan gho robes, featuring four different
patterns from very narrow to quite broad <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Bhutan_(8026021154)_-_detail.jpg>Four Bhutanese men, 2012, in /gho/
robes, with four different /mathra/ patterns, from vary narrow to quite
broad

In Bhutan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Culture_of_Bhutan>, traditional
men's robes (/gho/)^[989] <#cite_note-1102> and knee-stockings (/omso/,
similar to argyle <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argyle_(pattern)>
socks),^[990] <#cite_note-1103> and women's dresses (/kira/)^[991]
<#cite_note-1104> are traditional national costume <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_costume> styles that are largely
mandatory for public dress since 1963.^[992] <#cite_note-1105> ^[dj]
<#cite_note-1107> Tartan (generally called /mathra/ or, after the
district of its primary production, /Bumthang <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Bumthang_District> mathra/,^[994] <#cite_note-1108> ^[dk]
<#cite_note-1110> among other names for specific patterns) is among the
many common textile styles for these garments, some much more elaborate
(generally called /yathra/)^[996] <#cite_note-1111> than tartan. The
tartan cloths are woven traditionally in yak <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Yak> and sheep <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sheep> wool, but
today also in cotton <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cotton> and raw silk
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Raw_silk>.^[997] <#cite_note-1112>

Lap of woman in red and white kira dress with a pattern that features X-
shaped details where the white stripes meet; prayer beads are also
featured in the photo <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Bhutan_-
_Flickr_-_babasteve_(83).jpg>/Gira/ dress featuring "X" patterns where
the white stripes meet, produced by supplementary weaving

/Mathra/ is woven primarily with a red ground. Some specific tartan/
plaid styles of Bhutan are: broad-checked /thra bom/; narrow-checked /
thra charuru/; /sethra/ ('golden pattern'), an orange or rust ground
with yellow and sometimes black checks (with black, it is more
specifically called /sethra dokhana/, and without, /dalapgi sethra/);
red, blue, and black patterns on a white ground, in at least four
varieties called /pangtsi/ (specifically red and black on white),^[998]
<#cite_note-1113> /Decheling <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Dechenling_Gewog> kamtham/, and other names;^[999] <#cite_note-1114> and
another style is named /burai mathra/.^[1000] <#cite_note-1115> Some of
these fabrics feature supplementary weft <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Supplementary_weaving> decorative patterns (flowers, etc.) added to the
tartan, with an embroidered <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Embroidery>
or brocaded <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brocade> appearance,
generally called /pesar/ ('new pattern'); one such style is more
specifically called /sethra metho chen/, the yellow-orange pattern with
flowers added. There are also patterns of simple linear stripes that do
not cross each other (generally called /adha[ng] mathra/ or /aikapur/),
with various names for specific styles.^[1001] <#cite_note-1116>


        Indian /madras/[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
        title=Tartan&action=edit&section=50>]

Main article: Madras (cloth) <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Madras_(cloth)>
A rough-stitched patchwork of tartan madras samples in a variety of
faded colours <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Patchwork_madras_fabric,_made_from_Indian_cotton_madras_plaid.jpg>Samples of tartan madras cloth, showing its muted look

Madras is a patterened, light-weight, breatheable, cotton cloth named
for the Madras (now Chennai <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chennai>)
area of India.^[1002] <#cite_note-1117> Traditional madras is hand-woven
from lumpy, carded <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carding>-cotton
thread, and coloured with natural dyes which may bleed together upon
washing to create a more muted pattern than typical tartan, as well as a
rougher texture.^[1003] <#cite_note-Checkered_Past-1118> Madras also has
a "softer" look because it typically lacks the black lines found in most
Scottish tartans. Madras cloth dates to at least the 16th century,
produced in a variety of patterns, including religious designs and
floral prints.^[1003] <#cite_note-Checkered_Past-1118> It is unclear if
tartan patterns were among the original designs, though they became very
popular later. Weaving, primarily for export, in Madras/Chennai became a
large-scale commercial enterprise after the British East India Company
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_India_Company> came to control the
area in the mid-17th century.^[1004] <#cite_note-1119> Major production
of this style of cloth also took place in Cambay State <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cambay_State> (present-day Gujarat <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gujarat>).^[1005] <#cite_note-Coo-1120>

Madras, ideal for warm-weather wear, became popular in the Philippines
(where it is known as /cambaya/)^[1005] <#cite_note-Coo-1120> and the
Caribbean;^[1003] <#cite_note-Checkered_Past-1118> mainly in undyed
form, it was also exported to Europe.^[1003] <#cite_note-
Checkered_Past-1118> Tartan madras reached America by 1718, and appeared
in the 1897 Sears <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sears>
catalogue.^[1003] <#cite_note-Checkered_Past-1118> It was popular in the
United States in the 1930s and again in the 1960s, often associated with
preppy <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Preppy> style.^[1003] <#cite_note-
Checkered_Past-1118> Substantial export of the cloth to South Africa
began in 1958.^[1003] <#cite_note-Checkered_Past-1118>

Modern madras cloth is commonly in tartan patterns, but also simply
striped (seersucker <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seersucker>). Unlike
Scottish-style tartan, madras is not woven in 2/2 twill <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Twill> pattern, but is a muslin <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muslin> of plain weave <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Plain_weave>;^[1003] <#cite_note-Checkered_Past-1118> it thus, when
viewed up close, features a "pepper and salt" colour mixture where
colours cross^[7] <#cite_note-Scarlett2008-7> (a dot matrix <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dot_matrix>, technically), not staggered diagonal
lines (see detail image <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Madras-
tissue01a.JPG>). It also usually lacks black lines.


        Japanese /kōshi/[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
        title=Tartan&action=edit&section=51>]

Japanese figure in a brown and yellow tartan-pattern kosode (early
kimono) <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Acteur_Iwai_Hanshiro_IV_in_de_rol_van_courtisane-Rijksmuseum_RP-
P-1956-661_(cropped).jpg>Woodcut <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Woodcut>
image of Japanese /kabuki <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kabuki>/ actor
Iwai Hanshiro IV dressed in /kōshi/, 1780s

In Japan, tartan patterns called /kōshi/ 格子 (also /koushi/ or /
goushi/, literally 'lattice') or /kōshijima/ 格子縞 date back to at
least the 18th century,^[398] <#cite_note-HaswellMiller1956-446>
possibly the 17th^[1006] <#cite_note-1121> in the Edo period <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edo_period> (1603–1867), and were popular for /
kabuki <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kabuki>/ theatrical costuming,
which inspired general public use by both sexes, for the /kosode
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kosode>/ (precursor of the /kimono
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kimono>/), the /obi <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Obi_(sash)>/, and other garments.^[1007]
<#cite_note-Scholten-1122> The name is a reference to the details of /
shoji <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shoji>/ room dividers, the grid
pattern said to stand for strength, with larger stripes representing
more power.^[1007] <#cite_note-Scholten-1122> /Kōshi/ range from simple
checked patterns to complex multi-colour weaves. Ikat <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ikat> thread-dyeing techniques were sometimes
employed before the weaving, such that a colour in the pattern was
mottled,^[1007] <#cite_note-Scholten-1122> and parts of the design may
sometimes have been embroidered <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Embroidery>, supplementary-woven <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Supplementary_weaving>, or dyed-over for additional highlight or
contrast.^[1007] <#cite_note-Scholten-1122> Some styles have particular
names, such as /misuji-kōshi/ ('three-striped lattice')^[1007]
<#cite_note-Scholten-1122> and /futasuji-kōshi/ ('forked
lattice').^[1008] <#cite_note-1123> A pattern with larger squares is
more generally called /ogoshi/ or with smaller squares /kogoshi/.^[1009]
<#cite_note-1124>

It is unclear whether there was a Scottish tartan influence on the
development of /kōshi/. The Edo period pre-dates the Perry Expedition
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perry_Expedition> of 1853–1854 and its
opening of Japan to general Western <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Western_world> trade, but mostly post-dates early European contact from
1543 to the closure of Japan to outsiders in 1639 under the /sakoku
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sakoku>/ isolationist policy.

Nothing suggests that particular patterns have been associated with
specific families or Japanese clans <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Japanese_clans>.

Today, /kōshijima/ is the general Japanese word for 'tartan/plaid,
checked pattern'.^[1010] <#cite_note-1125> Tartan is popular in present-
day Japan, both for high fashion and for streetwear,^[308] <#cite_note-
NatGeo-352> as well as school uniforms <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
School_uniforms_in_Japan>.^[674] <#cite_note-Tuckett_20-763> Since the
1960s, the Japanese department store chain Isetan <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isetan> has used an emblematic tartan as a
marketing tool (e.g. on all its shopping bags); the pattern is based on
some MacMillan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_MacMillan>
tartans.^[1011] <#cite_note-1126> Japan hosted a major museum exhibit
about tartan in 2018.^[1012] <#cite_note-1127>


      Eastern Europe to Western Asia[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/
      index.php?title=Tartan&action=edit&section=52>]

Tartan-style patterns are common throughout Southeastern Europe.

In northern Eurasia, tartan fabrics are also common to Finnic peoples
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Finnic_peoples> of Russia and nearby
Latvia <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latvia>, Estonia <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Estonia>, and Sápmi <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/S%C3%A1pmi> (Lappland, a cultural region that crosses northern
Russia, Finland, Sweden, and Norway); John Francis Campbell <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Francis_Campbell> (1862) described native
weaving of the Sámi <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/S%C3%A1mi> (Lapps) of
his era as being hand-loom tartan.^[977] <#cite_note-CampbellJF_366-1089>

Considerably to the southeast, the Tatars <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Tatars>^[dl] <#cite_note-1128> and Chuvash <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chuvash_people>, Turkic <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Turkic_peoples> peoples of Tatarstan <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Tatarstan> and Chuvashia <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chuvashia>,
respectively, in the Russian Federation, have worn tartan, striped, and
other patterns since at least the 19th century.

        Russian /shotlandka/[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
        title=Tartan&action=edit&section=53>]

Robert Jamieson <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Robert_Jamieson_(antiquary)>, writing in 1818 as editor of Edmund Burt
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edmund_Burt>'s 1727–37 /Letters of a
Gentleman in the North of Scotland/, said that in his era, married women
of the north-western provinces of Russia <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Russia> wore tartan plaids "of massy silk, richly varied, with broad
cross-bars of gold and silver tissue".^[95] <#cite_note-
StewartDW_33-111> This seems quite distinct from Scottish-style
construction.

Pushkin at age 28 with mutton-chop beard, a high-necked coat, and a red
and green tartan shoulder plaid <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Kiprensky_Pushkin.jpg>Alexander Pushkin <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Alexander_Pushkin> wearing a tartan cape; by Orest Kiprensky
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orest_Kiprensky>, 1827

The Russian poet Alexander Pushkin <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Alexander_Pushkin> (1799–1837), who was influenced by the romantic-
Highlands writings of Walter Scott <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Walter_Scott>,^[1013] <#cite_note-1129> ^[1014] <#cite_note-1130> posed
for one of the most famous paintings in Russia, the 1827 portrait
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Kiprensky_Pushkin.jpg> by Orest
Kiprensky <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orest_Kiprensky>. Pushkin wears
what looks at first like a Scottish-style tartan shoulder plaid
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Full_plaid>, but is more probably a
sleeveless "Almaviva" cape/cloak, a style in fashion at the time and
known to have been worn by Pushkin.^[752] <#cite_note-Kirsanova-844>

Tartan was commented on in the /Moscow Telegraph/ in 1826 as being in
broad fashion in the city for all sorts of garments (often as a
decorative accent).^[752] <#cite_note-Kirsanova-844> Scottish-style
plaids apparently did come into some fashion in Russia as women's wear
for a space during the mid-to-late 19th century, a style picked up from
stage productions; some 19th century Russian paintings illustrate
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:T.P.Musina-Pushkina.jpg> use of
plaids as shawls <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shawl>.^[752]
<#cite_note-Kirsanova-844> Tartan (and plain-striped) shawls were also
common among the Volga Germans <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Volga_Germans> and Bessarabia Germans <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Bessarabia_Germans> in Russia; a mixture of hand-woven (originally as
bedclothes and other household goods) and mass-produced in Russia, the
shawls became emblematic of the German-from-Russia diaspora <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diaspora> in North and South America from the
nineteenth century to the mid-20th.^[1015] <#cite_note-1131> ^[1016]
<#cite_note-1132>

Around the end of the 19th century, the Russian equivalent of Regency
and Victorian British tartanware objects, such as decorative Fedoskino
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fedoskino_miniature> boxes with tartan
accents in a style called /Shotlandka/ Шотландка (literally
'Scotlandish'), were produced by companies like the Lukutin Manufactory
on the outskirts of Moscow <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Moscow>.^[1017] <#cite_note-1133>

Today, /shotlandka/ or /shotlandki/ шотландки are simply Russian words
for 'tartan/plaid' generally.^[1018] <#cite_note-1134>

  *
    1839 portrait of Maria Arkadievna Bek by Pimen Orlov may illustrate
    one of the Russian plaids with silver thread <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Orlov_P_N_Portret_M_A_Bek.jpg>
    1839 portrait of Maria Arkadievna Bek by Pimen Orlov <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pimen_Orlov> may illustrate one of the Russian
    plaids with silver thread
  *
    Posthumous portrait of Alexander Pushkin by Carl Peter Mazer, 1839,
    shows him in a red and green tartan dressing gown.[752] <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:A.Pushkin_by_Mazer.jpg>
    Posthumous portrait of Alexander Pushkin <https://en.wikipedia.org/
    wiki/Alexander_Pushkin> by Carl Peter Mazer <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Peter_Mazer>, 1839, shows him in a red
    and green tartan dressing gown.^[752] <#cite_note-Kirsanova-844>
  *
    Tatyana Petrovna Musina-Pushkina, Princess Kropotkina (1800–1865),
    portrait c. 1840s by unknown artist <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:T.P.Musina-Pushkina.jpg>
    Tatyana Petrovna Musina-Pushkina, Princess Kropotkina (1800–1865),
    portrait c. 1840s by unknown artist


      Oceania[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
      title=Tartan&action=edit&section=54>]


        Adoption by the Māori[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/
        index.php?title=Tartan&action=edit&section=55>]

Pōtatau Te Wherowhero <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
P%C5%8Dtatau_Te_Wherowhero>, the first Māori <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/M%C4%81ori_people> king <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
K%C4%ABngitanga>, adopted a particular house tartan with design elements
symbolizing his ancestry, such as inner stripes representing migration
canoes <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/M%C4%81ori_migration_canoes> that
first arrived in Aotearoa New Zealand; this tartan was presented by his
descendant Tūheitia Paki <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
T%C5%ABheitia_Paki> to Charles III <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Charles_III> in the former's visit to Buckingham Palace in May
2023.^[1019] <#cite_note-1135> His following tribes <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iwi> concentrated around Northland <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northland_Region> have also adopted green
tartans.^[1020] <#cite_note-1136>

    title=Tartan&action=edit&section=56>]

    Portal:Clans_of_Scotland>

    Portal:United_Kingdom>
