    linearly-striped cloth that was common in the Scottish Western Isles
  * Flannel <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flannel>, a type of fuzzy
    cloth often produced in a tartan pattern
  * Hodden <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hodden>, a non-tartan cloth of
    undyed wool, sometimes also used for kilts, especially for non-
    Scottish pipe bands
  * List of tartans <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_tartans>
  * Mackinaw cloth <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mackinaw_cloth>, a
    dense woollen cloth often produced in tartan patterns
  * Madras (cloth) <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Madras_(cloth)>,
    cotton cloth of India often woven in tartan patterns
  * Tartan Day <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tartan_Day>, a day of
    celebration, in Canada, Australia, the US, and some other countries,
    recognising the influence of Scottish immigration
  * Tartanry <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tartanry>


      Patterns[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
      title=Tartan&action=edit&section=57>]

  * Argyle (pattern) <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argyle_(pattern)>
  * Battenburg markings <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Battenburg_markings>, a check (dicing) pattern used on UK emergency
    vehicles
  * Border tartan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Border_tartan>
  * Check (pattern) <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Check_(pattern)> or
    chequer
  * Gingham <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gingham>
  * Glen plaid <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glen_plaid>
  * Harlequin print <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harlequin_print>
  * Herringbone (cloth) <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herringbone_(cloth)>
  * Houndstooth <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Houndstooth>
  * Sillitoe tartan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sillitoe_tartan>, a
    check (dicing, not actually tartan) pattern commonly used on police
    headgear
  * Tattersall (cloth) <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tattersall_(cloth)>


    Notes[edit <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
    title=Tartan&action=edit&section=58>]

 1. *^ <#cite_ref-16>* The use of /plaid/ to mean 'tartan' has not
    been /exclusively/ North American; in 1808, the London publication /
    La Belle Assemblée <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    La_Belle_Assembl%C3%A9e>/ referred to "plaid scarfs".^[14]
    <#cite_note-14> Also, it has sometimes been claimed that /plaid/
    refers to all such patterns generally, and /tartan/ only to patterns
    of Scottish clans,^[15] <#cite_note-Black_3-15> but there is no
    support for this idea in works of tartan scholarship.
 2. *^ <#cite_ref-20>* MacBain (1911) <#MA>, p. 277. Cognate <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cognate> words in other languages are the
    Luwian <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luwian_language> /pldtmn/ and
    later Latin <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latin> /paludamentum/ for
    'cloak'. The /paludamentum/ was a cloak put on by Roman officers in
    time of war.^[17] <#cite_note-18> ^[18] <#cite_note-19>
 3. *^ <#cite_ref-22>* Solid-colour, non-tartan kilts were often thought
    to be an Irish invention of the late 19th century, but an example of
    a belted plaid or "great kilt" from Scotland was found in a 1635
    portrait of Sir Duncan Campbell of Loch Awe <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Loch_Awe>,^[19] <#cite_note-
    Newsome_Wilkinson-21> among other Scottish examples.
 4. *^ <#cite_ref-30>* The two Scarlett sources provide two exact
    formulas which seem at first to be contradictory, but one is for
    number of blends and the other for number of colours total.
 5. *^ <#cite_ref-38>* The term /mirroring/ can be ambiguous, because
    the longer phrase /mirror pattern/ may refer to "one in which ...
    two alternating ground motifs are the same size and arrangement but
    in different colours."^[33] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2022-37>
 6. *^ <#cite_ref-40>* The term /repeating/^[28] <#cite_note-
    SRT_threadcount-32> has also been used, as distinct from /
    mirroring/, but is so ambiguous that sometimes the same patterns are
    referred to as /non-repeating/.^[34] <#cite_note-
    EsleaMacDonald_wedding-39> Neither term will be used further in this
    article.
 7. *^ <#cite_ref-42>* A well-known example is the main Buchanan
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Buchanan> tartan.^[35]
    <#cite_note-SRT_Buchanan-41>
 8. ^ Jump up to: ^/*a*/ <#cite_ref-ExMacMillan_45-0> ^/*b*/ <#cite_ref-
    ExMacMillan_45-1> An example is the most popular MacMillan <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_MacMillan> tartan, in which the warp and
    weft are different, though similar; the largest blocks of colour are
    green rectangles instead of squares.^[37] <#cite_note-SRT_MacMillan-44>
 9. *^ <#cite_ref-51>* Early collectors of tartan, like Logan in 1831,
    recorded setts by measuring the width of each stripe in eighths of
    an inch.^[38] <#cite_note-STA_threadcount-46> ^[39] <#cite_note-47>
    A persistent legend that tartans were originally recorded on little
    "pattern sticks" has been dispelled as a "telephone game <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Telephone_game>"-style progressive, willful
    misunderstanding of an early description of the warp as wrapped on a
    warp beam/roller for the loom. It was poorly described by Martin
    Martin <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martin_Martin> in 1703 as "an
    exact Pattern of the Plad on a piece of Wood", which Logan (1831)
    misunderstood as a small stick used as a perpetual "record" of the
    tartan pattern on it, after which the "Sobieski Stuarts <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sobieski_Stuarts>" in 1842 blatantly falsified
    a supposed 16th-century description of "pattern sticks", and
    Archibald Campbell (1890) repeated the story again as factual. No
    such artefact has ever been found by modern researchers, and the
    idea has been described as impractical because the threads would not
    stay put indefinitely, and it would make much more sense to simply
    write or draw the pattern on paper, or keep a strip of the woven
    material.^[40] <#cite_note-48> ^[41] <#cite_note-
    EsleaMacDonald2015-49> Mackay (1924) claimed he had seen some
    examples and appeared to describe warp beams, but then claimed they
    were used as a long-term record of "clan tartans" of the area.^[42]
    <#cite_note-50> Eslea MacDonald (2015) points out that Mackay had a
    tendency toward "manipulating the evidence" when advancing his ideas
    about very old clan tartans, and that he made up a fake-Gaelic name
    for the alleged pattern sticks.^[41] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2015-49>
10. *^ <#cite_ref-55>* For example, Stewart, D. C. (1974) <#SDC>,
    Scarlett (1990) <#SJD>, and Scottish Register of Tartans (2009–) all
    use full-count-at-pivots "bare" thread counts, without slash or bold
    notation, while Eslea MacDonald (2012) <#EMP> uses them to represent
    half-count-at-pivots, but states this explicitly.
11. *^ <#cite_ref-61>* /Ground/ in this sense dates to at least 1895.
    Telfer Dunbar (1979) <#TDJ>, pp. 112–113, quoting 1895 letter:
    "... dress Stuart tartan on a white instead of on a red ground ....
    the 'Stuart hunting-tartan' on a green ground". However, /ground/
    has a different meaning at the thread-dyeing stage, where it refers
    to a first layer of colour which is then over-dyed with another,
    either to deepen the hue or make a new ones, e.g. purple from blue
    over red.^[50] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_227-60>
12. *^ <#cite_ref-69>* Wilsons, the near-exclusive producer of Georgian
    through Victorian regimental tartan, produced different grades of
    cloth for officers, sergeants, and enlisted.^[57] <#cite_note-
    EsleaMacDonald_2012-01-68>
13. *^ <#cite_ref-91>* Scarlett (1990) provided a reconstruction of what
    17th- to early 18th-century arisaid tartans probably basically
    looked like <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:Earasaid_tartan_reconstruction_by_Scarlett,_centred,_zoomed_out.png>, based on the appearance of later wider-banded "bar blanket" tartans which evolved from the arisaid setts. His sample is modernised in being simplified, symmetrical, mirroring, and not having a decorative selvedge.^[78] <#cite_note-90>
14. *^ <#cite_ref-103>* The French term /écru/ has also been
    applied,^[88] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2012_10-101> but is
    ambiguous, as it technically refers to the colour of undyed linen
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linen> not wool, and has been taken
    to indicate a richer, sandy range of hues in English usage than in
    French.^[89] <#cite_note-102>
15. *^ <#cite_ref-104>* The Lord Lyon <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Lord_Lyon_King_of_Arms>'s colour-coding system actually had three
    reds: "gules/scarlet", "red" (a dull red), and "crimson".^[46]
    <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_63-56> But it is not entirely clear what
    the difference between them is.
16. *^ <#cite_ref-108>* Multiple hues of pink appear in Wilsons' colour
    lists around the early 19th century, which included colours for wool
    and other weaving, but orange does not.^[88] <#cite_note-
    EsleaMacDonald2012_10-101>
17. *^ <#cite_ref-112>* For lists of such natural dye materials and
    their preparation, see: Kok, Annette (1979) [1962]. "Appendix: Early
    Scottish Highland Dyes"". In Dunbar, John Telfer (ed.). /History of
    Highland Dress/. London: B. T. Batsford Ltd. pp. 222–240.Some
    additional such information is available in: Mackay (1924) <#MJG>,
    pp. 59–64; and Eslea MacDonald (2012) <#EMP>, pp. 76–77. See also:
    Campbell, J. F. (1862) <#CJF>, pp. 367–368.
18. *^ <#cite_ref-148>* A romantic legend about such a thing goes back
    quite a way, however. According to Innes of Learney (1971): "The
    late J. G. Mackay, like Lord Archibald Campbell, claimed that clan
    tartans were not only deliberately arranged, but formed an elaborate
    system of identification by dress, as technical as armorial bearings
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coat_of_arms> .... [T]artans were
    never intended to, and did not, have the precise distinctions and
    ready recognisability of armorial bearings. Mr Mackay gives much
    interesting information ...; he does not, however, succeed in
    adducing evidence that there was a scientific system of arrangement,
    and circumstances are against the existence of a /science/."^[129]
    <#cite_note-146> In summary, Mackay believed that lines of various
    colours formed a heraldic <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heraldry>
    system of cadency (differencing) <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Cadency> between related family branches.^[130] <#cite_note-147> The
    argument depends on the Victorian-era clan tartans having been used
    in the 17th–18th centuries, but all modern tartan scholarship shows
    this idea to be broadly false.
19. *^ <#cite_ref-159>* For a photograph of the Falkirk cloth fragment,
    see: "Record: Cloth (Fragment) – found at Falkirk Stirlingshire"
    <http://nms.scran.ac.uk/database/record.php?usi=000-100-036-743-
    C>. /NMS.Scran.ac.uk/. National Museums Scotland <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Museums_Scotland>. 2015. Retrieved 3
    June 2023.
20. *^ <#cite_ref-161>* For a photograph of the Caracalla statue
    fragment, see (about 1/3 down the page): Lamley, Hamish (21 February
    2022). "Pictish Fashion" <https://www.pictavialeather.co.uk/post/
    pictish-fashion>. /PictaviaLeather.co.uk/. Retrieved 10 June 2023.
21. *^ <#cite_ref-175>* Nor any relation to the modern Tatar people
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tatars> (see § Russian shotlandka
    <#Russian_shotlandka>).
22. *^ <#cite_ref-180>* This historic Glen Affric tartan is not to be
    confused with various competing modern district tartans named "Glen
    Affic" available from some vendors such as Clan/Scotweb, Stevens &
    Graham, and Spoonflower.
23. *^ <#cite_ref-183>* There are possible mentions earlier, to the 14th
    century, in both Early Scots <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Early_Scots> and Middle English <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Middle_English> using the French-borrowed terms /tiretain/ and /
    tartarin/ in various spellings, but they do not clearly refer to
    tartan, either the cloth or the pattern, but rather seem to refer to
    valued cloth in general.^[5] <#cite_note-DOST-5>
24. *^ <#cite_ref-196>* There is a legend that during some period there
    was a "caste" system by which chiefs were entited to up to seven
    colours in a tartan, fewer colours were allowed for clansmen
    according to position in the social hierarchy, and just single-
    coloured cloth for servants. Barnes & Allen (1956)^[94] <#cite_note-
    Barnes_&_Allen_266-110> attributed the idea to Frank Adam (1908). He
    did indeed write that "it is said" there was such a system, but
    cited no evidence.^[134] <#cite_note-Adam_385-152> The fancy is from
    Logan (1831), who cites nothing but an ancient "Achy Edgathach
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eochaid_%C3%89tgudach>" of
    Ireland.^[168] <#cite_note-191> That was a legendary ancient Irish
    king in the /Lebor Gabála Érenn <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Lebor_Gab%C3%A1la_%C3%89renn>/, said to have passed a sumptuary law
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sumptuary_law> limiting clothing
    colours by social status, during his very short reign of only four
    years (some time between 1537 and 1155 BC).^[169] <#cite_note-192>
    It is old Irish folklore and nothing to do with history of Scottish
    tartan. Scarlett (1990): "it is difficult to allow such a tale any
    credibility".^[136] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_10-154> No modern
    Highland-dress scholars repeat it seriously (the last one to do so
    seems to have been Mackay (1924) who said it applied to "Druidical
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Druid> times"),^[170]
    <#cite_note-193> and the idea is contradicted by existence of old
    regional tartans of complexity, and by chiefs adopting tartans of
    marked simplicity. Practically, the extra dye and weaving-labour
    expenses of complicated tartans meant that they cost more and so
    were more often worn by monied persons,^[136] <#cite_note-
    Scarlett1990_10-154> as clearly reported by John Lesley <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Lesley> (1578)^[171] <#cite_note-
    StewartDW_7-194> and Robert Heron <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Robert_Heron_(writer)> (1799).^[172] <#cite_note-195>
25. *^ <#cite_ref-207>* Buchanan (1582): "They delight in marbled
    cloths, especially that have stripes of sundrie colours; they love
    chiefly purple and blue; their predecessors used short mantles or
    plaids of divers colours, sundrie ways divided, and among some the
    same custom is observed to this day, but for the most part they are
    brown, most near to the colour of the hadder [heather <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calluna>], to the effect, when they lie among
    the hadders, the bright colour of their plaids shall not bewray
    <https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bewray> them."^[181]
    <#cite_note-205> (Buchanan's wording was recycled in 1603, in the
    anonymous /Certayn Mattere Concerning Scotland/.)^[182]
    <#cite_note-206>
26. ^ Jump up to: ^/*a*/ <#cite_ref-Tielsch_210-0> ^/*b*/ <#cite_ref-
    Tielsch_210-1> The attempt to depict tartan is fairly crude, done as
    divided stripes, instead of a staggered pattern of blending
    rectangles, though it is possible it represents a weave with
    differing warp and weft <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Warp_and_weft>, which could produce more of a striped pattern. The
    exact details shown in the image are open to other question, because
    the artist illustrated an imaginative sword that is a combination of
    a Scottish claymore <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Claymore> hilt
    with the blade and quillions of a German Landsknecht <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Landsknecht> sword of a type more familiar to
    the German (Silesian <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silesia>)
    artist. Also, Telfer Dunbar (1979) called the colours yellow, blue,
    and red (not green, blue, and red), so the palette accuracy of the
    photo could be in doubt.^[184] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_53-209>
    The original art is in Huntington Library <https://en.wikipedia.org/
    wiki/Huntington_Library> MSS: HM 25863, f. 28r.
27. *^ <#cite_ref-215>* There is a recurrent legend running through
    Victorian works on tartan that the tartan cloth for each Highland
    man was usually made at home singly by his wife or mother,^[187]
    <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_CampbellA-213> but this proves to be an
    impractical idea,^[188] <#cite_note-214> for which there is no
    evidence, and considerable evidence against, including rich folk
    tradition of (mostly women's) group labour.
28. *^ <#cite_ref-221>* Innes of Learney (1938/1971) believed that
    Highlanders wore multiple tartans because some were personal
    (perhaps inherited), some geographical, and some clan-
    specific,^[193] <#cite_note-220> but presented no real evidence for
    this hypothesis. The idea can be traced to Lord Archibald Campbell
    (1890), who asserted (with no evidence at all) that a Highlander
    wearing multiple tartans at once could be explained by him donning
    the pattern of his commander, his own paternal clan, and maternal
    clan.^[187] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_CampbellA-213>
29. *^ <#cite_ref-240>* Even this, however, is ambiguous, and could mean
    that the Highlanders wore striped mantles, and worse their mantles
    in the same fashion that the Irish wore their own mantles, striped
    or not. And "striped" does not necessarily mean tartan. The Camden
    material is also contemporaneous with the Plantation of Ulster.
30. *^ <#cite_ref-248>* Taylor <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    John_Taylor_(poet)>: " ... all and every man in generall in one
    habit .... For once in the yeere, ... many of the nobility and
    gentry of the kingdome (for their pleasure) doe come into these
    Highland countries to hunt, where they doe conforme themselves to
    the habite of the Highlandmen, who for the most part speake nothing
    but Irish [i.e. Gaelic] .... Their habite is shooes, with but one
    sole apiece; stockings (which they call short hose) made of a warm
    stuff of divers colours, which they call tartane; as for breeches,
    many of them, nor their forefathers, never wore any, but a jerkin of
    the same stuff that their hose is of; their garters being bands, or
    wreathes of hay or straw; with a plaed about their shoulders, which
    is a mantle of divers colours, much finer and lighter stuffe than
    their hose; with blue flat caps on their heads ...."^[218]
    <#cite_note-247>
31. *^ <#cite_ref-249>* Adam (1908/1970) makes the surprising claim that
    in Taylor's time, "any one who assumed the tartan of the clan was
    considered as being under the special protection of that clan" and
    implies that Taylor said this.^[179] <#cite_note-Adam_387-203> Adam
    invented it, as nothing like this is in Taylor's original material.
    Taylor simply said that vistors wearing Highland dress would be
    "conquered with kindnesse, and the sport will be plentifull".
32. *^ <#cite_ref-260>* E.g. in the revised 1707 edition of Rev. James
    Brome <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Brome>'s /Travels over
    England, Scotland and Wales/, is material partly adapted from
    Buchanan (1582): "They go habited in Mantles striped, or streaked
    with divers colours about the Shoulders, which they call Plodden,
    with a Coat girt close to their Bodies, and commonly are naked up
    their Legs, but wear Sandals upon the Soles of their Feet, and their
    Women go clad much after the same Fashion."^[228] <#cite_note-259>
    This suggests a span of at least 1582–1707 of Highland fashion being
    rather consistent and unisex.
33. *^ <#cite_ref-272>* The same artist earlier painted a three-in-one
    portrait of actor-playwright John Lacy <https://en.wikipedia.org/
    wiki/File:John_Lacy_by_John_Michael_Wright.jpg>, in 1675, which
    featured trews and belted plaid,^[239] <#cite_note-271> but the
    tartan there is very casually represented as simple red and blue
    lines on white.
34. *^ <#cite_ref-279>* Martin Martin <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Martin_Martin> (1703) wrote: "each Isle differs from the other in
    their fancy of making Plaids, as to the Stripes in Breadth and
    Colours. This Humour is as different thro the main Land of the
    Highlands, in-so-far that they who have seen these Places are able,
    at the first view of a Man's Plaid to guess the Place of his
    Residence ...."^[244] <#cite_note-Scarlett/Stewart/
    Dunbar_Martin-277> Scarlett (1990) says some earlier writers used
    this to just assume "a fully organised system of District tartans at
    that time" though Martin said nothing of the sort.^[245]
    <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_34-278> Scarlett considered Martin's
    account to have "a rather sweeping style that suggests some
    exaggeration", but generally plausible on other evidence of
    particular patterns, with minor variations, being common across wide
    areas.^[245] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_34-278>
35. *^ <#cite_ref-288>* These tartan jackets are not to be confused with
    the later short regimental Highland doublet <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doublet_(Highland_dress)> styles, borrowed
    directly from the military Highland regiments <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highland_regiments> starting in the late 18th
    century; these are also of plain colour, not tartan.
36. *^ <#cite_ref-296>* Sources conflict sharply on the date. Telfer
    Dunbar (1979), relying on Mackay Scobie, says 1717-1739;^[260]
    <#cite_note-295> while Eslea MacDonald (2016), relying on R. W.
    Munro's /Highland Clans & Tartans/ (1977), says 1754.^[166]
    <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2016-189>
37. *^ <#cite_ref-302>* Banks & de La Chapelle (2007) give an
    implausible 1724 date for the founding of Wilsons,^[265]
    <#cite_note-BanksdeLaChapell_100-301> but this does not agree with
    other scholarship, and they cite no source for the assertion.
38. *^ <#cite_ref-303>* Scarlett (2008): "Red, blue and green have been
    recorded as the first colours to appear in all primitive art, so
    there may be some deep physiological or psychological reason for the
    predominance of these colours."^[7] <#cite_note-Scarlett2008-7>
39. *^ <#cite_ref-306>* Britain has many native plants that can produce
    at least a thin yellow, but they seem not to have been favoured,
    except as a ground-colour for over-dyeing with blue to create
    green.^[267] <#cite_note-305>
40. *^ <#cite_ref-321>* A small piece of tartan believed to be from a
    plaid of Bonnie Prince Charlie, given in 1746 to Lady Anne
    Mackintosh <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anne_Mackintosh> of Clan
    Farquharson <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Farquharson>,
    survives in the National Records of Scotland <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Records_of_Scotland>.^[159]
    <#cite_note-SRT_NRS-181> The prince apparently had a habit of giving
    out plaids as thanks for hospitality, and several recorded (but
    quite different) tartans are said to have come from these plaids,
    e.g. SRT 4220,^[277] <#cite_note-316> 4421,^[278] <#cite_note-317>
    4422,^[279] <#cite_note-318> and 4423.^[280] <#cite_note-319>
    According to Telfer Dunbar (1979), various museums and other
    collections hold at least 40 pieces of tartan claimed to have been
    worn by "the Young Pretender", eight at the Battle of Culloden, and
    they cannot all be genuine.^[281] <#cite_note-320> One sample in
    particular is more likely than the others to be legitimate.^[65]
    <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2014-11-77>
41. *^ <#cite_ref-331>* There are several other tartans called
    "Jacobite". One dates to c. 1850,^[68] <#cite_note-
    SRT_Jacobite,_Old-80> or might be a bit older and is probably a
    Wilsons design,^[33] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2022-37> and the
    others are more recent commercial inventions of c. 1930^[289]
    <#cite_note-329> and the late 20th century.^[290] <#cite_note-330>
42. *^ <#cite_ref-334>* Telfer Dunbar (1979) says the correct year is
    1725 and that 1729 was an error introduced by Stewart of Garth
    (1822) and copied by later authors.^[213] <#cite_note-
    TelferDunbar1979_155-242>
43. *^ <#cite_ref-342>* Specifically, as defined in an earlier act of
    Parliament, north of the "Highland line" running from Perth in the
    east to Dumbarton in the west.^[299] <#cite_note-
    EsleaMacDonald2012_3-341>
44. *^ <#cite_ref-343>* The Dress Act /per se/ did not enumerate
    exceptions for the nobility, but the enclosing Act of Proscription did.
45. *^ <#cite_ref-357>* Lt.-Col. Sir John MacGregor Murray <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sir_John_Macgregor_Murray,_1st_Baronet>, newly
    chief of Clan Gregor <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Gregor> and
    later vice-president of the Highland Society of London, wrote of the
    difficulty of raising a new regiment, in 1803: "It will require much
    to rekindle the martial spirit of our ancestors, which has,
    unfortunately, been systematically broken down – we were so long
    degraded by the privation of our arms and dress, and so much
    unmanned by being converted into manufacturers".^[312] <#cite_note-356>
46. *^ <#cite_ref-412>* Not to be confused with the second Seaforth's
    Highlanders, also raised as the 78th, in 1793. The original
    Seaforth's Highlanders were amalgamated with other units under the
    Childers Reforms <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Childers_Reforms> to
    become the 1881 Seaforth Highlanders <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Seaforth_Highlanders>.
47. *^ <#cite_ref-417>* Not to be confused with the earlier 74th
    Regiment of (Highland) Foot <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    74th_Regiment_of_(Highland)_Foot>, raised 1777.
48. *^ <#cite_ref-441>* The commercial tartan weaver D. C. Dalgliesh
    provides a list of those that they supply, and it includes a mix of
    obscure tartans from defunct regiments, ones still used today for
    surviving regiments, tartans of overseas units that were "Highland"
    only in name, some that are now only associated with clans, and a
    number that are/were reserved for military pipe-band use and were
    not used in regular dress or undress uniforms.^[393] <#cite_note-440>
49. *^ <#cite_ref-456>* As one example, in /The Lockhart Papers
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Lockhart_Papers>/, first
    published in 1714, is a passage describing how opposing battatlions
    of MacDonalds <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_MacDonald> from
    different places could only tell each other apart by colour of
    bonnet cockade. D. W. Stewart (1893) leapt to the conclusion they
    must have worn the same tartan, despite the material saying nothing
    of the sort^[407] <#cite_note-StewartDW_Lockhart-455> (they could
    have been wearing whatever tartans they happened to have, not
    uniforms, making tartan meaningless for distinguishing units of
    men).^[132] <#cite_note-Thompson1992_iv-150>
50. *^ <#cite_ref-458>* D. W. Stewart (1893) sometimes leaned toward the
    uniform interpretation: "It appears from the regulations issued to
    the retainers of the Clan Grant <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Clan_Grant> anent <https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/anent> the wearing
    of a uniform tartan that distinctive patterns were in use, at least
    for military purpose, or on occasion of great gatherings".^[250]
    <#cite_note-StewartDW_29-284> The Grant case is covered in detail
    later.
51. *^ <#cite_ref-459>* D. W. Stewart (1893) again came down on the
    "uniform" side, despite otherwise being a booster of the idea of
    early clan tartans;^[242] <#cite_note-StewartDW_22-23-275> so did
    Scarlett (1990).^[243] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_13-276>
52. *^ <#cite_ref-461>* Adam (1908/1970) confirmed that there were two
    different grades of tartan worn,^[179] <#cite_note-Adam_387-203> as
    did Logan (1831), but both are sources of dubious quality. Scarlett
    (1990, 2008) also observes that there were once at least two kinds
    of tartan weave, a coarse, dense sort in which the weft threads were
    thicker than the warp,^[7] <#cite_note-Scarlett2008-7> and a finer
    equal-twill weave, seen often in portraits, that is more like the
    kilt cloth produced today.^[261] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_22-297>
    (But he does not describe one as being specially intended for war.)
    Such a fineness split seems to have continued for a long time;
    Wilsons of Bannockburn manufactured regimental tartan in both coarse
    ("hard tartan") and fine qualities as late as 1819,^[409]
    <#cite_note-460> perhaps as an undress and dress distinction, or
    enlisted and officer.
53. *^ <#cite_ref-468>* Innes of Learney's motte-and-bailey tactic
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Motte-and-bailey_fallacy> when it
    comes to what "clan tartan" means is exemplified by his supposition
    that similar tartans used in lands of Murray <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Murray>, Murray of Athol <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earl_of_Atholl>, and Sutherland <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Sutherland> must mean they went back to a
    common tribal tartan "from the twelfth century" (which is not
    attested), and that: "It was no doubt 'the Murrays' tartan' without
    being '/The/ Murray tartan'".^[153] <#cite_note-
    Innes_of_Learney_10-173>
54. *^ <#cite_ref-473>* E.g., the district tartan <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Huntly_district_tartan,_tileable.png> of
    Huntly <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Huntly>^[304] <#cite_note-
    SRT_Huntly-348> was sometimes called Brodie <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Brodie>, sometimes associated instead
    with Forbes <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Forbes> or Gordon
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Gordon>, while Forbes did not
    have a distinct clan tartan until the key date of 1822, nor Brodie
    until the beginning of the 19th century.^[418] <#cite_note-471> The
    several tartans named Gordon all date to 1798 or later (and that
    earliest one was adopted from a 1793 regimental tartan).^[419]
    <#cite_note-472>
55. *^ <#cite_ref-479>* However, not only is it not certain that a
    single cloth of mixed colours was intended, rather than three cloths
    of distinct colours, Stewart contradicted himself: When the lands in
    question were restored to the MacLeans in 1630, the grey did not
    revert to green but remained gras, i.e. grey. Nevertheless, Stewart
    asserted: "The explanation is simple enough. White and black and
    green are the only colours in the oldest authenticated Mac Lean
    tartan."^[423] <#cite_note-477> But that design dates only to the
    fraudulent 1842 /Vestiarium Scoticum/ and is not "authenticated" by
    anything;^[424] <#cite_note-478> several other (red-based) MacLean
    tartans date to at least 1819 (/STR/ reference nos. 2603, 2605, and
    2606).
56. *^ <#cite_ref-483>* The Scottish Tartans Society seemed to think it
    was something very similar to Black Watch <https://en.wikipedia.org/
    wiki/42nd_Regiment_of_Foot>, with the red-and-white-striped Murray
    of Pulrossie version somehow, despite its 1618 prohibition,
    eventually becoming the primary Sutherland tartan.^[425]
    <#cite_note-480> Innes of Learney also supported the interpretation
    that it was a dark Black Watch-style tartan, related to others used
    in the region.^[394] <#cite_note-Innes_of_Learney_9-10-442> On the
    other hand, House of Gordon USA, a clan society, proclaims: "It was
    a Red Gordon!",^[426] <#cite_note-481> referring to a primarily red
    and teal tartan, also known as old Huntly <https://en.wikipedia.org/
    wiki/File:Old_Huntly_or_Gordon_Red_(1819)_tartan,_tileable.png>,
    recorded in 1819,^[427] <#cite_note-482> and appearing in a
    "stripey" variant, with differing warp and weft, in the 1766
    painting of William Gordon <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    File:William_Gordon_Batoni.jpg>. The society does not publish any
    basis for their assertion.
57. *^ <#cite_ref-486>* The piper's name was William Cumming. Telfer
    Dunbar (1979) describes this tartan, and that of a companion
    portrait of Alastair Grant Mòr "the Champion", as also showing thin
    yellow over-checks which are not really visible in this photo. He
    also describes the green as "grey".^[429] <#cite_note-485>
58. *^ <#cite_ref-494>* This problem of no consistent tartans in old
    family portraits recurs in other clans, such as Murray <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Murray> and MacDonald <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Donald>, going back to the 18th
    century.^[396] <#cite_note-Ward_Edwards-444> Trevor-Roper (1983)
    also notes this inconsistency among Highland portraits,^[175]
    <#cite_note-Trevor-Roper_23-199> as does Haswell Miller
    (1956).^[398] <#cite_note-HaswellMiller1956-446>
59. *^ <#cite_ref-498>* Willie Scobie, in 2012, railed against "an
    influential and determined body of opinion set against the idea of
    clan tartans having existed prior to the late 18th century",
    analysed the /Tartana/ lines in light of known clan tartans, found
    no correspondences aside from the Royal Company of Archers
    (supposedly using a Stuart tartan, which in reality they did
    not,^[246] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2012-280> and not being a clan
    anyway) having green edging on their jackets, and nevertheless
    decided: "we have in this piece of literature strong (one is almost
    tempted to say irrefutable) evidence of the existence of clan
    tartans in the year 1718."^[439] <#cite_note-497>
60. *^ <#cite_ref-518>* Thompson (1992)^[132] <#cite_note-
    Thompson1992_iv-150> said none of them survive; but the coat of the
    older boy is in what is now known as "MacDonald, Lord of the Isles",
    though the sett was reconstructed from the painting.^[458]
    <#cite_note-517>
61. *^ <#cite_ref-528>* A legend started by Lord Archibald Campbell
    (1890), who was working from a copy of the painting not the
    original, is that in his words: "No more conclusive proof of
    distinctive clan colours has been exhibited and it silences all
    dispute on the question at once and for all time."^[465]
    <#cite_note-525> Modern researchers do not take this seriously.
    E.g., J. Telfer Dunbar's evaluation: "This is an extraordinary claim
    as the tartans are clearly and accurately shown and not one of them
    agree with any clan tartans as known when Lord Archibald was writing
    or even to-day."^[187] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_CampbellA-213>
    Scarlett (1990) pointed out that Campbell himself claimed, later in
    1899, that the models for the painting were Jacobite
    prisoners.^[466] <#cite_note-526> If that were the case, they could
    be wearing whatever they were told to put on, even material supplied
    by the painter; i.e. the tartans depicted would be accurate
    representations of the cloth of the period but could not signify
    anything, even if they did match. The Jacobite Relics and Rare
    Scottish Antiquities Exhibition of 1946 agreed that the models were
    prisoners.^[467] <#cite_note-527>
62. *^ <#cite_ref-531>* James Ray, who served in the government forces
    at the Battle of Culloden, wrote in 1752: "In their flight I came up
    with a pretty young Highlander, who called out to me, Hold your
    Hand, I'm a Cambell. On which I asked him, Where's your Bonnet? He
    reply'd, Somebody have snatched it off my Head. I only mention this
    to shew how we distinguished our loyal Clans from the Rebels; they
    being dress'd and equip'd all in one Way, except the Bonnet; ours
    having a red or yellow Cross of Cloath or Ribbon; theirs a white
    Cockade".^[468] <#cite_note-529> Telfer Dunbar (1979): "If it had
    been possible to distinguish a Campbell by a 'Clan Campbell' tartan,
    either Ray would have done so or else remarked on the fact that the
    man was not wearing an identifiable tartan."^[469] <#cite_note-530>
63. *^ <#cite_ref-533>* /A Journal of the Expedition of Prince Charles
    Edward in 1745, by a Highland Officer/ provides this account: "We
    M'Donalds were much preplex'd, in the event of ane ingagement, how
    to distinguish ourselves from our bretheren and nighbours the
    M'Donalds of Sky, seeing we were both Highlanders and both wore
    heather in our bonnets, only our white cockades made some
    distinction".^[407] <#cite_note-StewartDW_Lockhart-455> Telfer
    Dunbar (1979): "If all the MacDonalds wore the same tartan, surely
    the writer would have mentioned this rather than the heather which
    they wore in their bonnets. A common tartan would have been much
    more confusing than a sprig of heather."^[470] <#cite_note-
    TelferDunbar1979_20-532> Also, this particular case does not
    demonstrate that /all/ the clans had different emblematic plants.
    Clan plants, like clan tartans, were solicited from chiefs in the
    early 19th century by the Highland Society of London, and there is
    no evidence of widespread assignment before then – only this single-
    clan mention in one period source.
64. *^ <#cite_ref-537>* And because the belted plaid in particular, as
    very practical for outdoor wear but not as work clothing, was
    believed to be conducive to a life of idle shirking and outright
    banditry.^[473] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_4-536>
65. *^ <#cite_ref-541>* Even the Sutherland/Pulrossie letter of 1618
    referred specifically to "the plaides of his men",^[394]
    <#cite_note-Innes_of_Learney_9-10-442> which is suggestive of his
    militia, not his entire clan.
66. *^ <#cite_ref-549>* Sometimes said to be Jean "Jenny" Cameron
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jean_Cameron_of_Glendessary>, without
    conclusive evidence; there are five other identity candidates.^[483]
    <#cite_note-548>
67. *^ <#cite_ref-550>* Another legend, started by James Grant (1886),
    has it that the tartan goes back to "Charles, first Earl of Dunmore
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Murray,_1st_Earl_of_Dunmore>,
    second son of the first Marquis of Tullibardine", but this was just
    a bad mis-reading of the Smith brothers (1850), from whom Grant
    plagiarised, referring to the /then-current/ Earl of Dunmore.^[316]
    <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2010-11-a-361> ^[8] <#cite_note-
    EsleaMacDonald_2010-11-b-8>
68. *^ <#cite_ref-559>* E.g., Telfer Dunbar (1979) provides this
    example: Wilsons' popular "Gordon" was green, purple, and black with
    over-checks of seven colours, and "unlike the present clan
    pattern".^[431] <#cite_note-TelferDunbar1979_146-488>
69. *^ <#cite_ref-572>* One example is today's Macpherson <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Macpherson>, adopted in 1817, which was
    originally "Caledonia" then "No. 43", "No. 155", or "Kidd" in
    Wilsons' pattern books.^[388] <#cite_note-Newsome_2005-435> ^[492]
    <#cite_note-Tuckett_17-560> (There is no "Clan Kidd"; the Kidd in
    question was a bulk orderer who used the tartan to clothe slaves in
    the West Indies.^[493] <#cite_note-Trevor-Roper_30-561> Confusion
    seems to have arisen when Wilson also assigned the pattern the name
    "Macpherson" after another West Indies customer by that name.^[494]
    <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2012_5-562> Another is Campbell of Cawdor
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Campbell_of_Cawdor>, originally
    "No. 230" or "Argyll <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argyll>", after
    the county.^[495] <#cite_note-563> ^[496] <#cite_note-564> A complex
    example is the case of Abercrombie <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Abercrombie_(surname)> or Abercromby <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Abercromby_(name)>.^[497] <#cite_note-565> Logan (1831) first
    published the tartan usually used for this name, but he modified
    it^[498] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_64-566> from an 1805 Wilsons
    tartan record for "No. 64" or "Abercrombie", named for Sir Ralph
    Abercrombie <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ralph_Abercrombie> not an
    entire family.^[499] <#cite_note-567> The design first popular for
    "Abercrombie" in the early 18th century changed names somehow^[498]
    <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_64-566> to Graham <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Graham> then later became today's Graham
    of Montrose <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Montrose,_Angus>
    tartan.^[500] <#cite_note-568> Wilsons' "Abercromby with
    yellow"^[498] <#cite_note-Scarlett1990_64-566> is today's Campbell
    of Breadalbane <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Campbell_of_Breadalbane>^[501] <#cite_note-569> after also being
    used by a fencible regiment.^[502] <#cite_note-570> The main
    Buchanan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Buchanan> tartan,
    famous for being asymmetric, originated as a Wilsons fashion tartan
    around 1800 and was not adopted as a clan tartan until the
    1830s.^[35] <#cite_note-SRT_Buchanan-41> "Logan" was invented by
    Wilsons, named after a merchant, and changed several times until it
    sold well.^[503] <#cite_note-DunbarJD1979_149-571> "Drummond" was
    originally Wilsons' "Perth".^[503] <#cite_note-DunbarJD1979_149-571>
    Wilson's 1819 pattern "Regent" turned into the MacLaren <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_MacLaren> clan tartan by 1830, with a
    shift from purple to blue.^[97] <#cite_note-
    EsleaMacDonald2012_9-114> Scarlett (1990) and the Scottish Register
    of Tartans provide numerous other examples of modern "clan" tartans
    actually just being renamed generic/fashion/fancy, regimental, and
    famous-individual tartans from Wilsons, when they were not taken
    from the later forgery /Vestiarium Scoticum/.
70. *^ <#cite_ref-578>* A prime example is the Black Watch tartan, which
    Cockburn collected four times and assigned the names "Campbell
    Argyll", "Grant", "Munro" and "Sutherland".^[508] <#cite_note-577>
    ^[506] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2023-575>
71. *^ <#cite_ref-587>* According to a documentary, Clan Gregor
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Gregor>, the Gordons <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Gordon>, and a MacDonald <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Donald> branch might also have had early
    informal clan tartans around this period.^[173] <#cite_note-
    Spinning-197> However, the chief of the MacDonalds indicated not
    knowing of a clan tartan in 1815, and the tartan that was the
    subject of the 1618 Gordon/Murray/Sutherland letter is uncertain.
72. *^ <#cite_ref-594>* Stewart of Garth may have had financial
    motivations for promoting an aristocratic "tartanry" or
    "Highlandism" <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tartanry> and attaching
    his name to it prominently – like many other Scottish lairds
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laird>, he was in dire fiscal
    shape.^[522] <#cite_note-593>
73. *^ <#cite_ref-602>* At least six at once claimed the Black Watch
    regimental tartan,^[506] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2023-575> ^[523]
    <#cite_note-PatersonMB_158-159-595> and "Several chiefs were asked
    to resubmit a different tartan in order to be seen to be different
    and thus support the idea of historical clan tartans."^[506]
    <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2023-575> In some cases, minor alterations
    were made, e.g. Forbes <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Forbes>
    was devised in 1822 by adding a white over-check to Black
    Watch.^[529] <#cite_note-601>
74. *^ <#cite_ref-604>* There are numerous examples, but a prominent
    case is that two of the Lord of the Isles <https://en.wikipedia.org/
    wiki/Lord_of_the_Isles> tartan variants were taken from portraits
    dating to the third quarter of the 18th century.^[530]
    <#cite_note-603> This practice, incidentally, has contributed to
    confusion about the age of clan tartans; a tartan adopted officially
    by a clan in 1850 from a painting dating to 1750 might misleadingly
    be said to be "a clan tartan dating to 1750".

77. *^ <#cite_ref-641>* In fairness, only most of the tartans in /

78. *^ <#cite_ref-648>* R. Martin (1988): "I would like to excuse the
    prevarications of the Sobieski-Stuart brothers with a nod to
    Baudrillard <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jean_Baudrillard>; they
    lied and they cheated, but they did something quite extraordinary in
    ascribing a meaning to textile design that has more or less stuck:
    false as it is, the Sobieski-Stuarts fostered a myth of textile
    identification and implication that has served a continuing and
    compelling social need for well over a hundred years. They may have
    been factually wrong, but culturally very right."^[570]

81. *^ <#cite_ref-677>* Revised in 1974, D. C. Stewart's /The Setts of

    <#cite_note-676> perhaps the most definitive work on tartan

83. *^ <#cite_ref-683>* /Electric Scotland/ published an annotated list
    of clans and their tartans' Lord Lyon registration status. The list
    is much shorter than some other clan lists, because it omits clans

86. *^ <#cite_ref-710>* Eslea MacDonald (2022) defines this "Highland
    Revival" period as the 1782 end of the Dress Act to the beginning of
    Victoria's reign in 1837.^[33] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald2022-37>
    The utility and accuracy of this term when constratined to
    Victoria's accession is questionable, because revivalism of Highland
    cultural trappings did not abate during her reign but actually
    intensified markedly. Also, the term /tartan revival/ has been used,
    with essentially the same meaning, though without closely prescribed
    dates.^[624] <#cite_note-709>
87. *^ <#cite_ref-714>* In this era, soldiering, especially as an
    officer, was the "aristocratic profession /par excellence/",^[626]
    <#cite_note-712> and this had a strong effect on fashion. In
    Highland dress of the period, sometimes civilian and military styles
    were commingled.^[627] <#cite_note-713>
88. *^ <#cite_ref-727>* Not to universal approval. The chief of Clan
    MacDonell of Glengarry <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/

    gathering: "I never saw so much tartan before in my life, with so
    little Highland material ... they have no right to burlesque the
    national character or dress of the Highlands."^[528] <#cite_note-
    Armstrong_37-600>
89. *^ <#cite_ref-731>* David Wilkie <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    David_Wilkie_(artist)>'s portrait of George IV <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom> depicts the
    king as being much slimmer than he actually was. Wilkie covered up
    the fact the king's kilt was too short – sitting well above the
    knees – and also left out the pink tights the king wore to hide his
    bare legs.^[642] <#cite_note-730>
90. *^ <#cite_ref-775>* A detailed summary of the 19th-century tartan
    books can be found in D. W. Stewart (1893) <#SDW>, pp. 57–61.
91. *^ <#cite_ref-786>* Queen Victoria wrote of her time in Scotland:
    "... I feel a sort of reverence in going over these scenes in this
    most beautiful country, which I am proud to call my own, where there
    was such devoted loyalty to the family of my ancestors – for Stuart
    blood <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/House_of_Stuart> is in my
    veins, and I am now their representative, and the people are as
    devoted and loyal to me as they were to that unhappy race".^[695]
    <#cite_note-785>
92. *^ <#cite_ref-794>* There were "tartanitis"-infused travel books of
    the era to go along with the tourism, e.g. /A Tour in Tartan-land/
    by Rev. Edward "Cuthbert Bede" Bradley <https://en.wikipedia.org/
    wiki/Edward_Bradley_(writer)> (1863).^[702] <#cite_note-793>
93. *^ <#cite_ref-954>* As examples, modern tartans have been created
    for Chinese <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_people>, Jewish
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jewish_tartans>,^[858]
    <#cite_note-950> Muslim <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Islam_in_Scotland>,^[859] <#cite_note-951> and Sikh <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sikh>^[860] <#cite_note-952> communities, as
    well as Italian Scots.^[861] <#cite_note-953> )
94. *^ <#cite_ref-957>* Wilsons of Bannockburn created several of the
    comparatively old ones – Aberdeen <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Aberdeen>, Crieff <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crieff>, Dundee
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dundee>, Glasgow <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glasgow>, and Perth <https://en.wikipedia.org/
    wiki/County_of_Perth> – simply by naming patterns after the places
    in which they were the most popular.^[431] <#cite_note-
    TelferDunbar1979_146-488>
95. *^ <#cite_ref-970>* Cornish "national" examples:^[870]
    <#cite_note-964> ^[871] <#cite_note-965> ^[872] <#cite_note-966>
    ^[873] <#cite_note-967> ^[874] <#cite_note-968> ^[875] <#cite_note-969>
96. *^ <#cite_ref-975>* Welsh national examples:^[869] <#cite_note-
    SRT_Welsh_National-963> ^[876] <#cite_note-971> ^[877]
    <#cite_note-972> ^[878] <#cite_note-973> ^[879] <#cite_note-974>
97. *^ <#cite_ref-988>* Manx national examples:^[881] <#cite_note-977>
    ^[882] <#cite_note-978> ^[883] <#cite_note-979> ^[884]
    <#cite_note-980> ^[885] <#cite_note-981> ^[886] <#cite_note-982>
    ^[887] <#cite_note-983> ^[888] <#cite_note-984> ^[889]
    <#cite_note-985> ^[890] <#cite_note-986> ^[891] <#cite_note-987> The
    last of these is inexplicably assigned a date of 1863 in /SRT/, but
    with a note that seems to indicate it was designed by D. G. Teall of
    STS in 1981.
98. *^ <#cite_ref-991>* Breton "national" examples:^[770] <#cite_note-
    SRT_Brittany_National-862> ^[892] <#cite_note-989> ^[893]
    <#cite_note-990>
99. *^ <#cite_ref-995>* Galician "national" examples:,^[894]
    <#cite_note-992> ^[895] <#cite_note-993> ^[896] <#cite_note-994>
100. *^ <#cite_ref-999>* For example, Bruce County <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bruce_County> has an official tartan.^[898]
    <#cite_note-997> An example of a Canadian municipality with an
    official tartan is Beauport, Quebec City <https://en.wikipedia.org/
    wiki/Beauport,_Quebec_City>.^[899] <#cite_note-998>
101. *^ <#cite_ref-1010>* E.g., Matheson <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Clan_Matheson> dress^[906] <#cite_note-SRT_Matheson-1006> is also
    known simply as Matheson, and is distinguished from a Matheson
    hunting tartan.^[907] <#cite_note-1007> As with many Scottish names,
    there are an accumulation of other fashion and individual tartan
    designs named "Matheson",^[908] <#cite_note-1008> but the only two
    recognised by the Clan Matheson Society are Matheson [dress] and
    Matheson hunting.^[909] <#cite_note-1009> Similarly, Shaw of
    Tordarroch <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Shaw> dress^[606]
    <#cite_note-SRT_Shaw_dress-691> is the main tartan, and is
    distinguished from a hunting variant,^[607] <#cite_note-
    SRT_Shaw_hunting-692> with the old, erroneous "Shaw" tartan being
    retained only as a memorial tartan for a particular family
    figure.^[605] <#cite_note-SRT_Shaw-690>
102. *^ <#cite_ref-1015>* A photo in Adam (1908/1970) confirms that
    tartans with white stripes were used for Highland dance outfits at
    least as far back as the Edwardian period, though the style of
    female dance-competition dress <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
    Aboyne_dress> has notably changed toward kilt-length instead of mid-
    calf skirts since then.^[913] <#cite_note-1014>
103. *^ <#cite_ref-1017>* Some writers have confused them as late as the
    1980s (which suggests that dance tartans as a /conventional/
    category unto themselves may date to the 1990s and later, though
    some specific dance tartans date to at least the mid-1970s).^[914]
    <#cite_note-SRT_MacGregors-1016> E.g., J. C. Thompson (1989)
    conflates dance and dress tartans and treats all dress tartans as if
    they were white-bearing,^[102] <#cite_note-Thompson_color-119>
    despite the clear fact that some dress tartans of considerable age
    do not have white in them, e.g. Matheson <https://en.wikipedia.org/
    wiki/Clan_Matheson> dress from c. 1850.^[906] <#cite_note-
    SRT_Matheson-1006>
104. *^ <#cite_ref-1019>* The white-heavy MacGregor dance tartan (in
    three colour variants dating to 1975–2005) is confusingly listed in
    the /Scottish Register of Tartans/ as both dance and dress,^[914]
    <#cite_note-SRT_MacGregors-1016> but the chief of Clan Gregor
    <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Gregor> insists it is for
    dancers only,^[602] <#cite_note-Malcolm_MacGregor-686> so it is
    demonstrably not a general dress-wear tartan. Several other dance
    tartans are listed also as dress tartans in the /SRT/, but most
    appear to be "fashion" inventions by individuals or by woollen mills
    and are not associated with clans or districts.^[915] <#cite_note-
    SRT_dance-1018>
105. *^ <#cite_ref-1021>* Possibly as early as 1850, and based on the Hay
    Stewart tartan or on royal Stewart, both probably by the Sobieski
    Stuarts.^[689] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2019-06-779> It is often
    misdated to 1853.^[916] <#cite_note-1020>
106. *^ <#cite_ref-1024>* An example of a writer uncritically
    perpetuating the story can be found in M. B. Paterson (2001).^[918]
    <#cite_note-1023>
107. *^ <#cite_ref-1027>* As noted above, an early regimental tartan of
    1787 was for a while called "Mackenzie–MacLeod" after two
    commanders, but this was a troop uniform tartan, not one for the
    named individuals.
108. *^ <#cite_ref-1030>* The sett actually survives in two variants in
    the /SRT/, created for an 1880 wedding; they are now sometimes used
    as Wilson family tartans.^[921] <#cite_note-1028> ^[922]
    <#cite_note-1029>
109. *^ <#cite_ref-1041>* The Lord Lyon would only accept formal clan
    tartan registrations from clan chiefs; this excluded chiefless
    armigerous clans <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Armigerous_clan>
    from tartan registration with the Lord Lyon, whether or not they had
    latter-day clan associations/societies. However, many now-armigerous
    clans /were/ able to register tartans with the Lord Lyon before they
    became chiefless, and these registrations remain in the Lyon Court
    Books. The Lord Lyon seemed to consider a clan that /has had/ a
    chief to remain a clan and not just a family/surname (the Lord Lyon
    did not do any registration of family tartans, i.e. those for non-
    clan surnames), though a statement by the Lord Lyon on this matter
    in 2002 is not as clearly worded as it could have been.^[600]
    <#cite_note-LL_list-682>
110. *^ <#cite_ref-1056>* In 2003, Burberry <https://en.wikipedia.org/
    wiki/Burberry> demanded members of the tartan industry to stop
    trading a certain Thomson Camel tartan.^[944] <#cite_note-1053>
    Burberry claimed this tartan was confusingly similar to their
    Burberry check and that it thus infringed their registered
    trademark.^[945] <#cite_note-1054> Burberry took legal action again
    in 2013 to protect its tartan trademark in China.^[946]
    <#cite_note-1055>
111. *^ <#cite_ref-1082>* For example, the Clan Cameron Association
    website states that the Cameron of Lochiel tartan "is the personal
    tartan of the Chief and his immediate family; as a rule it should
    not be worn by clansfolk".^[971] <#cite_note-1081>
112. *^ <#cite_ref-1086>* Since 1937, the only non-royals permitted by
    the British royal family to wear the Balmoral tartan are the
    monarch's own personal piper and pipers at the royal Balmoral
    estate. Even royal family members only wear it with the permission
    of the monarch.^[689] <#cite_note-EsleaMacDonald_2019-06-779> The
    official website of the monarchy of the United Kingdom <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monarchy_of_the_United_Kingdom> claims the
    tartan is not available for purchase.^[973] <#cite_note-1084> ^[974]
    <#cite_note-1085>
113. *^ <#cite_ref-1100>* Oyange-Ngando (2018): "the intentional and
    specific arrangement of colour where each bears a certain meaning,
    for example a colour arrangement could represent age, clan or
    marital status of an individual". Oyange-Ngando's paper cites many
    sources, but cites none at all for this claim. Modern photos of
    Maasai show members of the same tribe/clan wearing a wide variety
    of /shúkà/ patterns, seemingly to taste.
114. *^ <#cite_ref-1107>* They are prescribed dress in at least in the
    more populous places. Remote areas, inhabited largely by ethnic
    minorities, still exhibit local traditional dress norms that differ
    from area to area.^[993] <#cite_note-1106>
115. *^ <#cite_ref-1110>* Just /Bumthang/ by itself is a term for a type
    of woolen cloth, regardless of pattern.^[995] <#cite_note-1109>
116. *^ <#cite_ref-1128>* Not to be confused with the Mongols <https://
    en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mongols>, who were called "Ta[r]tars" by
    medieval Europeans, and supplied patterned cloth among other trade
    goods (see § Medieval <#Medieval>, above).
